How to Change What Power Sounds Like, Starting with You

To the moms













INTRODUCTION


The New Sound of Power




What we need is some old-fashioned consciousness-raising about what we mean by the “voice of authority” and how we’ve come to construct it.


—MARY BEARD,
 
Women & Power: A Manifesto
1




At my local indie bookstore on the Eastside of Los Angeles, I got to chatting with the cashier. She asked what I was going to call the book I was writing, and when I told her, the man behind me in the queue—tall, redheaded, and rosy-cheeked—piped up with a guffaw: “Permission to speak? That’s not something I’ve ever waited for!”


The cashier and I shared a delicious look. 
Precisely, sir. Precisely.


This book … is not for him.


When I was twenty-four, I lost my voice. I needed this book—pretty desperately. I remember most mornings I’d be able to speak, my voice tentative but at least audible, but by evening it’d be gone again. I would push through to talk during the day, feeling a constant scraping in my throat, the effort of it stealing my energy and thrashing my spirit. Something was very wrong, but I didn’t know what. Was I sick? Was I broken?


I was living in Providence, Rhode Island, halfway through a graduate program in acting—a dream I had fought for—but in truth, it was a terrible fit. I was desperately lonely, and now 
feeling ever more isolated because I couldn’t even talk. I dropped out of the play I was in. I attended classes like a ghost, aware of my every impulse to communicate and how I’d squelch each one. I stopped calling friends and my parents on the phone because it hurt too much. I definitely stopped laughing. Nights, in the dead of winter, I would huddle in the one-room guesthouse I was renting, drinking tea with honey, wondering what my voice was trying to tell me and why I couldn’t just be 
normal
.


It turned out, I had the beginnings of vocal nodules, pissed off little blisters developing on my vocal cords. I still have the alien-looking photo of my V-shaped cords the doctor captured by running a tiny camera up my nose and down my throat, and remember him pointing out where they were rubbing against each other—aka, at the wrong spot. It turned out, I had picked up a habit of speaking that was a bit lower than my body’s preferred pitch. It turned out, the fix was relatively minor. And with the help of a speech pathologist during winter break, I worked on a slightly new way of speaking that felt strange until it didn’t, and ushered myself back into the land of the living.


But I was left with major questions: Why had I picked up this habit that had unleashed such chaos on my life? Had there been some sort of payoff for me to speak this way? Or was it simply “bad usage,” as the head of my acting program pronounced it in front of the whole class, with a 
tsk
 in his voice, when I’d first returned from the doctor with the diagnosis. Like he was accusing me of messing up my voice on purpose. Like I could have 
chosen
 good, healthy speaking, but I chose bad instead—out of, what? Laziness?


And there was the question that took shape more slowly as I began to share my brush with nodules post-graduation and 
began to hear others’ stories: Was I actually abnormal? I wondered how many others experienced a hard-to-place, out-of-sync feeling between their bodies and voices. How many women especially, it seemed, spoke in ways that weren’t natural and free because they had heard whispers in their own heads that said: 
You’re not talking right.
 Or 
You’re not speaking up like you should.
 Or 
You don’t sound like what powerful people sound like—what is wrong with you?


Maybe you’ve heard these whispers yourself. Maybe you’ve even heard them voiced aloud by well-intentioned mentors or bosses who took you aside for “just a word of advice.” Maybe you’ve been the recipient of a gentle warning that no one will take you seriously if you keep saying “like” so much, or if you remain so soft-spoken. Or maybe you’ve been told you sound too angry or strident or pushy or shrill, or you speak with an accent or a particular style of speech that codes for unprofessional or undereducated, according to those well-meaning educated professionals.


Bad usage, bad usage, bad usage.


Empowerment gurus and memes everywhere say “find your voice,” but we don’t talk enough about why these voices of ours may have gone missing. We don’t talk about why exactly it’s hard to give ourselves permission to speak as readily as that jovial gentleman behind me at the bookstore. Spoiler alert: it’s not our fault. There is a certain kind of voice we’ve all grown up hearing that sounds like command and conviction. It’s John F. Kennedy in the clips we watched in school and Winston Churchill’s rousing wartime oratory. It’s Steve Jobs delivering his famous Stanford commencement speech and Tom Hanks sitting on the late-night couch charming everyone and Stephen Colbert bantering with him across the desk.


Look, it’s not their fault either; they just said what they came to say. But these men—these straight, white, rich, remarkably large men—have defined easy authority for generations. They are what being taken seriously 
sounds
 like. They are who a whole lot of our parents, and their parents, and their parents, reflexively offered the benefit of the doubt. They are who we tend to believe. They are our experts and anchors and leaders and heroes. They represent the standard voice of authority as much as a symmetrical, light-skinned, youthful appearance represents the standard of beauty, even if we know ogling dumb photoshopped pictures of models in magazines and influencers on social media will only make us feel inadequate. Sometimes, when we’re not being vigilant, we don’t even realize we’re doing it—
we just don’t like the way we look.


The problem with the voice standard is the same: when you or I want to become experts or leaders, we’re stuck trying to approximate the speech of powerful men, just as we might feel stuck spending too much time debating injections or trying to lose that extra weight. And maybe you don’t even realize you’re doing it—
you just don’t like the sound of your voice.
 That’s how standards work; they’re everywhere, so, like a fish, you don’t notice the water.


But take a course on “executive presence” or do a search for how to sound like a leader, and the advice will be suspiciously consistent. Keep your voice “low” and “steady,” they’ll say. Avoid getting “emotional” or “sounding singsongy,” “tighten up” your words, don’t use “uptalk” at the end of your statements, for heaven’s sake, and keep a measured pace, at about “75 percent of your normal rate.” That’s the standard. That’s what power traditionally sounds like. That’s, as famed British classicist Dr. Mary Beard says, the voice of authority as we’ve 
come to construct it. Run all your own instincts through this rubric or run the risk of getting laughed at, undermined, or ignored.


I mean, honestly, is it any wonder we end up feeling uncomfortable speaking in public? Of course we worry we’re not talking right or using “um” or “uh” too much instead of speaking fluidly. Of course we wind up with mindgunk, complexes about sounding too soft or too harsh or too high-pitched or too girly or too informal or too foreign, and have picked up habits to counter these fears. There are traditions! Standards! And a whole industry—not as conspicuous as the beauty industry, but effectively reaching all corners of mainstream culture nonetheless—telling us how to sound if we want to succeed. And the answer is like a straight, white, rich, remarkably large man.


Hi. This book is for the rest of us. It’s for those of us who’d also enjoy knowing what it feels like to just say what we came to say. For those of us who wonder, is it possible? To really 
show up
 when we show up, as whole, coherent people in sync with our bodies and our voices, ready to land our message, own the room, and find ridiculous amounts of joy in speaking to our public? Joy, after all, is what those standards rob us of—having 
fun
 when we speak up. Anxiety about how we sound distracts us from loving on our audience and letting them love us back with wild abandon. It spoils the sheer pleasure of being seen and heard and absolutely adored for what we’ve come to say and do. How 
could
 we be seen and heard when we’re scrambling to hide the parts of ourselves that don’t fit the standard and squeezing our voices into the mold that was never meant to fit us? In philosopher Bertrand Russell’s Nobel Prize speech, he said, “
Look at me
 is one of the most fundamental 
desires of the human heart.”
2
 Look at me, yes. Yes, Bertrand. But on my own terms. See me and hear me for who I am and what I need to do in the world. Listen to me speak in a voice that is shamelessly, utterly, wantonly 
mine
.


So, instead of becoming an actor, I became someone who helps with that—the riotous process of bringing your best self into your most public moments. In a way, maybe I was preparing for it my whole life. I was a preteen obsessed with Shakespeare, sitting in the audience of my hometown theater festival watching modern takes of the plays performed outdoors in a redwood forest. I nerded out hard on the language and the heartbeat rhythm of the text, and was fascinated even then by what it is to open our mouths and change the energy in a space.


There was also my favorite movie as a kid, 
My Fair Lady,
 the musical about a poor British flower girl trained to sound fancy as a social experiment orchestrated by a misogynistic dialect coach. I would repeat the cockney phrases and practice doing impressions like I was the newest cast member of 
Saturday Night Live
. My other favorite subject was Marilyn Monroe, who was also a social experiment in building a persona on a very public stage: The made-up name. The bottle blonde. But above all, the iconic breathy voice. Maybe I’ve always been intrigued by the relationship between how we sound and how we’re treated. Or, to put agency into the idea, the nitty-gritty means by which we come to own how we present in the world.


Of note: there were also these physics conferences all around the world that I grew up attending with my parents for my dad’s work—I didn’t realize quite how unconventional an upbringing this was till I went off to college. But I vividly remember brunching with Stephen Hawking and sitting in on endless talks about the universe at palaces in the Loire Valley 
in France and monasteries off Lake Como. Physicists have impressive taste in venues. But I had come to the conclusion quite young that no matter how magnificent the forum, apparently speaking in front of an audience was 
supposed
 to be dry and unemotional. (The few standouts from those years were of such a different nature, they stayed with me. Was it just dumb luck? Innate charisma? A stand-up comedian moonlighting in a serious field?)


And there was college. I got into Princeton and left the West Coast for adventure and all-nighters, pulling off the great trick of earning an English degree by reading plays—short and almost entirely dialogue. I acted in and directed plays too. I thought I was constructing a life in the theater; really, I was laying the groundwork for a life coaching people to express themselves on a much bigger stage.


At first, out of school, I coached actors focusing on accents and dialects. I lucked into collecting two of New York’s best dialect coaches as mentors and they graciously threw me jobs when they had surplus, even before I believed I was ready. I was a proud member of the Broadway and off-Broadway theater scene and then a giddy Angeleno as I began working in television and film—spending hundreds of hours prepping clients at dining-room tables and in quiet trailers right near set, easing them into speaking a slightly new way. A good ear isn’t the only art of it, I realized early on; giving clients permission to play and integrate and be bad at something before they’re good—that’s the secret.


Word spread and I got to coach on indie darlings like 
Loving
 and massive Hollywood blockbusters like 
Wonder Woman: 1984,
 and on TV series like 
American Crime Story: Versace, Black Mirror,
 and 
Hunters
. Along the way, a program in Los 
Angeles that teaches people from any industry how to be a television host or on-camera expert asked me to lead voice classes, and I found I could consistently zero in on the participants’ vocal hang-ups and offer gentle advice that worked. With my dad in mind, I wrote to the Alan Alda Center for Communicating Science back in New York, and they flew me out to train in their method of coaching scientists to connect better to their students and the public. Then Microsoft called, and a social-media giant wanting a workshop for their female employees to help them speak up more freely. I developed presentations for Tory Burch’s foundation, Chief, and Create & Cultivate, and I partnered with MoveOn.org, who needed coaches to support first-time candidates for office. I coached TED Talkers and United Nations presenters and a delegate to W20 (the women’s G20 organization) and a global pop star on an award-show speech that was making him uncharacteristically nervous. I pitched a podcast to iHeartRadio—the first one 
about
 how we speak, in a medium made for it—and they bought it in the room.


My way into this work was dialect coaching, but the truth is, the tête-à-têtes I’ve had with my actors over the years during their career crises and bold power plays have shaped my work as much as coaching them on sounds has. This book isn’t a tell-all, and intimate details and most names have been changed. But suffice it to say: coaching movie stars has been an incredible lesson in how to work the power we have, generate the power we don’t, and find our people so we set fire, rise, and celebrate together.


And now I coach everyone. Or rather, I coach everyone who needs help uncovering a voice they might eventually call their own, independent of those ill-fitting, old-fashioned standards: 
entrepreneurs and CEOs and new political candidates and creative types and moms going back to work and celebrities too. I coach women on a mission, and not just women: immigrants, people of color, queer folks and those who reject binary labels, those of you who worry you’re too big or too small, those who are in toxic masculinity recovery, those who want to fit in and those who want to stand out. (By the way, I use “woman” and “her” a lot in this book, but that’s because centering women is a practice, not because this work is exclusive. If you see yourself in these pages, welcome. I bet even Bookstore Man could use a little help.)


Maybe you’re here because you’ve got a can’t-quite-put-your-finger-on-it sense that you’re not using your voice right. Maybe you feel stuck. Maybe you’ve got an itch to evolve your attitude about speaking up from 
I hate this
 to literally anything else. Or maybe you’ve got an urge for, I’m just gonna say it, 
more power
. After all, a lot of us have begun to hear—perhaps quietly or perhaps so loudly it drowns out everything else—what civil rights activists and feminist firebrands have been shouting for hundreds of years: both prestige power (leadership roles in the workplace and government) and everyday power (getting through a conversation without interruption, or, hell, just getting home safely) aren’t doled out fairly.


On the one hand, duh. On the other hand, a whole lot of us who grew up in the ’80s and ’90s were sold a bill of goods that went something like: “Our mothers fought for equality so we wouldn’t have to,” and “Congratulations, we live in a post-sexist nation!” The inevitable tension between the moments in our own lives that didn’t fit this narrative and the sheer comfort of the narrative itself played on each of us differently; we turned a blind eye, we internalized it, we kept our heads down. 
Or maybe you actually did speak up and you were met by schools or institutions in no way equipped to handle a complaint that threatened to rend the beautiful lie. 
Everyone’s equal. Everything’s fair. Chill out. Shh.


I am here to say, please don’t shh. Thinking about your voice is thinking about your power. And that means looking at your own voice story—the lifetime of habits you’ve likely formed as a functioning member of a dysfunctional society—and then choosing to own those habits or scuttle them. This book will help, by offering you the permission and tools you need to change the story and find your voice. Maybe you’ve got a habit of going quiet when you’re uncomfortable or maybe your voice ends up sounding a bit disembodied. Maybe your throat clamps up and you strain when you speak so the sound comes out raspy. Or maybe you always sound “nice” when all eyes are on you. Maybe you speak in monotone, pressing down on your throat so you don’t come across as too enthusiastic in a context where you’d rather seem cool. Maybe you fear getting it wrong or not being perfect or taking up too much time.


When we step out of our comfort zone, whether it’s to try out new sounds in our mouths like those actors or to speak to a larger crowd than we ever have before, our bodies go wild. You’ve probably noticed this. Alongside general nerves, each of our unique insecurities comes into play, flustering our senses and waking up a lifetime of dormant messages. Maybe you were constantly told as a child to be quiet, or to speak up more. To stop mumbling or sound more proper. Maybe you’ve been rewarded because your voice makes you seem young in an industry that prizes youth, or unintimidating in a world that prefers you not threaten the powers that be. This voice of yours in high-stakes scenarios is riddled with habits based on where 
you’re from plus what you’ve done to get by in rooms of power every day since.


And whether you want to or not, all of these habits help you hide. Some of them can still help you get what you want some of the time, and that’s valuable intel. Some you’re likely sick of, and some soothe because they’re what you’re used to. But this is the important part: we are not our habits. Our habits have formed, more often than not, to serve someone who is not us. And they’ve formed to hold us back from our power.


Let me be clear. When I say power, what I mean is respect. When I say power, what I mean is control over our bodies and our finances and our destinies. When I say power, what I mean is the opportunity to run the show and feel our feelings and want what we want and love what we love and rest when we please and leave the room in gales of laughter. I mean, if we could all magically sound however we damn well please and still gain and retain power, we wouldn’t just embrace our voice and stop worrying about how we’re perceived. 
We would not have the voice we have today.
 We’d have one that’s richer, more relaxed, more expansive, expressive, unlimited by social norms and alive with possibility. We would sound like the person we are around our favorite people. We would sound free. We would speak with permission.


Fortunately, the world is changing and has changed, and experts and anchors and leaders and heroes have emerged from your community and mine who don’t fit any of those pat demographic labels we’d use to describe Tom Hanks. They represent a new sound of power, or an array of new sounds of power. (This new sound of power doesn’t sound only one way. It must not; the diversity is the point. That our voices reflect our life experiences is the point. That each of us sounds like a lifetime 
of 
lifetime
 is the point.) There’s Oprah, of course, and Jacinda Ardern and Lizzo and Ai-jen Poo and Jameela Jamil and Dolores Huerta and Mona Eltahawy and Bozoma Saint John and Dr. Amani Ballour and Rabbi Sharon Brous and Representative Katie Porter with her dry-erase board and Alok Vaid-Menon and others in the pages to follow. And men who dare to sound whole as well, Baratunde Thurston and Harry Styles and Senator Cory Booker and Justin Baldoni and the guests on his podcast, and my client Édgar Ramírez speaking against Latin machismo on behalf of the #heforshe campaign. And our emerging leaders, Amanda Gorman and Greta Thunberg and Malala Yousafzai and X González and local activists and your own kids and your friends’ kids.


And there’s you. I imagine you are on the precipice of launching yourself into fresh and exciting circles of power; it’s why you’re here and it’s what I wish for you, if it’s what you want. There are conferences and meet-ups and life-changing pitches just around the bend. Plus, thanks to what journalist Jia Tolentino calls the “monetization of selfhood,”
3
 the digital platforms that permeate our daily lives offer each of us the chance, or saddle each of us with the obligation, to jump onto the bully pulpit. These platforms nudge us to brand ourselves but also to show up with authenticity and vulnerability (which are just words to get at that experience of watching someone speak who seems like a whole, coherent person). They nudge us to rack up eyeballs and engagement, but also to stand for something. “In the end,”
4
 said Martin Luther King, Jr., “we will remember not the words of our enemies, but the silence of our friends.” So here we are, figuring out what exactly the opposite of silence 
sounds like
.


This is what I know: breaking habits is about getting curi
ous, trying out alternatives, and reveling in the new sounds that pop out, even if they’re strange at first. Breaking habits is about discovering what’s nonnegotiable (sometimes you don’t need to change at all—it’s the world that’s got to come around, and you find that line and you hold it). And breaking habits is a thing we do together, normalizing our vocal drama and talking about it at the level of the problem, which is global, not alone, huddled with a cup of tea and private doubts.


Because we are not individually broken. We are collectively creating new standards, which is confounding and awkward and hope-fueled and a true joy if we do it for one another and 
with
 one another.


Voice work is actually super fun. Navigating what bell hooks called our imperialist, white-supremacist, capitalist patriarchy … is not.
5
 But finding a space to grapple with how this inherited toxicity has shown up in our bodies and in our voices is a relief. It’s a chance to exhale, to be honest, to swing our ribs wide and take up space together, to get cozy with our alter ego (who might just be us plus permission), to turn the early rumblings of tentative giggles into full-throated laughter at the absolute absurdity of it all. I have come to see my coaching sessions and workshops and now this time with you as a chance to, with mischief in our eye, imagine a better world. And a chance to turn something invisible—a voice, many voices—into a revolution that leads us to that new world together. Because here’s the thing: when we change the story of what power sounds like, we change who has power.


We change who has power.


Keep track of those who speak up in voices that seem entirely unconcerned about the old standards, who just say what they came to say. Notice who’s already doing it. Listen for them 
and think of them as stars mapping the new sound of power across the sky. Just as it is with constellations, we have to search for them and describe the pictures that emerge to each other. Otherwise we blink and they blur back into oblivion—and when we get up to speak in public ourselves, we forget. And we revert to trying to sound like some dude we’re not, trying to talk low and slow and reflect the old sound of power, and the sky remains dark in the patch where our light could shine.


Using our voices well involves changing the way we speak sometimes, but other times deciding that how we speak is just fine, thanks. So, I’m calling it out: there is tension inherent in this work. For you, on any given day, giving yourself permission may be about daring to take big, dangerous risks or daring to make the safe choice and protect yourself. Sometimes we want the thing (the job, the promotion, the vote) and sometimes we want the feeling (integrity, pride, well-being), and if the thing and the feeling are at odds, it’s hard to pick one without self-recriminations.


There are also basic paradoxes of speaking in public that everyone, including JFK, has had to contend with for all of recorded history: how to practice enough that you’re prepared but think on your feet enough that you can pivot in the moment. How to seem confident enough to be credible yet human enough to be believed. How to have fun but speak with the gravitas your subject requires. How to be both a leader and a member of the community, special but relatable.


It can feel impossible, and a little 
why bother?
 And yet. The first time I offered a workshop called “How to Use Your Voice to Get What You Want”—at a women’s conference at my alma mater—I had been told to expect about fifty attendees. It was 
a small breakout workshop on the schedule, wedged in between sessions with power-pose legend Dr. Amy Cuddy and, I kid you not, U.S. Supreme Court justices Sonia Sotomayor and Elena Kagan. But 465 women signed up and the organizers had to move my workshop to the biggest auditorium on campus. Turns out, a whole lot of us are looking to scale up our lives, influence others, and honor our mission at the magnitude it deserves. To speak into the camera on our phones and broadcast ourselves to our followers, or to speak into a mic and amplify our voices to the back of the ballroom. To ask for that raise or lodge that formal complaint or speak on that panel or say yes to that podcast. To leave the safety and comfort of home to get up in front of an audience and say something that hasn’t yet been said.


As you might have noticed, I define “public speaking” pretty broadly—in fact, I think it might just be speaking in front of people who do not know everything about you already. No matter the forum, it does require courage. But not just courage to get up there despite your audience’s potential judgments across the chasm. No, the real courage is to reject the old conceit of a chasm between speaker and audience at all. Even if you’re the only one talking, it’s a dialogue. What that matters to me, matters to you? What do we share? What do we want in common? That’s like fundamental human coexistence stuff. It’s the promise of belonging and the pleasure of community. It’s saying, 
Let’s care about things together
. Public speaking is just caring about things together, bigger.


How do you sound when you actually believe that? And how do you get there? This book is partly an exploration of how we got here, and mostly a path out. In the coming pages you’ll find stories I hope resonate, frameworks for thinking 
about how to be a whole person in front of other people, tools to notice and name your habits and try out new options, and shout-outs to public moments you can listen to and learn from with a keen ear. It’s a chance to consider who your role models are and what they 
really
 sound like. A chance to get coaching in the form of a hug or a kick in the pants (sometimes what we need is the opposite of what we think). And a chance to hear from luminaries in the fields of public speaking, linguistics, and social science, plus friends and clients in the thick of it, hacking their way out.


They know and I know and now you know: this is a movement, and you’re in it.















1


Breath




I am willing to be seen.
 I am willing to speak up.
 I am willing to keep going.
 I am willing to listen to what others have to say.
 I am willing to go forward even when I feel alone.
 I am willing to go to bed each night at peace with myself.
 I am willing to be my biggest, bestest, most powerful self.
 These seven statements scare the absolute shit out of me. But I know that they are at the crux of it all.


—Emma Watson
1




Hold on a second and just breathe. There’s the regular ol’ in-and-out rhythm that prevails while we sleep or perform mundane tasks; maybe you feel it now. And then there’s the sort of breath that accompanies moments of true surprise in our lives—a warm touch, a punch line, the unexpected sighting of a friend. The rhythm changes and we take in more air than usual. It’s an instinctive response to what’s new and original. It’s the moment I read aloud the pregnancy test pamphlet to my husband to figure out the symbol on the little stick—and stopped mid-word with a gasp. It’s the shaky sigh that followed, full of possibility and pure vulnerability. This breath is life on the move.


But breath is something else too. For almost a decade, “I can’t breathe” has been a rallying cry against the policing of Black bodies, a reminder of how easily a delicate windpipe—or a human—can be mishandled. And when the coronavirus hit, desperate cries for breath resounded in hospitals around the world, in every language humans speak. In order to avoid getting sick we locked ourselves away from others and covered our noses and mouths to literally keep our breath to ourselves. Gathering in a public space, mingling our aspirations and inspirations together, had become dangerous. That very first weekend of the lockdown in the United States, I remember sitting up in bed reading everything I could. Hyperventilating. Thinking, My God, sharing breath might now mean death.


Against this backdrop, it’s no small thing to start to notice your own breath, in trivial moments as well as in moments that scare the absolute shit out of you. Breathing is the easiest thing to do; it sort of just happens, like blinking. And breathing is the hardest thing to do when you’re taking a risk, when that in-breath is preparing to let a feeling or an idea from inside of you 
out,
 and the outcome is uncertain.


But in a way, sharing these breaths in public has always been dangerous. For those of us with fresh perspectives who are inevitably drawn to question conventions, the public has never entirely welcomed our feelings or ideas. We’ve been threatened and discredited and ignored. We’ve lost our jobs and been tried as witches. We’ve not been believed. For anyone outside traditional positions of power, to take a deep breath and speak requires undoing thousands of years of messaging about who gets to have a voice in public and how they get to sound. There’s a whole mythology to reckon with.


Because here’s the deal: when you take a real and full 
breath, air from outside your body is sucked in and then emerges a fraction of a second later 
as you
—as your hopes and dreams, your furies and joys. Your demands. Of course we each need air simply to stay alive, but we each need air to activate our furies and joys as well, and we must activate them if we want to change the story—it’s how we reshape the mythology about who gets to speak in public and how they get to sound. It’s how we become our own new heroes. My dream for you is that when the stakes are enormously high, you step into the moment ready to breathe deeply and let yourself out.


But sometimes we find ourselves in rooms where we are the only one—the only member of our race, or the queer community, the only woman, only person in a wheelchair, only one wearing flashy colors or secondhand clothing or visibly pregnant or speaking with a different accent or willing to lose money to do what’s right. Sometimes we find ourselves in an arena that’s not designed for us to conquer, as Black Lives Matter activist Tamika Mallory says.
2
 That’s when we feel the deep desire to shrink or hide our difference, and a great way to accomplish this disappearing act is to stop taking real breaths.


But sometimes that room is due for a renovation—and maybe you’re feeling up for blueprinting the new design. I’ve seen it over and over, with the clients I’ve coached, with outsiders on big stages everywhere: we are ambitious and bold, on a mission and powered by purpose, primed to conquer the world. But then we get up in front of those whose lapels we are so ready to grab, and … we hold our breath, forcing air through a constricted throat that’s trying to both let us out and keep us in. We speak, but we stifle, and our voices come out raspy or flat. We sound halfhearted. We’re ready to change the story, but our voices haven’t gotten the memo. Maybe we’ve 
braced ourselves for so long—against not belonging, not being enough, against being misunderstood, discounted, or booed off the stage—that we do it now out of habit. And those lapels remain untouched.


I am here to help you break this habit, which is as much about giving yourself permission as it is about reacquainting you with your breath. You have to trust, truly trust, that your body belongs there, and invite yourself into spaces that scare you, wholeheartedly. This kind of trust takes practice, and I’ve got exercises that will help you get there.


And I’ve got some perspective, too, because you are not alone. You are not the only one who senses you don’t belong and whose breath hitches at the thought of declaring what you believe. Our discomfort with speaking in public, which is really discomfort with 
being public,
 is a story that belongs to so many of us, and to our mothers, and grandmothers, and all the black sheep, misfits, and dreamers. None of us were invited to the table when social conventions were drafted eons ago. It helps to keep an eye out for each other now, to notice the other “onlys” and make them feel welcome, allow them to breathe easier. Let yourself imagine us as a collective, let the camaraderie dance on your cheeks, and when you catch each other’s eye perhaps you will see your reflection and breathe easier yourself. There’s nothing like the power of solidarity to pick up the pen and rewrite old conventions. For all of our sakes.


When Vice President Kamala Harris accepted the nomination at the Democratic National Convention in the summer of 2020, she was at times fiery, at times playful. She was conversational and intimate but also drummed up the weight of ceremony and spoke with purpose. It’s worth rewatching as a master class on breath. She starts with “Greetings, America!”
3
 
in a room devoid of the buzz of a crowd, without any of the organic feedback politicians relied on in a pre-pandemic world. But she nonetheless takes in a hearty breath before speaking further—as though breathing in everyone who has helped her get to that stage, as though breathing in her own sense of belonging. She could have shortchanged herself and sped on ahead without breath support, but she didn’t. “It is truly an honor to be speaking with you tonight,” she proceeds, breathing again after this thought. She centers herself with this pause, and uses the air to prompt the next thought, and the next.


You could call this pacing—but I don’t care about a measured tempo. I care that you’re breathing for the thought you want to convey, and for the people who need to hear it—which means that you must be present enough to continually read the room and read yourself. Some statements call for a deliberate delivery with silence at the end so the meaning can reverberate as you look out at your expectant audience. Maybe even a full cycle of breath, in and out and in again, before proceeding. Silence and nothing but a room breathing can be surprisingly powerful. But some statements deserve a hastening rhythm because the momentum itself lends meaning to the statement—often near the end of a speech or the end of an argument, as you build triumphantly toward your conclusion. And you can accomplish this with a speedier, more athletic in-breath. Kamala did it all: she took up space, she honored her message with the right-sized breath for the size of her thoughts, and she was deeply, keenly present.


When we take responsibility for our breaths like that, we have a shot at fully showing up. We have a shot at creating moments of true surprise, life on the move. Rebels take deep breaths; in fact, I believe we need breath support in order to 
rebel. Deep breaths allow us to be audible and visible in our full glory. Deep breaths allow us to bring our whole, human selves into rooms that may not be ready for us and then teach them what they’ve been missing. That’s what changes the story.


And so, this first chapter in a book about the voice is actually about the quiet moment before there’s a voice. It’s about the moment when you’re about to step onstage and you tell yourself, 
Breathe!,
 only to find that you lift your shoulders or puff out your chest and your body responds with more panic, not less. It’s about the moment you reach the mic, look out at your audience, and shortchange yourself by attempting to breathe into a body too sticky from stress or habit to accept the full breath. Or the moment you hold your breath in anticipation and then forget to let it go. It’s about everything that can go wrong and how swiftly, with a bit of help, it can go right.


“If 
you
 don’t support yourself, who will?” I remember the urge to roll my eyes when my high school chorus teacher said this, admonishing us for not breathing enough to hold the note. It sounded so cheesy. Breath support, moral support, get it? But now, I officially retract that eye roll.


There is an extraordinary mind-body connection between how well we allow ourselves to breathe and whether we allow ourselves to believe we’re worth it. And I mean worth anything: higher pay, attention, compassion, power. And it runs in both directions. The better we breathe, the more powerful we feel; the more powerful we feel, the better we breathe. And when we support ourselves—with breath, with belief—we change the world.


It’s funny, because when you and I were born, we knew everything we needed to know about how to breathe. It’s the first 
useful thing babies do for themselves the moment they’re out of the womb, and it remains the most useful thing we do for ourselves each and every day—if we do it well. By which I mean, if we do it with the ease we had as babies, before the world told us how to be—how loud to be, how gentle to be, how much space to take up, how powerful we 
aren’t
.


Breathing well before we speak is the most fundamental way each of us has to either stand up for ourselves or undermine ourselves, and signal to others that they should do the same. If Vice President Harris had taken shallow catch-breaths and strangled her sound in her throat, we would have gotten an entirely different message from her speech that night. Had she done it habitually in the years leading up to that night, I bet she wouldn’t have made it to that stage to begin with. If you have a feeling that this might describe you, it’s not your fault. But you can and must reclaim your breath.


Breathing is deceptively simple. After all, we do it over twenty thousand times a day. And indeed, our body breathes by itself just fine, when we’re sleeping, or going for a run, or laughing with friends. It naturally expands to fuel the activity. But in our bigger moments, when how we speak could change our lives—the presentation, the pitch, the podcast, the promotion, even attempting to set boundaries with a partner or parent—nothing feels natural. And we don’t let ourselves expand nearly enough. Because on a biological level, our bodies still work just like our ancestors’ bodies did hundreds of thousands of years ago: when we’re anxious or sense danger, we release a burst of hormones that direct our breath to become shallow and fast so we can get oxygen to our brain quickly in order to combat the threat. And this occurs to the detriment of everything else. We become tunnel-vision focused on the 
threat, coked up on adrenaline, our heart pumping blood so loudly we can hear it, ready to rush or run.


When it comes to speaking in public, the threat is no longer physical, but rather “those people out there,” watching us and judging us. And even after our body performs this process, those people are 
still out there
. Only now they’re watching a version of us that feels less and less recognizably like us; we’ve become a panicky, flushed parody of a person just trying to hold it together, let alone fulfill our purpose. Our body knows how to mobilize all of its fight-or-flight faculties to take on the challenge of a bear hurtling toward us. Yay, body! But if we want to take on the challenge of being our biggest, best, most powerful self in front of others, we’ve got to teach our body to mobilize other faculties entirely, and it’s less about biological imperatives and more about habits. Like, how we tend to think about ourselves in the spotlight, our relationship to our audience, our value to them, our patterns of behavior when we’ve got the floor.


How do you break a habit? I’m using “habit” to mean tendency you’re accustomed to that, like a drug, might offer comfort in the moment but is a crutch in the long run. The problem with habits is that most of them are formed unconsciously—and by others. Did anyone ever tell you, growing up, if you don’t have anything nice to say, don’t say anything at all? Did you get the message that anger is ugly? That keeping the peace is paramount? That it would behoove you to be smaller and quieter? Our parents, well-meaning as they may have been, our schooling and peers, the movies we watched and YouTubers we followed, the lessons we learned from rule-breakers about the consequences and rule-followers about the rewards, have all civilized us. Without knowing it and over decades, we’ve 
discovered ways to use our breath to choke, reroute, and manage our unseemly impulses so we remain proper members of society. But what makes us good members of society makes us crappy revolutionaries—or, if that feels too dramatic, it makes us crappy public speakers too.


Just as I am not interested in faulting you for using these crutches, neither am I interested in offering you new ones. Rather, breathwork is about finding something more true and more freeing than the habits we’ve acquired. When our habits whisper to us, 
You’re not the kind of person who …,
 we can whisper back, 
But what if?
 We tend to pick up habits when conditions dictate, and maybe, for you, the conditions have changed. Maybe you got this book because that panting, bear-fearing version of you isn’t cutting it anymore.


There’s a lot we can learn from our bodies when they’re calm. In fact, on a technical level, when we’re speaking in an entirely comfortable scenario we tend to take just the right amount of breath for the thought we’re expressing to ride the air on its way out. In essence, 
thought
 connects to 
breath
 connects to 
voice
. The internal process is beautiful: What we call “speech” begins with an urge to communicate, which renders on a brain scan as a simple impulse of the motor cortex, whether it’s a massive need to be heard or simply saying hi as you pass a stranger. An electrical current alights down the spine to the nerve endings associated with speech and breathing muscles, which is a 
lot
 of them. Then a shock of coordinated movements expands the rib cage, engages the massive muscle separating our upper and lower torso called the diaphragm, and pushes around our organs to create space for our lungs to expand, allowing the air to rush in and then rush back out just as 
smoothly—a little bit for a little thought, a lot for a long and winding one. A little for a casual remark, a lot for a shout.


In this platonic ideal of breathing for speech, air passes through the V-shaped vocal cords on its way out, vibrating them like a string. And this creates sound, though it’s still not out of our bodies yet. Those vibrations bounce around first, turning our bones into amplifiers, and the hollows between our bones into sound chambers where the vibrations build, multiplying and resonating—literally “re-sounding”—like an echo. Then finally, we use our lips, our teeth, our tongue, and the hard and soft parts of our mouth, to articulate those sounds into meaning as the waves find their way out through the shapes we make for them. That initial urge to communicate finds satisfaction when the sound waves land on someone else’s ears, or a whole lot of ears, and they hear and understand us.


Think of an occasion when you were on a roll, when you were retelling a juicy story or responding in wonder to a friend’s (“Oh my God, no 
way
!”). You probably actually pulled off this platonic ideal. When we’re truly present, the system functions magnificently. And those moments when we have nothing to prove are instructive: we breathe better when we’re feeling safe and loved—when that urge to communicate isn’t impeded by an opposing urge to hide.


The problem, of course, is the other times—when we get misunderstood, when we get talked over, dismissed, or when we second-guess our own thoughts in the middle of expressing them. Then, fight-or-flight instincts take over or old habits take over. Then, breathing becomes labored. We run out of air too soon or forget to breathe and find ourselves grasping for more. Our throat muscles tighten from adrenaline, trapping the sound and forcing the vocal cords to work overtime to com
pensate (notice how, in the platonic ideal of thought-to-breath-to-voice, 
throat
 was nothing but a passive participant?). The mythology of public spaces plays on us, rattling the link between mind and body, and suddenly our elegantly coordinated nerve endings and muscle groups, capable of concerto, resemble a bunch of five-year-olds in band practice.


If you consider yourself very put-together, perhaps you’ve found a habit to conceal the awkward struggle, even if you haven’t conquered it yet. My client Meg was raised in a Korean immigrant household in Los Angeles and is, she told me, driven by that familiar need to make her parents proud at all costs—a self-proclaimed classic perfectionist. She was aiming for a big promotion at her start-up, a very public-facing position. She had the right disposition and skill set for taking on her company’s problems, but her obsession with getting everything exactly right also made her a stilted speaker who wasn’t going to get the gig.


When I asked her a question about her goals, early in our session, she answered in jargon expressed with perfect poise. She spoke without pause, in big words and full paragraphs, but nothing quite added up. Even alone with just me, she wasn’t truly hearing the questions and searching herself for real answers, in real time. She didn’t allow herself the space to surprise herself. It was as though she thought taking real breaths or pausing to reflect would indicate weakness, or at least she had thought that when she was younger and the habit stuck. Like a singer wanting to hide an in-breath before delivering a line of music, Meg wanted to hide the effort at all costs. This approach did make her seem polished, but in a way that read as contrived. Which can accidentally make any of us come across as untrustworthy. It struck me that she seemed more 
like a portrait than a person, and I was reminded of marketing wizard Seth Godin’s warning, “Perfectionism is another form of hiding.”
4
 I have nothing against polish; it’s lovely to be well prepared and self-possessed, but do not mistake gripping self-policing for polish. The difference between the two comes down to breath, and the difference between whether or not you seem like a living, breathing human in the room comes down to breath too.


To seem like a human, you have to 
live in your body
. This fleshy situation doesn’t just carry around your head or join you for a Pilates class or get turned on at the thought of the couple from 
Outlander.
 You may have forgotten your body, or detached from it, or learned to hate it. And you may have done so for good reason: that major muscle separating the top and bottom half of your torso, the diaphragm, is responsible for a wild amount of angst. At the intersection of public-speaking fears and beauty standards, personal trauma, and making sure we come across as “likable” rather than the alternative is the damn diaphragm. It’s the shape of a dome and like all muscles, it can either hang out in resting position, rounded upward in this case, or it can contract and 
do
 something, and for this muscle, that means flatten down and let all hell break loose.


When the diaphragm is able to fully contract down, the movement draws valuable air into your lungs; as it completely relaxes, it pushes out oxygen-depleted stale air. But if you hold in your tummy throughout the day, you limit your diaphragm’s range of motion—breaths will be shallower, and weak exhalations will trap up to 30 percent of the leftover carbon dioxide in your lungs. Why would we do this to ourselves? The answer is, because there are real, practical benefits to holding your dia
phragm in place and tensed. For one, there’s a good chance no one will be drawn to looking at your belly area because there will be no movement there. For another, you won’t feel all your feelings—there’s a reason we associate deep body wisdom with the “gut.” When you take shallow breaths, you don’t dip down into that area at all, and you don’t have to hear its warnings. Instead, you might have a satisfying feeling of being in control. It might be what normal feels like, and there’s comfort in that. And finally, if your diaphragm stays tensed, your voice will likely come out a little soft, weak, or strained, or even higher-pitched or stereotypically feminine. In any case, you won’t seem threatening. British comedian Deborah Frances-White starts her popular 
Guilty Feminist
 podcast episodes off-loading a handful of blistering “I’m a feminist, but …” admissions. I’m a feminist, but … tensing your tummy is a pretty spectacular defense mechanism. It helps life run smoothly; it helps you not rock a single boat. It’s got its perks.


Here is the aforementioned hell that will break loose were you instead to contract your diaphragm on each cycle of breath so air has the opportunity to rush into your lungs: You will feel more of everything. You will be able to tap into your intuition, read the room, and pivot in the moment as new information comes in. A body that’s breathing well hears itself think. As a result, you will surprise yourself and your listeners. It might feel dangerous. Boats will rock. But your voice will come out fuller, with more resonance and color and expression, which means your audience will not only hear you better but 
see you
 better. Your voice will read as confident. You will feel powerful. You will literally be more powerful.


Of note: It’s terrifying for most of us to take a true breath 
because it requires the relaxation of our abdomens, and we’ve all grown up in suck-in culture. We can’t change the size of, say, our thighs just by breathing differently, but we can change the size of our middles … so we do. It’s hard to believe how much bravery is required to take up the amount of space that our bodies actually inhabit, when we’re being watched. Instead, most of us have a tendency to negotiate away our vocal power for a sense of perceived visual power. Or we’re so used to doing so, we’ve never stopped to ask ourselves if it’s a trade-off we’re willing to make.


A client of mine is a movie star in one of the biggest franchises of all time, and she told me that in acting school she could not figure out how to take a belly breath. When she finally did it, after all kinds of poking and prodding from her voice teacher, she sobbed and sobbed. She told me, “Who knows why—maybe it was finally facing the grief of self-sabotage?” That may be part of the process for you too. And it’s fair to wonder if it’s worth it, facing down habits that have in some ways protected you your whole life.


In fact, if the goal is to use your voice to get what you want, tensing your diaphragm and avoiding true breaths 
might work better
—in certain rooms, in certain relationships. We can all think of times it was more convenient to be smaller, to detach from uncomfortable feelings, to sound unthreatening, and to execute perfectly with no surprises. There are serious short-term gains to these habits. But have you noticed how many women give TED Talks wearing those form-fitting, underwear-line revealing, Spanx-requiring torture devices called sheath dresses? I perk up at the talks by women wearing anything looser. I’m not judging them based on their clothing choices; I just know there’s more of a chance that something 
real
 will 
happen on that stage—that they’ll surprise themselves and us simply by virtue of breathing more freely. I’m interested in you picking up new tools so something real will happen for you too. So you’ll switch out your actual or metaphorical sheath dress and tell off suck-in culture and take up space even if it’s a risk. So you get what you 
really
 want. I have a feeling that’s why you’re here: for the other rooms, the other relationships, the ones you want to have long-term. So you can grab the lapels and change everything. So you can become the new sound of power.


Like my friend Cathy. She sent me this text from the campaign trail: “Yo! I was told very nicely tonight that I either talk in my throat or squeak. Can you help me??” After years as an activist and inspired by the 2018 midterm elections (dubbed a “blue wave” because of the tsunami of newly elected Democrats, many of whom were women and people of color), Cathy felt the pull to do more than organize and march, so she announced her bid to run for United States Congress in 2020. Her district needed a change, and her background in healthcare primed her to understand her future constituents’ needs as well as to handle the inevitable slog of legislative bureaucracy she’d have to trudge through to get those needs met. Plus, she had just survived a serious skiing accident that left her feeling utterly fearless. She thought, When if not now? Who if not me? But the throat-talking and voice-squeaking didn’t care if she was the ideal candidate; they were going to take her down.


“Yup” is how I responded to her text. “Have you ever thought about your body when you’re speaking?” Twenty-four hours later we were facing each other, standing in ready position—like we were about to engage in the world’s fiercest 
one-on-one volleyball match. With our eyes glued on each other, she followed my lead as I held my belly and jiggled it, encouraging my guts to let themselves release into my hands as I breathed in through my nose and out through my mouth. She started laughing.


“It’s like stretching before a run. Of course you have to stretch before speaking in public!”


Indeed, whether we’re pitching ourselves to our community or pitching our idea to an inscrutable room, the trick is to expand. To take up space. Stretching reminds our body of its actual size and warms it up so that that size feels normal.


“Why had this never occurred to me?” she asked.


I shrugged and said, “The patriarchy?”


Look, it’s been quite convenient for women to be disconnected from the power of their own breath for millennia. I mean, not convenient for 
us,
 but convenient for 
somebody
. Anyone who doesn’t breathe deeply enough won’t be able to tap into all the resources at their disposal—from a strong voice to a strong sense of what is not fair and can no longer be tolerated.


At this moment we were trying to connect what Cathy knew in her head—that she was ready to lead—with a body that wasn’t used to investing in the energy leadership takes. She had decided she was fearless, but her body had not been informed. 
Her breathing was stuck in habits her ambition had outgrown.
 So, we bounced on our knees, chins down, sighing out and adding just a touch of sound—a “huuuuh”—letting the sound shake us as we manually moved our tummies, noticing ever so gently how unusual it feels to let our middles go.


We can argue intellectually with ourselves for a freer body, or we can just shimmy it out. There are benefits to each ap
proach, but one is heavy, and one is light. One takes a lifetime, the other gets results within the hour. For me, the tight spots are always my digestive tract, between my shoulder blades, and under my armpits. For all of us, it’s the places we pull in out of fear, out of habit.


I want you to practice expanding, with a little touch of silly if you can swing it. I think of this warm-up in terms of four shapes. Try them all. Ultimately if you want to use this to prepare yourself for a big day or for every day, pick and choose what’s right for your body—but dare yourself to figure out what 
right
 really means.


The whole thing can be done in one minute flat, or in half an hour if you add some calming music and turn it into a meditation. Or put on your favorite dance music and let yourself get down. You have permission to be flirty and mischievous. You certainly have permission to get winded: when you do aerobic activity your body’s own wisdom kicks in. In his 1862 treatise “Walking,” Henry David Thoreau wrote that “that primal act of mobility connects us with our essential wildness, that spring of spiritual vitality methodically dried up by our sedentary civilization.”
5
 When we move, we remember ourselves. We remember our wildness.


SHAPE 1: BIG-DECISION BREATHING


Lying on your back on the floor with your knees up toward the ceiling, encourage your lower back to sink into the floor. Place one hand on your stomach, one hand on your heart. Empty out your lungs and wait for the urge to breathe. As that urge hits, feel your stomach balloon and float up—try not to force it in any way—and rather than holding at the top, ride the cycle of 
breath. All the way in, all the way out. And when you’re empty again, hold till you feel a new urge. Think about ocean waves, foaming up to your toes on the beach and then sliding back out to sea.


The goal here is to discover your own natural rhythm of diaphragmatic breathing or what I’ve been calling “breathing well.” When we take this kind of breath, and we allow our diaphragm to activate and flatten, it pushes down into the abdomen, temporarily rearranging those organs and creating an air-pressure imbalance like a vacuum, which the lungs then hasten to resolve by filling up with enough oxygen to balance out again. That new rush of oxygen gives our whole system a boost.


Yogis call this full breath 
prana,
 which literally means life force in Sanskrit, or “a universal energy, which flows in currents in and around the body,” according to the Oxford English Dictionary. It’d be hard to sound more woo-woo than that, but also, scientists agree that this kind of breathing changes lives.


A healthy dose of oxygen is crucial to defend our immune system and balance our nervous system. (Turns out it’s physically impossible to take deep, restorative breaths and also panic.) And good breathing changes the way our brain processes information: a breathing practice like this one can serve as a non-pharma intervention to reduce patterns of anxiety, depression, and even professional burnout, according to various studies. Its opposite—habitually taking in not quite enough air—stresses the heart and is associated with weight gain, mood swings, fitful sleep, and shoddy executive function. You know what this means? It means that how we process the world, how we figure out what matters to us, and then how we choose to go after it, all comes down to the kind of breath we 
take in. It sounds nuts. But here we are, with the science to back it up. So if you need to make a big decision, lie on your back with your knees up.


SHAPE 2: BREATHING TO UNSTICK


Stand with your arms up. Grabbing your left wrist, bend to the right as much as you can and breathe all the way out with your mouth, like you’re blowing out a billion birthday candles. Wait for that urge to breathe, and then, let it rush in through your nose, feeling your rib cage along your left side expand. Continue to pull your wrist and try to become as elongated as you can along that left side from your ankle to your wrist. Do this one more time on that side, and then switch sides.


Now shift your focus to that impulse to take in air after you’ve emptied out. As you grab your wrists once more on each side, breathe in through your nose and pretend that you’re smelling the greatest smell in the world. Maybe your favorite flower, the cologne your crush wore in high school, a new baby, the sea. Let it affect you.


The goal with this exercise is to unstick your ribs. The image of a rib cage might evoke a birdcage, with bars that don’t move, and it’s true that the bones themselves don’t bend, but the spaces between your ribs 
can
 expand. However, if you’re uncomfortable, feeling resistance, trying to make it through a difficult moment, or dealing with longer-term emotional trauma, your rib cage can lose mobility, get stiff or frozen, and feel like a prison. It’s self-protection. But it makes breathing harder, which ends up hurting you even more in the long run. This exercise offers some relief, even if the relief is accompanied by more complicated emotions as they release out of your body. 
When you do this exercise, if you’re feeling safe, let yourself feel things and just continue breathing.


SHAPE 3: EASY BREATHING


Standing on your feet, bend at the waist and hang down, encouraging your head to feel heavy. Allow your arms to hang loose and your knees to bend comfortably. Breathe in through your nose, out through your mouth, and as you breathe in notice if you can feel your ribs expand, or your lower back. For me it even feels like my butt expands in this position. Try to breathe from as low as possible for the rest of this exercise. Shake and nod your head, gently bounce your knees and sigh out, adding some voice so you’re humming. Over multiple breath cycles, slowly roll up. Make eye contact with something across the room and open your mouth so the hum turns into a “maaaaaa.” Shimmy a bit to make sure you’re not getting stuck. Relax the back of your neck.


Breathing again, say “my” to that point across the room. Then, on the next breath, “My name is ____.” Trill your lips to release more tension. “Bruhmmmm ma, ma, ma.” With your hands, massage your jaw at the joint, stick out your tongue and count to ten with your tongue all the way out of your mouth (if you’re doing it right, the counting will be pretty unintelligible). Allow yourself to play. Now try gasping, like you just had the best idea ever, and let it out on a “huh!” Did you breathe from as low as possible? Try it again.


The goal with this exercise is to connect breath to speech. Although there are hundreds of theories about how exactly humans began using language, according to Bill Bryson in 
The Mother Tongue: English and How It Got That Way,
 almost all 
of them are based on the premise that early speech entailed the sort of spontaneous utterances we still make today when we’re surprised by pain, joy, or alarm.
6
 That intake of breath and release with sound is speech’s purest form. It’s childlike, genuine, maybe entirely uninhibited. Catch yourself the next time something surprises you—even if it’s just a new thought you have in the shower—and notice what happens with your breath. These exercises help you get back that simple relationship to your impulses, even when the pressure’s on.


SHAPE 4: HIGH-STAKES BREATHING


For most people it feels incredibly vulnerable to jiggle your belly with your hands, but try it and see. Start by standing with a strong base, feet shoulder-width apart, knees and waist lightly bent, heart forward, eyes focused. Then send some love to your middle through your hands, breathing in and out through your mouth, sighing out on a breathy “huuuuuh.”


Experiment in this position with what feels powerful to you. Try beating your chest. Add sound (“uuuuh”) and hear it jostle around as you pound. Raise your hands like a V for victory and let them fall while shimmying and sighing out on sound, high pitch to low. Do what makes you feel expansive. Try the Amy Cuddy power pose (hands on hips) and the Rapinoe (hands extended out like you’re holding the world and it’s yours). While you’re there in the Rapinoe, imagine your in-breath filling all the way to your middle fingers and shooting out from there. Try another “My name is _____.” Not too loud, just fill the room you’re in. Drop your arms and try again. Try saying “I need your help.” Like you’re talking to a car mechanic who’s ignoring you. Try again, this time like you’re talking to a boss. 
(Are you still breathing? From as low as you can?) And one more time, like you’re inspiring your community to join you. Say it like it’s coming at the end of a long speech and you 
know
 they’re about to cheer after you say it. Do you recognize your own voice? Breathe in and out one more time, listening to them cheer in your head. And if you’ve only been reading these exercises up until now, stop and actually try this one.


The goal here is to play with how you breathe when you’ve got something to say and the stakes are high. Yoga and meditation and running use different breath patterns than speaking does; for each of those activities, the point is a kind of consistency within a closed system. Breath fuels the activity, but the activity’s goal is internal and all we need already exists inside of us. For speaking, the point is to convey something from inside of us out, to throw the system wide open. And the goal of speech, more often than not, is to get something we need from someone else. That’s scary because we’re asking for help—even if it’s just to ring us up for a latte, or for our kid to get dressed because we’re running late, or to fix our car, or to appreciate us. It’s scary because we might not get it; 
how
 we ask will affect the outcome and on some level we know it. The first kind of breathing is called vegetative breathing; the second is breathing for 
speech
.


This final shape will teach you what it feels like to support yourself while you communicate, with incrementally harder asks so you can experience new levels of breath support. Cathy’s body wasn’t used to investing in the energy it takes to activate the diaphragm; this is your chance to experiment with how much energy 
is
 required to do just that. Adapt the scenarios to what feels relevant for you. Maybe you practice belly breathing and connect it to the first line of a tough conversa
tion you know you need to have. Or you connect it to your response to the “What do you do?” question, if you tend to get flustered when asked. Or to the first few lines of Vice President Harris’s speech. Feel your diaphragm setting you up for success. Feel yourself supporting yourself.


You know, only a few generations ago, women were trespassers the moment they stepped outside their homes. In 1900, unescorted women in the biggest cities in the United States were not legally allowed into men’s domain—or what we might call today “the public”—and if you weren’t middle-class or white, it was even more recently. Social mores began to shift in part thanks to the advent of department stores and their promise of a blended public/private sphere—a well-documented calculation to better woo women into spending more of their household’s money.
7
 Capitalism for the win. But once out of the house, it’s not like women were suddenly in a position to stop shopping and hop on a soapbox, drop their purchases and hold the floor, speaking freely to a mixed-gender crowd.


And how about now?


“Gaining a Public Voice” is Dr. Judith Mattson Bean’s twenty-first-century exploration of the cultural attitudes toward women in public during the nineteenth century in the United States. It reads as oddly familiar today. Women were taught to be, as Dr. Bean sums it up, “silent, self-effacing, and publicly invisible.”
8
 Even prominent women intellectuals of the day, like Harriet Beecher Stowe’s sister Catharine Beecher, argued (somewhat ironically) that women speaking in public on their own convictions should not be trusted—that they do so at “the promptings of ambition, or the thirst for power.”
9
 It’s hard to imagine audience members willing to give women speakers 
authority in that context, let alone women giving it to themselves. It’s also hard not to hear in Catharine Beecher’s remark modern headlines that accompanied Vice President Kamala Harris’s run for office.


Our great-grandmothers were taught to downplay accomplishment and ambition; our grandmothers were taught by their mothers to divert attention away from their bodies, and away from their convictions as well. For women, publicly invisible is publicly acceptable and has been for millennia. Publicly absent is best of all, in fact. A hundred years ago, unaccompanied women walking down a city street in broad daylight (to say nothing of after nightfall) were assumed to be either domestic workers rushing from one house of employ to another, or prostitutes—literally called “public women.” But even now, if you’re assaulted in public one of the first questions is likely to be “Why were you walking alone?” So, if you start to shake at the thought of voicing your pitch in a meeting, if something inside you urges you to take up as little space as possible, to hold your breath in a bid to be neither seen nor heard, there’s, shall we say, precedent.


Historically, the act of speaking in public has even been seen as incompatible with being a woman. According to actual medical specialists writing in the nineteenth century, women were at real risk of being “rendered sterile through strenuous intellectual efforts such as oratory, ‘unsexing’ themselves literally as well as figuratively,” writes Dr. Bean. An influential letter crafted by religious leaders in the 1830s warned that women who attempted public speaking would “not only cease to bear fruit, but fall in shame and dishonor in the dust.”
10
 Don’t even try to speak up, ladies; your uteruses will shrivel up and also you might die.


“Solid things come and go, but stories endure,”
11
 writes Elizabeth Lesser in her 2020 book, 
Cassandra Speaks
. As deranged as those voices from a few hundred years ago sound, they were the logical extension of stories told for centuries about what power looks and sounds like. Western culture’s origin tales from Adam and Eve onward tend to paint women as “erotically seductive yet emotionally fickle, in need of protection yet dangerous, all at the same time,” Lesser says. “Who could trust such a creature?” And though we can easily scoff at this sentiment today, something lingers.


According to Dr. Mary Beard, some of the oldest stories in the Western world, 
The Odyssey
 and the Greek and Roman myths, still live in us and still govern our sensibilities today. As Dr. Maria Tatar, author of 
The Heroine with 1001 Faces,
 put it, “Our foundational narratives (myths, biblical stories, epics, and fairy tales) are used not just to educate but also to indoctrinate.”
12
 And none of us is entirely immune. Consider when we announce we don’t like the sound of a woman’s voice, or when we mistrust a woman seeking a leadership position. Consider when we question our own voice’s validity. The lessons we’ve inherited from those old texts sway our modern tastes about “what counts as good oratory or bad, persuasive or not, and whose speech is to be given space to be heard,” Beard writes in 
Women & Power.
 “Public speech was a—if not 
the
—defining attribute of maleness,” so a woman using her voice in public was “by definition not a woman.”


It’s quite an inheritance: women who raise their voices in public are either trying to be a man (the archetype is Androgyne in Greek mythology, a male-female creature), or trying to get attention they’re not entitled to, as an exhibitionist or temptress (a siren in Greek mythology, a dangerous woman-
like creature with a beautiful voice who lures men to their deaths). They are either unsexed or oversexed—lacking the body parts that make us women, or all boobs and vaginas. In the stories of Ovid’s 
Metamorphoses,
 women who spoke up were turned into cows, or plants, or could only echo others, or had their tongues ripped out. The options! And though we can scoff at this barbarism, too, still something lingers.


Despite it all, there have been fervent iconoclasts who bucked the system, and we are their descendants as well. But they, including Dr. Beard herself, still get rape threats just like the women in those classic myths, only now it’s over Twitter. Old stigmas die hard. The shame around public speaking has changed shape, and perhaps size, with every generation, but it’s still there, when we open our mouths to speak before a public still deciding if it wants to give us authority. It feels risky, and it is.


And that’s why it’s hard to breathe deeply. To do so is to fight through shame and impropriety, to bring our whole selves into the room despite the risks, and not as someone who is unsexed (acting like a stereotypical man) or oversexed (performing some parody of femininity). And not by going invisible or downplaying our convictions. To take a deep breath and speak is to reject the archetypes we didn’t forge to begin with and that don’t fit us now. If we breathe deeply and let ourselves be seen and heard, if we render ourselves truly knowable, we are committing a profound act of social change. We are breaking from tradition (maybe even our own family’s tradition), changing history, being a radical and a revolutionary—when honestly, all we wanted to do was just fucking say a thing we were thinking.


Cathy wrote me from the campaign trail to report, “I was in 
the congressional dining room closet making the face noises so I would have my full vocal range.” At every stop she dutifully found discreet spots to do her breath warm-up, shimmy around a bit, gather her bravery, and tell herself she deserved to take up space. And she won her primary. In fact, her opponent decided to drop out because she so outraised him in campaign funds. But in November 2020, she lost her bid for Congress anyway; her district was too red to flip in a single cycle, and her ground campaign too impeded by the pandemic. She changed the story, though. And she inspired thousands up and down her district—who are now imploring her to run again. She might, but she needs a moment to breathe.


Ultimately, this is about daring. With breath, with belief, with our arms around each other and our hands resting on each other’s backs. We must do this work, each of us, for ourselves, but we must not mistake that for doing it alone. The first step in opening a door into any space not made for us is to take a deep breath and to will ourselves to hear in the silence the collective breath of millions of others, daring us on.

















2


Size




Nature calls us to expand. The fear is learned.


—TANYA O. WILLIAMS
, social-justice educator and coach
1




In the documentary 
Knock Down the House,
 Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez sits on a faux-leather couch in her tiny apartment in the Bronx, giving herself a pep talk before a big debate. She gestures wide, a little sheepishly, repeating to herself, “I need to take up space.”
2
 She had an inkling her opponent was about to try to make her feel small, young, and inexperienced. She knew she might accidentally start to shrink if she didn’t prepare herself, mentally and physically.


Fifty-nine million people have watched Dr. Amy Cuddy’s TED Talk on the power of posing in order to make our bodies feel big.
3
 Soccer star Megan Rapinoe’s outstretched arms, fired up through the fingertips, have inspired women around the world to stand in their lives like they actually own the real estate. 
The Body Is Not an Apology
 author and activist Sonya Renee Taylor’s book features an unforgettable cover image: her naked body in confident repose, shimmering and strewn with flowers.
4
 All three of these powerhouse women have gone all in on this wager: when our bodies boldly take up space, our 
thoughts do, too, and then our lives have the chance to expand to the size they deserve to be.


By now, the prompt to 
take up space
 and 
play big
 thrums through our modern understanding of what it is to be a strong woman: notice where we shrink, try not to. It’s the patter of our lives, whether we’re joking about laying off the exclamation points in emails or deciding if it’s worth it to quit our job or get another degree or leave that relationship. We are constantly debating with ourselves how big our lives actually deserve to be. We’re all goddamn cheetahs, but sometimes our growl eludes us. Then we feel like imposters or frauds; we feel annoyed at the cliché but stuck with it anyway. I’ve had sweaty hikes in the Hollywood Hills and teary sessions across my desk, wending deliberations with my most competent friends, with CEOs and movie stars and fiery activists and my own mentors, all of us trying to navigate the intricacies of expanding in the moments that matter, all of us again and again noticing where we shrink and trying not to.


I sat in awe watching the first female president of an Ivy League school, a decorated biologist named Shirley Tilghman who had raised billions of dollars for Princeton University, as she grappled with her own answer. “When did you feel like you were 
enough
?” the interviewer asked, veering off-script as the session was wrapping up. In front of three thousand eager women, the president’s mouth opened and closed, her face revealing wave after wave of discarded half-answers, none of them true enough to speak aloud. She was silent. Finally, she nodded gently, letting out a soft chuckle, baring herself to us. And an understanding settled over the crowd—we looked around, catching one another’s eye, sharing knowing smiles. Clearly, we’re all in this crap together.


And the attacks come from within and without. These attacks—microaggressions we face individually as well as full-scale cultural biases written into our laws and retold in our stories and embedded into our psyches—are what pressure us to play small. It’s not just some pathology of our own making; the fear is learned. As Ruchika Tulshyan and Jodi-Ann Burey wrote in the 
Harvard Business Review,
 “Imposter syndrome directs our view toward fixing women at work instead of fixing the places where women work.”
5
 The responsibility to change the system lies with the power players who write pretty diversity statements but don’t know the first thing about listening to diverse voices with open ears.


But we can’t wait for them to figure it out. So we practice our power poses and consider the connection between taking up physical space and the mindset that preempts it, or that follows (it flows in both directions). We work on doing whatever we can to gain more power so that eventually, maybe even soon, we’re in charge of those same workplaces.


There’s a vocal equivalent to taking up space unapologetically as well—and it will help in this endeavor. To speak with the full wingspan of your voice requires some technical know-how as well as a certain flex of the permission muscle. But when you do it, you’re not just more likely to speak up—you will affect the actual conveyance of your speech. 
You will sound different.
 And there’s a chance you’ll actually get heard.


Another phrase that often accompanies 
take up space
 is 
speak up
. This, of course, is my jam, but I want to get very specific here. When you’re gathering your courage to take a breath and actually speak up in a tough moment, the choice can feel binary: to speak or not to speak? You may think, Will I get it to
