gether to call that guy out, or will I let it pass? Will I share my fresh and weird idea or abandon it? Will I advance to the podium when my name is called or conveniently get trapped in a bathroom stall? But once you choose to say the damn thing, the story’s just begun.


How we speak 
through
 each utterance is as important, if not more so, than whether we begin. This is about taking responsibility all the way till we’re done speaking. The alternative sounds like “I believe in something to my core … 
but, like, maybe not, what do you think
?” The back half of our offerings is where things get interesting, because in the project of taking up space with our speech, the proof is in the ending. That’s where we reveal, usually unintentionally, whether we’ve come to play. In a way, the first chapter of this book was already about size—what is required of our bodies when we’re preparing to speak to a lot of people or just one important one, and the amount of breath we need to pull it off. Good breath, well taken, is a huge accomplishment. But when our words hit air, new trials await. I wrote of letting your body expand into the actual space it inhabits, with breath, even when you’re being watched; here I want you to consider that your speech works the same way.


First of all, it helps to think of a person talking as not just opening and closing their mouth and emitting sounds but 
doing something
 to their listeners. Speech turns thought into action. If you ask a new colleague, “Do you want to grab lunch sometime?” you may never actually go for that lunch, but it doesn’t mean the offer has no value; it’s made them feel welcome, it’s made you feel that you’re the welcoming type, it’s begun a relationship. Linguists call even the most basic communication 
“making social moves” or performing “speech acts.” And though speech acts usually involve words, they’re not entirely about them: 
how
 you asked your colleague to lunch will define the exchange and maybe even the entire relationship moving forward. Were there notes of forced cheerfulness in your voice? Flirtation? An honest enthusiasm that became somehow muted by the end to cover that you secretly fear you’re not that cool and they’ll probably turn you down? Words emerge from inside us alongside a slew of other valuable intel in the form of pitch, volume, tone, and tempo—the music and rhythm of us in that moment, with all our habits and all our hopes. And as listeners, we pick up on all of it; we’ve had millennia to practice.


In the 1970s, a zoologist named Eugene Morton at the Smithsonian Institution studied the vocalizations of more than fifty bird and mammal species to chart their low growls and high squeaks, and the range of options between those two extremes.
6
 What he found connects with the endless possibilities embedded in our “Do you want to grab lunch sometime?”: the amount and range of sounds that animals make along that spectrum is directly related to how social those species are.


As journalist John Colapinto explains in his book 
This Is the Voice,
 “Those animals who live in larger cooperative groups, with more complex social interactions, produce and process signals with more points—more sounds—along the emotional spectrum.”
7
 Lizards, who will actually eat their own young if the babes don’t scamper away fast enough, don’t have the brain capacity for emotional attachments and cannot make a sound. We humans are the most socially complex species there is, and we’ve got the vocal options to prove it. We make a tiny, subtle shift in intonation and we’re offering concern but with a tinge 
of jealousy, or wistfulness with gratitude, or fury with an eye roll because we’re mad that we’re mad.


But we can also confuse people, and ourselves, with the smorgasbord of vocal options at our disposal. Because, to further complicate matters, we don’t just have options based on thousands of years of evolution, we also have shadings and nuance we sprinkle in based on modern trends we’ve picked up from our friends or idols. And sometimes those options turn into habits that run counter to our actual intentions. In other words, sometimes our speech acts are at cross-purposes with our speech.


Enter vocal fry and upspeak. Women around the world get ridiculed for sounding like bacon sizzling in a pan (vocal fry), or for pitching up at the end of statements (upspeak), as though we’re asking a question when we’re not, as well as using words like “like” and “I mean” when they’re not strictly necessary. We get pulled aside by charitable mentors, both men and women, who tell us these habits will undermine us and we should stop—like it’s a rite of passage to put away childish things. A friend in the audience of a workshop I ran overheard two older women whispering and caught one asking the other on the way out the door, “Do you think Samara used ‘like’ so much 
on purpose
?” My knee-jerk reaction upon hearing this was mortification; my next reaction a little quiet pride. I know the story.


For decades now, think piece after think piece has argued how these “self-imposed speech impediments” render Millennial women and younger generations incapable of sounding professional, let alone like a boss. Headlines from 
The Atlantic
 to 
The Guardian
 have proclaimed “Vocal Fry May Hurt Women’s Job Prospects” and “Young Women, Give Up the Vocal 
Fry and Reclaim Your Strong Female Voice.”
8
 Serious articles on gendered language are rife with pronouncements like “the use of uptalk is one of the most common elements undermining women’s credibility,”
9
 since it’s associated with hesitancy and uncertainty. Studies claim that these features of contemporary speech, sometimes even called “mallspeak,”
10
 have “taken on a negative, feminine connotation imbued with immaturity and even idiocy.”
11
 The story is that we ladies have inherited the delicate instrument that is our vocal apparatus, fine-tuned over hundreds of thousands of years, and are bashing it against the stage like wannabe rock stars forgetting people came to hear music.


I’d like to throw a finger at all this rhetoric and tell you not to listen to curmudgeons who resent that they’ve grown old and irrelevant 
and sound like it,
 but it’s not so simple. There is, after all, a lot of data to support the claims that upon hearing vocal fry and upspeak, gatekeepers tend to hold that gate shut. In one such study from 2014, funded by Duke University’s Fuqua School of Business, the analysts concluded that “in a large national sample of American adults we find that vocal fry is interpreted negatively. Relative to a normal speaking voice, young adult female voices exhibiting vocal fry are perceived as less competent, less educated, less trustworthy, less attractive, and less hirable.”
12
 If you want to get hired, this data matters, even if it’s based on vocal prejudice.


Perhaps you didn’t get a job you went for because someone decided you sounded too young, or you weren’t chosen to represent the company at that conference because “there’s just something about your voice” that rubs your boss the wrong way. Or maybe it rubs 
you
 the wrong way, so you demurred. I’ve heard these comments and similar from my clients. Who 
among us has not chosen at one time or another to turn off a podcast with a female host because her voice was too distracting? 
I have.
 If we’re talking about giving ourselves permission, inviting ourselves wholeheartedly into rooms of power, what do we do with all this evidence? Don’t let the bastards grind you down but also, according to science and even our own biases, speaking like you actually speak will hold you back. So good luck.


Too many conversations around women’s voices pick a side and stop there. Police them! Let them be! But this is a false binary too. We do not have only one way we speak, and not even only one 
authentic
 way we speak. We don’t take up some consistent but arbitrary amount of space when we talk, and no matter what our habits have been in the past, they don’t define our next breath or our next. Consider the variety of social acts you negotiate every day. How do you sound when you’re talking to your grandmother, or to a lawyer? How about someone who’s ignoring you, or someone who’s in love with you? Think about how you sound when you feel the least sure of yourself—and the most. Who is that latter version of you? The one who feels free. Now really picture her: How much space is she taking up, with her body and her voice? How does she move through a room? How does she sound? What would it take to bring 
her
 into more rooms with you?


The night of November 2, 2020, Glennon Doyle and Oprah Winfrey hopped onto an Instagram Live to discuss coping with the collective anxiety surrounding the U.S. presidential election. Here were two of the most powerful women in the world, both celebrated for their wisdom and leadership, both a fair bit older than your standard Millennial, and both extremely public about their respective histories of discomfort living in their 
skin and their relative peace at present. Here were two women showing up, with authority and heart, and easing the souls of thousands and thousands of viewers. And here were two women using vocal fry and upspeak off and on throughout the whole thing.


About a minute into the recording, talking about the impulse to anxiety-clean her house, Glennon says, “It’s like, a more hopeful version of you starts? And then you get halfway through and … [fried] oooh.”
13
 And Oprah picks up in all fry: “I’m not doing this todayyyyy. I knooooow.” Needless to say, they were effective anyway. If their goal was to offer robust but tender support and a true sense of community in a time of need, they did it.


Clearly, the story of women and vocal habits is more complex than simply excising vocal fry or upspeak or “like” from how you talk in order to be taken seriously. And it’s more complicated than simply listening to this speech coach or that one, this mentor or that one. And yes, perhaps Oprah and Glennon earned the right to speak however they want, but there’s more to learn from them than just pay your dues. What’s going on vocally with the two of them, and with all of us, has a hell of a lot more to do with permission and less to do with speech. In the video, they are inhabiting—truly living in—the moments that they choose to speak up, and 
speaking up at the size that works best for those moments
. You can get there too. Glennon and Oprah use vocal fry and upspeak, whether they think of it this way or not, as tools to serve their purposes. And not when they don’t. This strategy is also yours for the taking.


Fun fact: when we speak in English, we tend to “front-load” our utterances. We start out with a big burst of energy as we 
cross the threshold from not speaking to speaking. It’s when we have the most breath at the ready and perhaps the most nerve because of all that built-up anticipation, like a runner at the starting line, raring to go. It’s natural to then expend that energy as we speak.


But if we’re uncomfortable speaking for any reason at all, the effects of front-loading become even more dramatic. As we start saying words, the racetrack bends unexpectedly and perhaps we flag: we see the room, the unreadable or hostile expressions, and we may begin to question our right to the space and time we’re trying to inhabit. We might start thinking, Maybe my idea isn’t so good after all. I should have thought it through more. I look dumb. I definitely don’t sound like that other person who spoke with all that confidence. He looked and sounded the part. What am I doing? Who do I think I am?


That big burst of energy we had at the top dissipates, and we sound less and less bold by the end of our statement, resulting in all kinds of “never mind, I take it back” energy—also known as vocal fry and upspeak (or uptalk, or, if you want to get really technical about it, a high-rise terminal pitch). Since most of the JFK-esque voice standards I described in the Introduction fly below the radar, I’m not surprised that some clients come to me sure that vocal fry and upspeak are ruining their lives, while others have never heard the terms at all. So, here’s the nitty-gritty. Vocal fry is the creaky sound speakers produce, often in the second half of a spoken phrase. (Try saying “I mean …” sardonically, dragging the phrase out as though no more needs to be said, and you’ll probably hear it in your own voice.) It’s defined as “a form of phonation [that just means sound production], characterized by a distinct laryngeal vibratory pattern”
14
 in the vocal cords, basically slow vibrations 
that produce a glottal effect reminiscent of bacon frying. It happens to most of us when we’re tired and not energizing our words with enough breath, or if we’re hoarse from talking too much at the end of a long day. But it happens also when we’re not tired at all, for all kinds of juicy reasons I’ll get to. Examples of it are everywhere, but our modern pop-culture paragon of vocal fry is Kim Kardashian and those who try to keep up with her. And lest you think this is easy fodder for stereotyping, remember: Oprah. Plus me. And probably you.


In case you’re wondering, men have picked up the habit as well: Ira Glass copped to using vocal fry regularly in a famous 
This American Life
 episode called “Freedom Fries,” where he responded to all the complaints he’d received about the voices of the young female reporters on his show by pointing out that no one complained about 
his
 voice. Indeed, it’s an everyone trend, but women are the ones who are maligned for it.


A Google search for the term “vocal fry” turns up hundreds of those think pieces aimed at women, alleging to save us from professional suicide; there are no equivalent articles for men, and often we don’t even notice when men do it. There’s an amazing video you can look up online that features an associate professor from Duke University’s Fuqua School of Business discussing how that 2014 study of theirs found that vocal fry in women is correlated with the perception of a lower sense of authority.
15
 But the researcher in question, a mid-forties Tom Cruise type whose head is center screen for most of the video, 
speaks with vocal fry at the end of almost every one of his statements
. It simply must not have occurred to a single person involved in making this vocal-fry-shaming project to even listen for it.


Upspeak is a cousin of vocal fry, and you can think of them 
as two manifestations of the same impulse, the impulse to shrink—to “never mind” at the end of your statements. Upspeak involves inserting a questioning pitch into a non-question, so the end of the thought swoops up, regardless of the content—even, say, telling someone what your name is? Classic caricatures of this vocal roller coaster include Alicia Silverstone in the 1995 film 
Clueless
 and Kristen Wiig in the 
Saturday Night Live
 skit “The Californians,” but really, it’s everywhere. A friend texted me the audio clip of an NPR correspondent saying, “Biden ran on the most progressive climate agenda in U.S. history? And won on it? So, it’s incumbent on him to take that seriously?” I listened over and over, rapt.


No one agrees exactly on the origin of upspeak, but the concept may have entered the cultural consciousness when composer Frank Zappa released his 1982 single “Valley Girl,” which featured recordings of his fourteen-year-old daughter speaking phrases associated with her young, white, upper-class peer group in Southern California. But now upspeak has become dissociated from this particular demographic; it’s on the Internet and the news, it’s coming out of most of our mouths no matter where in the English-speaking world we live, it’s coming out of our daughters’ (and sons’) mouths as young as three years old.


And then there’s “hedging,” a linguistic catchall term for everything that might be construed as a “filler” word, from “um” and “uh” to frequent use of the word “like” to softening terms we might throw into a pronouncement, such as, “I mean, like, maybe you should just talk to him about your, you know, concerns or whatever.” It’s tough to find anyone who doesn’t harbor secret fears that they’re guilty of hedging too much and undermining their credibility. The common assumption is that 
direct speech is strong, and that strength is the goal. Why ruin a perfectly good straight shot with frivolous detours and pit stops?


Finally, there’s a habit that resides outside specific vocal tendencies but that undergirds the whole lot of these trends—and it’s that women apologize. A lot. We can probably each point to examples of excessive apologies coming out of our own mouths or the mouths of women we know. I have a friend named Mandy who’s a community organizer and activist. She recently started a meeting I attended by apologizing, for literally nothing. She hadn’t begun the meeting late. She wasn’t ill prepared. She had hurt no one; far from it, she was helping all of us. But “Sorry, hi, everyone!” popped out of her mouth as smoothly as “welcome” might have. It reminded me of when she won a coveted spot on a state committee. Because it had taken a few days to certify the results, her big announcement began with “Sorry, I have been slow to let you know the outcome of Tuesday’s election!” I cringed.


But because I want to be honest with the fraught topic of how women speak up, and not default to easy answers that are useless in our real lives, I’m going to be obscenely evenhanded about the benefits and drawbacks of these trends. If you’re hoping for a guide on what to do and what to avoid, you won’t find it here. Instead, I want you to know your options, and I want you to know what they do to your listeners. Then it’s up to you to give yourself the permission to play and the grace to get it wrong and recover. I want you to think about who you’re trying to connect with and 
what will work with them.
 I also want you to consider what about your voice brings you joy and, above all, to sound like the version of yourself that’s more joyful. We shrink and expand in relation to one another, all 
day, every day. It’s painful and heartbreaking and beautiful, the way we accommodate others and sometimes, thankfully, ourselves.


Here’s an example of what I mean, if you worry you apologize too much. I remember nodding vigorously as I sat on set reading a 2019 
New York Times
 piece by author Ruth Whippman, highlighting nearly all of it on my phone. She suggests, “Surely, many of our most pressing social and political problems—from #MeToo to campus rape, school shootings to President Trump’s Twitter posturing—are caused not by a lack of assertiveness in women but by an over-assertiveness among men.”
16
 She quotes a famous 2010 study that concluded that women say they’re sorry more often than men because we have a “lower threshold for what constitutes offensive behavior,” and wonders why this is framed as a female deficiency. Why should fostering a “high threshold for offensive behavior” be anyone’s goal? She warns us against assuming traditional masculine standards:




Rarely in the course of this anti-apologizing crusade do we ever stop to consider the social and moral value of apologies and the cost of obliterating them from our interactions. Apologizing is a highly symbolic and socially efficient way to take responsibility for our actions, to right a wrong and clear space for another person’s feelings. It’s a routine means of injecting self-examination and moral reflection into daily life.




Hallelujah. My kid’s school borrows the language of psychotherapy and teaches its students about social repairs—don’t just say the word “sorry” if you’ve messed up but also take re
sponsibility for the harm you may have caused and articulate what you’ll do in the future so it doesn’t happen again. It’s very Ruth Whippman of them. Clearly, telling women or anyone to stop apologizing whole-hog is foolish; our society benefits from righting wrongs and repairing rends.


But this is where the work begins: each of us has to take a hard look at our own habits. Is your impulse to apologize born of a hurt, no matter how minor, you wish to heal? An imbalance you’d like to even out? Or is it, perhaps, more generic than that? Is it a reflexive reaction that doesn’t actually fit the occasion? Because if, like my friend Mandy, nothing specific in front of you requires repair, then you will sound like you’re apologizing for taking up time and space—and inadvertently offering to repair your very existence. It’s hard to shrink any further than that.


As we dig into the choices we each have to use vocal fry and upspeak and hedging and apology, bear this in mind: they 
are
 choices, and practical ones at that. They may be just the right tool for the job. But they may not, and we must wield them with care because there’s one thing these habits all have in common: they keep the peace. They tell the powers that be that we know our place. And especially as we expand into newer, more powerful places, these linguistic curlicues act as an homage to the size we were before, back when we weren’t making claims on time and space that used to belong to others. That’s quite a function. There are benefits to apologizing when it’s unwarranted and there are benefits to ending our pronouncements with a whimper rather than a bang. In fact, here’s something the think pieces never mention: the same people who scoff at our vocal fry and upspeak would likely be even 
more
 uncomfortable with the version of our voice that’s 
strong all the way to the end; they just don’t realize the whole damn thing’s a setup.


But it’s a setup we can outwit.


First of all, listen for vocal fry in yourself and your friends. Listen when your favorite movie stars go on the talk show circuit and YouTubers post new videos. You’ll start to notice that every time their voice enters that scrapy lower register it 
does
 something. One function of vocal fry’s throwaway quality is that it can be employed to help you carve out an aside or a parenthetical, which can be a valuable way of signaling high priority and low priority thoughts for the listener. (Watch Emma Stone and Jennifer Lawrence together in a loose and chatty 
W Magazine
 shoot and you’ll hear it all over. J-Law says to Emma, “You’ve watched 
Land Before
—no wait! We’ve already [goes fried] talked about [then even more fried] 
Land Before Time
.”)
17
 Indeed, another function of vocal fry is to reference an element of irony or humor. Relatedly, it can help us acknowledge that what we’re saying is tropey and we know it, like the aural equivalent of scare quotes. I use it like that all the time. It’s in Oprah’s “I knoooow.” And in Jennifer suggesting Emma’s real name is a great porn star name. Without missing a beat, Emma says in all-fry, “That’s … really nice of you to say.” And Jen responds, equally fried, “You’re welcome.”


When we speak with vocal fry at work around our superiors it can also serve as a trial balloon—tentatively suggesting something with a keen eye to how it’s received. It functions as an effective way to throw an idea out but then take it back just a little bit at the end for safety’s sake, in case someone in a more powerful position disagrees with us. That’s not necessarily a bad thing: we may well be signaling that we have an idea, but we’re not rigid; we’re open to others’ views. In any case, it’s 
a protective move, indicating we can withstand disagreement and we’re low-maintenance.


Indeed, vocal fry is associated with a quality of chill. Speech language pathologists will tell you it’s not possible to speak in vocal fry if your throat is tight. It 
requires
 that the vocal cords be relaxed. Professional singers will tell you that deliberately frying their voice while holding a vowel sound is actually part of a healthy vocal warm-up—it relaxes tension in the cords. There’s a reason academic papers on the phenomenon use words like “uninterested,” “detached,” and “unenthusiastic” to try to capture the spirit of the sound (or of the user … the line blurs). And since vocal fry can imply a relaxed attitude, you may feel the impulse to use it to signal a downshift into a casual tone from a more formal one. In all these cases, its use can read as confidence because you’re, like, so cool in that room. At the intersection of gender and age bias lies this widespread cultural assumption that “childlike” enthusiasm and zeal marks you as naïve. This drives me mad, and I have a lot to say about it (see the following chapter). Here, I’ll offer that if vocal frying the ends of your remarks reads as cynical and just plain over it, it can be a pretty useful defense against being branded as girly or wide-eyed. In short, this vocal flourish can keep us uncommitted and unknowable, which is always safer than committed and known. As Aaron Burr says in 
Hamilton,
 “Talk less; smile more. Don’t let them know what you’re against or what you’re for.” He’s not the one we love, but he does manage to stay alive.


Vocal fry has become such a common element of modern speech that it serves one more important function: it’s a membership card to the club. As Deborah Tannen wrote in her pop-linguistic classic 
You Just Don’t Understand: Women and 
Men in Conversation,
 “There’s a fundamental human impulse to imitate what we hear.”
18
 She says, “Teenagers talk this way because other teenagers talk this way, and they want to sound like their peers.” No matter your age, it can give you social capital to sound current, and it feels good to sound like the people you associate yourself with or want to. If enough of us are vibrating our vocal cords in this manner, then a well-placed, super-fried “let’s be honest” or “too soon” or “amaaaazing” will inevitably help build trust and connection with one another.


In fact, in any of your utterances you might use vocal fry not because you’ve got an impulse to shrink but because you’re mirroring your friends, fitting into your tribe, signaling you belong. And what’s wrong with sounding like the people you love? It’s literally why there are differences in how we speak all over the world. Language variation is the result of millennia of tribal us-versus-them-ness presenting in how we speak. Imagine a lost caveperson coming upon a group of hunters and breathing a sigh of relief because they all grunt in matching vocal fry.


And this is certainly a reason why characterizing your own vocal fry as either good or bad is pretty useless: a lot of the time, it simply helps us connect. It helps us be funny and relevant and real. It’s a habit that feels entirely comfortable to quite a lot of us, and the more comfortable we are when we speak, the more likely we’ll feel free and joyful. And that’s when our greatest communication happens.


Some of the earliest groundbreaking research on upspeak came from Dr. Cynthia McLemore, who was a fly on the wall at one particular Texas sorority in the late 1980s.
19
 She studied the sisters’ intonation in various contexts and discovered some
thing profound: swooping up at the end of sentences wasn’t used to suggest uncertainty or deference. Au contraire. It was most employed by the sorority’s leaders and senior members as a helpful rhetorical device, to tag new information. For example, “Okay, so we’re having a party? On Saturday? That’s fire and ice themed?” But upspeak 
wasn’t
 used for previously discussed information, like “As you all know, we’re raising funds for our new hot tub.”


In many cases that’s still how upspeak functions today—to highlight something new (go up) instead of something already established (go down, maybe even into fry). That NPR correspondent—you know? The one talking about Biden?—was offering a fresh take, rather than repeating some universally acknowledged truth. And as is the case when we introduce ourselves (and, ahem, especially if our name is unusual), our intention is likely not just to dole out the name but also to check that it lands. We’re stating new information and asking 
Do you know what I mean?
 at the same time. It’s efficient. And it’s about taking into account the experience of our listener.


In fact, various studies suggest upspeak is a generous act, even if it’s one we’re not always conscious of committing. It helps buoy conversation; it’s like a welcome sign with our voice. Linguists have found that in chat among men versus among women (leaving aside mixed-gender company, for the moment), men tend to add to a discussion to compete while women do so to collaborate. The upward energy at the end of a statement suggests an open-ended offering instead of a closed one, a built-in “But what do you think?”


According to Amanda Montell in her book 
Wordslut: A Feminist Guide to Taking Back the English Language,
 women’s conversations “have a distinctive turn-taking structure—
a style of talk that [British linguist Dr. Jennifer] Coates likens to a musical jam session.”
20
 In other words, when women talk together, the floor is open and the hierarchy is flattened; everyone involved is saying, without saying, 
Come on in.
 Montell points out that “in such conversations, you might hear overlapping talk, speakers repeating one another, or rephrasing each other’s words. Everyone is working together to construct meaning, and thus the one-speaker-at-a-time rule does not apply.” The open-ended energy of upspeak is like a handoff to the next person to make her mark on the discussion because the conversation itself is more important than any one conversationalist. Needless to say, if men don’t traditionally converse this way but instead take turns holding the floor and “playing expert,” mixed-gender conversations can easily tangle.


When I work with actors on scripted language, we tend to talk about these pitch options at the end of the line of dialogue in terms of “open or closed,” referring to whether the arc of the pitch curves up at the end of the thought or down. It may seem minute, but choices like these reflect a character’s state of mind. “I’m gonna go” on the page can sound like “I’m gonna … go?” with an upward energy at the end, which suggests checking in with the other character, maybe it’s a threat or a test, or maybe it’s seeking permission, but in any case, it’s 
seeking something
. Upward energy engages the other person—it’s a request for a reaction. And it’s a generous act. “I’m gonna go” with a downward energy, where you really play the period the writer wrote at the end of the line, suggests someone who’s truly done, who has shut down the conversation. There’s nothing inherently wrong with that, but it’s a choice. As speech acts go, these two options offer a stark contrast—which can even be relationship-defining, in more dramatic scenes. And so it is 
with us. The benefit of upspeak is that it keeps us open—vocally, emotionally, philosophically, all ways.


In fact, hedging is a generous act as well. Adding fillers or softening terms to your statements—um, uh, I mean, just, kinda, like—are what critics call vocal tics, little tells that we lack confidence or clearheadedness. And some of the time, that may be the case. But savvy linguists call these vocal tactics, not tics. Tactics to achieve what? To take care of each other, vocally. Dr. Coates, the linguist who defined conversational jam sessions, explains that hedges “are used to respect the face needs of all participants, to negotiate sensitive topics, and to encourage the participation of others.”
21
 We’re often acutely aware of face needs, though we may not use that term—it’s helping everyone around us avoid humiliation or the appearance of being wrong in order to keep things running smoothly. Softening terms do this heavy lifting and, according to a famous study that analyzed 263 separate transcriptions, they’re “more common among women, younger participants, and more conscientious people.”
22
 We hedge when we don’t have power, or not enough. We help the more powerful save face, stay in their good graces, and earn a little sway in return.


And “um” and “uh” are generous as well. They’re actually tiny, helpful signposts on the path toward finishing our thoughts so listeners don’t get lost. And it turns out they’re used relatively evenly among men and women. If you’re worried that they’re awkward indicators that you’re floundering, you might find comfort in considering them instead as well-placed hints to your listeners that you’re about to take a brief pause to find a word. In fact, there are studies suggesting that we instinctively tend to use “uh” when we’re about to take a short pause, and “um” for a slightly longer one. And if we fear 
we’re uh-ing all over the place, according to one study, we actually use these signals of an upcoming delay mostly mid-thought, not in between thoughts. It seems we really do just want to make sure that if our language lacks flow, we acknowledge it and help it along. “Uh” and “um” don’t detract from our communication; they are part of our communication. Just like a big in-breath at the top of a tough statement, or a sigh at the end, they mean something to our listeners—and something not at all bad.


And “like” is just unfairly maligned.
23
 Canadian linguist Dr. Alexandra D’Arcy points out there are six, count ’em, 
six
 completely distinct meanings of “like.” Is it our fault they’re all pronounced the same? English speakers tend to not even notice that the two oldest and least contentious of them—
I like you
 (verb), and 
but not like that
 (preposition)—are completely different words with unrelated meanings and origins. They are just homonyms, waltzing around confusing everyone. Check out Montell’s 
Wordslut
 for an exquisite takedown of the “like” critics and an explanation of all six “likes.” Google the definition of “like” to get a taste. Suffice it to say: it turns out that almost every single one is used as much by men as by women—including “I’ve had this, like, forever.” Except that when men do it, it’s called speaking, and when women do it, it’s considered an annihilation of the English language and, apparently, our credibility along with it.


So, here’s the deal: These habits—vocal fry, upspeak, hedging, excessive apology—clearly have their benefits. Many beautiful and useful and relationship-nurturing benefits. But, you knew this was coming, they also have their drawbacks. One, of course, is how these vocal signals are perceived by people who don’t use them (or don’t know they use them), 
people who may very well be the gatekeepers to the future you want. It could be worth it to explore other options simply to get through the bolted gate.


But to be fair, those gatekeepers aren’t all just shortsighted bias machines. Vocal fry might read as “uncertain” to some listeners, or “cool” to others, but either way, 
if it’s a means of hiding, the result is that you sound like you’re hiding.
 Listeners may well just be confused, wondering if you mean what you’re saying or not. And upspeak, too, can be confusing. If we habitually go up on every clause as well as at the end of every thought, it’s easy for the ear (and not just old, crotchety ones, alas) to get lost in the repetitive pattern of swoops and lose the logic of what we’re actually saying.


But there’s another profound reason to reconsider all these habits. Below the surface of so many of their benefits is a hard truth: our parents, our dolls, our magazines, our teachers, our crushes, our clothing options, have told us in ways subtle and obvious that taking up too much space is unfeminine, and that extends to the size of our voices too. According to Penelope Eckert and Sally McConnell-Ginet in their seminal feminist linguistics book, 
Language and Gender,
 “From infancy, male and female children are interpreted differently, and interacted with differently. Experimental evidence suggests that adults’ perceptions of babies are affected by their beliefs about the babies’ sex.”
24
 For example, “adults judged a 24-hour-old baby as bigger if they believed it to be a boy, and finer-featured if they believed it to be a girl.” And not surprisingly, these judgments affect the way people interact with babies and toddlers and bigger kids too. “People handle infants more gently when they believe them to be female, more playfully when they believe them to be male. And they talk to them differently. Parents use 
more diminutives (
kitty, doggie
) when speaking to girls than to boys.” Is it any wonder that by the time we’re women we’ve perfected diminutive? That we take up as little space as possible with our voices too?


Listen: private, intimate, and deliberately casual conversations among pals might benefit from fluent use of the vocal tactics above. In fact, that’s what Glennon and Oprah were doing—they just let us watch. Same with Emma and Jen. But big, public presentations, or even the sort of private conversations where the stakes are high, the ideas are consequential, and there’s the chance power might shift in the room, are just different. The difference is that 
it is not a jam session anymore.
 You do not have to share the floor, and in fact, you shouldn’t. You do not need to lean on the vocal tactics you’ve picked up to stay small, be generous, and get by in rooms of power. Keeping the peace isn’t the only goal anymore—it may actually not be your goal at all.


We are wildly social creatures who pick up on whispers in the air, tiny shifts in allegiance or trust among friends or coworkers, and we vary our voices accordingly. But we do not always know how to read a bigger or less familiar room. So we bring our habits with us, unexamined, and every habit above—from vocal fry to upspeak, hedging, and apologizing—helps us stay unintimidating and small when big might seal the deal. We accidentally reflect our lifetime of squeezing rather than our intention to expand.


In Oprah’s famous 2018 Golden Globes speech, when she spoke to a solemn audience wearing all black in honor of #TimesUp and told the painful story of Recy Taylor, she left the vocal fry in the wings.
25
 We, too, can choose to expand or contract our voice to the size of our audience’s need for scope 
or intimacy, big or little, lofty or light. We, too, can choose how we interact with our crowd, choose what sort of relationship we’re building with them. Our actual voice can reflect these choices, laden with markers of conversational and generational style—or not. And it’s 
still us
.


So, who is this enormous-venue version of you who hogs the floor and owns the room and throws in some trendy vocal flourishes when she pleases but not when she doesn’t? I want to know this woman who is unyielding in getting to the end of her thought and hands it over without apology because she matches her voice to the size of her mission.


In order to take charge of what might previously have been habits rather than conscious vocal choices, my favorite trick is the Imaginary Ball exercise. It’s a chance to get clear about your options, to grab hold of your vocal energy and experiment with throwing it up (upspeak), down (vocal fry), or out (scary as hell). This exercise will help you notice your own habits, play with breaking them, and help you learn to take up the right amount of space for your goals. (P.S. There’s no official “right” amount. It’s about what feels good, brave, expressive, and true, and what works with your listeners. Did I mention, this is a practice?)


To do this exercise, stand up, ideally alone in a room, and get in ready stance—feet apart, breathing from as low as possible. Now use your arm to throw an imaginary ball (the size of, say, an orange) underhand, up toward the ceiling, while saying something uncomplicated, like “Hello, my name is ___.” Try to match your pitch to the curve of the ball’s trajectory, so your voice swoops from low to high as your ball does. Really match it; it’s okay if it sounds more singsong than normal speech. 
Time the flick of your wrist and imaginary ball release to the most important part of the thought, likely your name. Next, throw this imaginary ball down, matching the pitch again. Then throw the ball out, with so much force that it feels like the ball—and your voice—will hit the wall across the room. Step back some more, so you can throw it even farther. And yes, it’s totally cool if you feel silly; you’re throwing a fake ball. That looks like an orange.


Check in with your body. Are you tensing your neck, or bracing and forgetting to breathe? Notice especially if you get louder when you try to throw the ball straight out—“my name is SAMARA!!!” That’s an understandable first step, but this isn’t about volume; it’s about intention. It’s about Megan Rapinoe’s fingers energized 
beyond
 the fingers themselves. If letting the end of your thought bend either up or down reroutes the energy away from your target, explore what it takes to hit your target. Is it more energy? Experiment with how quietly you can hit your target with your words. How intensely. Now with how much ease. How much charm. How much 007 bravado. Sometimes clients laugh, not because the exercise is silly but because saying something like you mean it, without taking it back or hedging, feels surprisingly dangerous.


The next step, after you get comfortable with throwing that pretend ball out and matching the trajectory with pitch and intention, is to speak aloud more and more complicated and emotionally charged statements:




I need help.
 (Go back to 
Chapter 1
 and try the variations I offered, but this time think about the end of the thought too.)


My idea will work. Trust me.


I am the best person for the job.


I believe that ___ is the greatest food in the universe.


I’m sure I’m right.


I’m speaking.




Personalize this for yourself. While throwing out the pretend ball in the relative safety of a room all alone, try saying something you’re proud of. Try saying something you’re bad at, but own the heck out of it. No “kind of” or “um” for now. Share something you like about yourself like it’s an assured fact. Share a secret that you haven’t told anyone, and mean it all the way to the end. Try it again. Challenge yourself.


Now see if you can drop the hand gesture and keep the feeling of throwing the ball out. Get familiar with the feeling of hitting that target; the more we hear ourselves do it, the more normal it becomes. Record yourself and listen back to the recording. Try it twenty-five times and by the end you won’t be thinking, 
I hate the sound of my voice;
 you’ll be thinking, 
I’ve got this
.


Alex is an epidemiologist in her early thirties I met when we were on set together for a Dwayne “The Rock” Johnson film in Atlanta during the fall of 2020—I was coaching; she was consulting on Covid protocols. We were both outside on a sunny Saturday afternoon with our laptops, sitting more than six feet apart, on a long, rounded bench in an abandoned amphitheater. We were both writing books, we discovered, and she told me that she’d recently been on a bunch of news shows as a guest expert. She was pleased (while, of course, suitably horrified) that her hard-earned PhD was now so relevant, and was in the process of turning her dissertation in for publication. 
After one interview on CNN, she received an email from the female anchor, who offered some stern advice. She told Alex, You kept stopping yourself. Don’t stop yourself; let us do that. Expand to take up the time you have and trust that we want to know what you have to say.


Alex said it blew her mind; she didn’t realize she needed that permission, but she did. It brought me back to college: In my senior year I finally got a leading role, playing Mrs. Lovett in a production of 
Sweeney Todd.
 The formidable head of the acting program sat in on a final rehearsal and asked to speak with me alone at the end. I was nervous as I approached him, resisting needing his approval but desperate for it nonetheless. The professor said, “Samara, you’re giving away all your power. You can be fantastic in this, but you’re being too generous. Pretend the show’s called 
Mrs. Lovett
.” It was a huge whoa moment for me. He was right. I had been turning to my costar instead of taking the spotlight for myself. I had been exceedingly generous when that wasn’t the job at all.


Just saying, I killed it up there. (Literally—it’s a musical about murder.) But seriously, I found so much joy and freedom after I was given that permission. And joy and freedom are what get us to take up the space we deserve. If you need permission to be the main character in your life, here it is. We think of “entitled” as a dirty word, but there are two very different definitions. As an adjective, “entitled” means snobby, believing you deserve special treatment. But as a verb it means to have 
just claim
 to receive or to do something. You have 
just claim
 to take up space. You are entitled to it. And let’s be honest: I’m happy to give you permission, like that professor did, but it works best if you give it to yourself.


Permission is always about vanquishing pesky limiting be
liefs, and this is why it’s a practice: when we conquer one round of ’em, there are more that are happy to enter the fray. It’s the former president of Princeton University sitting on that stage nodding at all of us, telling us that uncertainty may never go away entirely. It’s AOC, who told 
Vanity Fair
 journalist Michelle Ruiz about the fear she had when she was younger of failing the memory of her father: “I used to, frankly, abuse myself mentally about how I’m nothing.”
26
 She added, “I realized that I need to choose myself because if I don’t, I’m just going to waste away.” In other words: she gave herself permission to take up space. But she still needed to sit on her couch and practice it and then practice some more.


When you feel those limiting beliefs tug at your joy and freedom, when you hear that infamous imposter syndrome whisper, 
You’re not up for this,
 in a voice that sounds like wisdom, please remind yourself that in the entire history of the world, women are still seconds into this having-a-voice-in-public thing. As my dear friend creativity coach Liz Kimball wrote in a 
Medium
 piece on imposter syndrome, “Most of us are aware of the professional and strategic value of becoming better advocates of our work, taking a stand for our accomplishments, and accepting compliments, but it can be helpful to remember that many of us are the first generation in the history of our families and cultures to do so (just think how regularly you heard your grandmother or a female relative deflect compliments or downplay her magnificence).”
27
 I know I have. We are disrupting some seriously entrenched shit.


And when it feels like an impossible task, here are some permission hacks I’ve collected to free up your voice. First, when you worry you’re a fraud, ask yourself: Who can I help? I say yes to almost every email I receive from someone seeking mentor
ship; those conversations reframe my struggles into lessons better than any self-affirmations ever could. When we help mentees, siblings, friends, and anyone coming up behind us or wracked with a problem we’ve already solved for ourselves, it reminds us of what we’ve overcome and how much we actually do know what we’re doing after all. It’s a gift to see ourselves through their eyes, and it reminds us how powerful we are.


Here’s another one: I don’t often turn to religion for speech coaching tools but I’m also exceedingly practical; why ignore ancient wisdom that’s helped countless people, if it might help us? So go with me on this. After she left the White House, the Obamas’ speechwriter Sarah Hurwitz wrote a book about discovering Judaism. It’s called 
Here All Along
 and in it, she describes a rabbi named Yitz Greenberg who speaks of “three inalienable dignities”:
28




We are each of infinite worth—no one is expendable, and we cannot quantify the value of any human life.


We are all fundamentally equal—no human being is any more important than any other human being.


We are each totally unique—there is no one else like us, and no one is interchangeable with anybody else.




We have value, we’re of equal value, but we’re all different. If you’re like me, you’re probably nodding, sure, sure. We were taught this as kids; we teach our own kids this stuff. You don’t have to be a member of any religion to recognize that these “dignities” are a set of values that all boil down to treating people fairly. The thing is, though, many of us aren’t living in alignment with our values, either because we have biases we haven’t considered and actually 
are
 treating people unfairly, or be
cause 
we’re treating ourselves unfairly
. Do you believe you are of infinite worth? Do you believe you are of equal importance as a human being compared to your boss or that celebrity or that brilliant speaker who’s got it all together? Do you believe that you are unique and that it is exactly your weirdness that renders you of infinite worth? Or are you denying yourself the regard you give others?


I think it’s also worthwhile to poke at the spirit of generosity bound up in the vocal patterns discussed in this chapter. Generosity is about giving. But we 
can
 give too much. We can perform gratitude till we have nothing left. Sometimes the nudge we get to be grateful, blinking at us from a meme or proffered in a yoga class, is a reminder to appreciate what we’ve taken for granted. Being grateful can lift our spirits. But sometimes the nudge we get to be grateful is a censure, a reminder to play the part. Maybe it comes in the form of “I don’t deserve to ask for more; I should be grateful for what I’ve got.” This kind of gratitude is a practice many of us have honed over a lifetime of performing it, in deference to those in positions of power. But this kind of gratitude amounts to policing ourselves against having correct responses to unfair circumstances. It erodes the spirit. So, here’s another permission hack. It’s a quick flow chart on how to navigate constructive versus destructive gratitude:




Is it something that makes me feel warm and connected? It’s gratitude.


Is it something that makes me feel cold and isolated? It’s not.




Just like an apology, a 
thank-you
 can be sincere and serve to even out an imbalance—or it can feel obligatory and synthetic, 
a gratitude show we perform for an audience we’re teaching to continue undervaluing us. It serves a purpose, but as with all the vocal tactics I described in this chapter, the purpose is to keep us safe, not help us grow.


Stacey Abrams knew she was “supposed to say nice things and accept [her] fate”
29
 after the 2018 Georgia gubernatorial race was stolen from her, thanks to systematic voter suppression. But she decided not to play the gratitude game anymore. In 
Lead from the Outside,
 she writes:




As outsiders, we are expected to continue on as the system commands, primarily to preserve our ability to participate and, just maybe, one day, win the lottery of opportunity to be the one who slips inside the door. I fully recognize the process and how effective it is. My problem is, I don’t like it, and I believe we can fix it.




This posture of gratitude, if we habitually adopt it even when circumstances are unfair, in no way prepares us to take up the space we deserve once we do slip inside the door. We are done clutching our lottery ticket, hoping for good luck. We’re making our own luck.


For me, when imposter syndrome strikes, I get claustrophobic. I forget how interconnected I am to those who are most dear to me, who, of course, would never call me an imposter. The voice that says, 
You’re not up for this
 or 
They’re gonna find out you’re a fake
 is really saying, 
You are not of value—you actually
 shouldn’t 
believe in yourself
. But everything shifts when I make a list of the people who do value me. So, this is your last permission hack: consider your sphere of influence, those who care about you and care about what you 
have to say. We are not just the size of our bodies in a room, or the size of our voices as they hit air, we’re also the size of our influence. We influence everyone who’s ever loved us. Everyone who’s ever celebrated us. Everyone who’s ever learned something new from us. And we’re actually already enormous.

















3


Emotion




If women had also been the protagonists of societies’ teaching tales, swords in stones and bombs bursting in air would have been no more laudatory than educating children and tending the garden …. The culture would not only revere the strong and silent type; it would also be cool to be talkative, brave to cry, noble to feel and relate.


—ELIZABETH LESSER,
 
Cassandra Speaks
1




My client Petra was called into a performance review at her investment firm and told that she had a bad habit of getting too excited when things went well and too upset when things went badly—and that she needed to learn to “compartmentalize better.” She remembers nodding at this, her face neutral while her mind lighted on all the times she’d already stifled herself at work. She remembers trying to keep very still while her body felt as if it was screaming like creatures from Dante’s 
Inferno
.


Petra isn’t the first to be told that pleasant detachment is the outlook of choice around the office and that emotions are dangerous territory for women. It’s a story that dates back to when emotions were called “humours,” a term coined in ancient Greece to describe bodily fluids like bile, which the Greeks believed dictated temperament, and when uteruses 
were thought to migrate around the body, rubbing up on various organs and throwing women’s humours out of whack. In those days, women’s tongues were ripped out for caring enough to speak up about injustice—say, naming their rapists. And a thousand or so years later, inconveniently loud and truth-telling women were burned at the stake, then in the twentieth century institutionalized or as late as the 1980s lobotomized if they didn’t act dutifully repressed. Anything to shut us up. Anything to keep those compartments firmly in place.


Today, we’re more likely to be merely humiliated or dismissed or gossiped about if we appear overly emotional. But we get the message: at the office, or on that regular Zoom meeting, we shouldn’t care too much or we’ll seem crazy—a word whose power to shame reverberates through time and space, from witch trials of yore to the systematic discrediting of survivors today. And if we accidentally come across as overeager or oversensitive or over-anything despite our best attempts to squelch it, we run the risk of being labeled 
too much
. The fear of that label looms, outsized, over every woman I know. And in ways both massive and microscopic, it muzzles us.


So maybe you, like me, like Petra, do this thing sometimes that feels like you are hiding your heart. Maybe you slouch your shoulders or cross your arms. Maybe you perfect a neutral face and steady voice, and you say “It’s fine” when it isn’t and “I’m good” when you’re not. Maybe you feel your throat tighten as you tamp down on your organic reactions, but reason with yourself that it’s the cost of doing business. Indeed, according to researchers who contributed a chapter to the 2016 
Handbook on Well-Being of Working Women,
 women rack up social and economic penalties for acting otherwise. 
“They are judged as lacking emotional control, which ultimately undermines women’s competence and professional legitimacy.”
2
 So, why 
wouldn’t
 we hide our emotions?


But this tendency to hide has a bigger cost when you bring practiced detachment with you onto a larger stage and when you’re speaking about work that’s closer to your heart. You might talk about what you care about, but sound like you don’t. Maybe this has worked at some point in the past; neutral can often pass for cool and collected. But in my experience, it only works up to a point—the point where you want to (a) feel alive and (b) become the most powerful and potent version of yourself.


“The way we use pitch, tone, or color in language has a purpose,”
3
 wrote twentieth-century speech and oratory scholar Dr. Wallace Bacon, “to arouse feelings in the listener necessary to convey the meaning to the listener.” It’s such a fundamental aspect of communication that it’s easy to take for granted: in order to convey the true meaning of what we’re saying, we need to pass along to our listeners not only our words but the emotions that go with them. We need to pass along the intention or spirit that motivated the words to begin with. That is the actual unit of communication. Our natural speech could be as flat as a robot’s if all we needed to convey was language. And if that were true, we probably wouldn’t have evolved to have the stunning anatomical apparatus that is our vocal cords.


But we have. And our ears have evolved to hear the emotions in others’ voices and feel roused by them. “The same neuronal areas of the brain are activated by seeing someone emotionally aroused as being emotionally aroused oneself,”
4
 confirms Dr. Michael Trimble, a behavioral neurologist at University College London. It’s an ancient dance our bodies were 
built for: we speak with emotion, it elicits emotion in our listeners, our nervous systems engage with one another, and we feel connection. We feel alive.


My client Juliana was taking a massive leap in moving from working as a full-time reality-TV editor to pitching her own projects to direct. She came to me because she could tell that her pitching style needed a boost: she sounded so flat and businesslike that she wasn’t moving the room, and the room wasn’t buying her pitches. She won the neutral game but lost out on real engagement.


My client Chris was pitching his latest TV show idea around Hollywood and came to me because he noticed his nerves weren’t helping his banter: “I keep telling dumb jokes up top and undercutting myself.” He got flushed even recalling it. “Why am I doing this?” he asked earnestly.


“In order to hide your vulnerability,” I responded. The word gets thrown around so much that it’s easy to lose sight of its real meaning. Scroll Instagram and “vulnerable” can seem like a shorthand for “Let them see you cry.” I’m not opposed to crying in front of people, but that’s not really the point. Vulnerability is the holes in our armor, which we can either scramble to patch up or daringly display—as if to say, 
This is where you can hurt me, because I’m a real human underneath. I’m soft. I have feelings.
 Alain de Botton has said, “What builds connection is an acquaintance with one’s own vulnerability and the recognition of the other’s vulnerability.”
5
 Vulnerability is showing that heart we’re so used to hiding.


And the surest way to do so is to show people that you care. Caring, out loud, is wildly vulnerable and wildly brave. The kind of bravery that makes listeners perk up and be braver themselves. This is what Juliana and Chris needed to practice. 
This is the opposite of neutral. This is the opposite of deflecting with jokes. This is dangerous and thrilling. When you stake a claim on what matters to you, you show the holes in your armor and you turn a regular ol’ meet-and-greet into trust and connection and love; it’s like alchemy.


I once heard a casting director say to a roomful of would-be TV hosts learning to read a teleprompter naturalistically, “When you fuck up, the audience falls in love with you.” She didn’t mean the fuckup itself held any power; she meant that seeing you grapple honestly with a misstep, because you care enough to get it right, is incredibly endearing. No matter what context, when you show you care, with your true emotions, you’re saying, 
This—here!—is the exact shape and size of the opening in my armor.
 You are saying, 
I am exposing myself.
 You are saying, 
You can hurt me, and it will cost me something.


And your boss, your audience, the media may indeed hurt you, and life is sometimes bone-crushingly unfair. In some rooms, vulnerability isn’t worth it. But if you insist on displaying your soft spots in the rooms that really do matter, despite the risk, your audience at least has the 
chance
 to fall in love with you. You will offer them the opportunity to be emotionally aroused by you, and you by them, and to dance together. Neither Juliana nor Chris were allowing themselves to be vulnerable enough to make an actual connection with the people in the room—but they both clearly wanted to. They cared about caring. They just needed practice.


I once coached a scientist—a lanky older man who taught civil engineering at an East Coast college and wore a blue flannel button-down and a furrowed brow. He ended up telling me the story behind a ring he wears on his pinky that holds great meaning for him and that he touches often because it 
reminds him of his calling. He got misty when he talked about this ring, and his brow relaxed. It was as though he unfolded himself, and I truly saw him. But when I suggested he might start his introductory lecture to first-year undergraduates by telling his students the same story, he folded back up. “I would lose all credibility.” His voice sounded like someone else’s, and it made clear he wasn’t willing to believe that vulnerability is a strength, not a weakness. He talked himself out of trying.


It is, after all, easy to make the case for playing it safe. As Anne Kreamer, a journalist and author of 
It’s Always Personal,
 says, “In the binary shorthand we use to compartmentalize modern life, we think of home as the realm of emotion and work as the place where rationality rules.”
6
 As you consider how you sound when you speak in public, and how you 
want
 to sound, does this dichotomy loom large? Research from the 
Harvard Business Review
 references widespread evidence of work environments rife with “masculinity contest culture”
7
 where “winners demonstrate stereotypically masculine traits such as emotional toughness, physical stamina, and ruthlessness.” Although speaking in public isn’t always work-related, this manly ideal of emotion-free workspaces has a wily way of influencing our public selves. Work, after all, is where many of us get our first taste of the rewards and punishments associated with speaking up. It’s where we try on and settle into what the professional, adult version of us sounds like. Even though, as Kreamer points out, this framework where we leave our emotions at home is “a tidy distinction that crumbles in the face of experience.”


My client Vanessa has perfected her “pleasantly nonresponsive face” at work, which she says keeps her safe from unruly emotions that would be a problem if revealed. But on the flip 
side, she can’t stand her job and knows she doesn’t contribute there in a way that adds meaning to her life. So she’s currently looking for an escape. Maybe, she told me, gathering herself, she’ll start her own business and build a new work culture on her own terms, one that encourages whole personhood from her employees. She isn’t sure it’s possible—but then, she’s a little beaten down at the moment.


It’s exhausting to pretend we don’t have emotions, like we’re still trying to prove our uteruses aren’t roaming around releasing unseemly bile. An emotion is actually, technically, an automatic physiological response associated with the nervous system. It’s 
automatic
. It happens 
to
 us—and it happens to 
all
 of us. Dutch primatologist Frans de Waal has a rare perspective, since he’s spent decades among chimps and bonobos, and he famously said, “I cannot name any emotion that is uniquely human.”
8
 We share the experience of these biological hits with humans everywhere and even with animals; they’re that universal.


But bonobos aren’t compelled to suit up and pore over the capitalism playbook on their way to work. And they don’t teach themselves to pathologize emotions. There’s a term for the current incarnation of dutiful repression we humans adhere to in professional settings: “display rules.” These refer to the choices we each make to cover or minimize the emotions we’re feeling that seem out of sync with the cultural norms of whatever context we’re in.


The rules are unwritten, and, I don’t need to tell you, they serve some of us more than others. See: the media’s response to Brett Kavanaugh’s anger on the stand versus Serena Williams’s on the tennis court. Never mind that he was defending himself against accusations of a crime and she was doing her actual job; his anger was treated as legitimate and correct and 
hers was not. We read the news and watch the replay. We get the memo.


As Yale professor Dr. Marc Brackett put it in his book 
Permission to Feel,
 “the person with more power has greater latitude in expressing emotions. Parents get away with displays they’d never tolerate in their children. In the classroom, the teacher is the boss, and the children are the bossed, a dynamic that carries over in the workplace, whether we want it to or not.”
9
 And it’s clear that often the power dynamic isn’t related to formal relationships at all; it’s a set of social biases reflecting who’s historically been allowed to show the full range of human emotions without penalty and who hasn’t. As self-proclaimed disrupter Jodi-Ann Burey put it in her TED Talk “The Myth of Bringing Your Full, Authentic Self to Work”: “A distorted elaboration of white cultural norms and the standards that meet the comforts of those who hold social and institutional power—that’s 
professionalism
.”
10


Nanci Luna Jiménez founded a thirty-year-old institute in the Pacific Northwest that offers anti-racism workshops for people and their organizations, and she asks, “Who gets to be human in a society that upholds white supremacy and patriarchy and domination and capitalism?”
11
 Who gets to be fully human is quite a question. And, since we’re thinking about our voices: Who gets to sound fully human? And who decides? If we’re changing what power sounds like, this must change too.


Nanci’s groundbreaking work focuses on undoing what she calls “adultism”—display rules we feel pressured to follow even before we enter the workforce. As she describes it, most of us were taught as children to hold back, downplay, and dissociate from our outsized emotions and to play at being adults too soon. “We were mocked for our idealism; we were mocked 
for our sheer joy,” she tells me when I asked her why she thinks we’ve got such a complicated relationship with our emotions.


As she sees it, it’s a cycle repeated generation to generation: As little kids, we’re wild and free and deeply attuned to what is and isn’t fair and we loudly express how we feel about that—until we learn that these displays aren’t socially acceptable. We hear 
shh, sit down, sit still, don’t cry, shake it off, relax, you’re fine, you’re overreacting, you need to calm down
. And we conform. And we grow up. Maybe we have kids of our own. And then, just as our parents did before us, “in an attempt to make life ‘easier’ for our children, we pass on the hurt that we didn’t heal from,” she says. “We think, if our children show up already contained, their life will go better. We think we’re protecting our young people from feeling disappointment. So, we keep projecting the guardrails that have limited our life and capacity to care and feel the range of human emotions. There’s no parent who will ever say, ‘Yeah, I became a parent to crush my child.’ But oppressive society forces us into that role.” She adds, “We become that adult that we thought our parents wanted us to be, but it was really who they thought we had to be to survive.”


“Adultism” is a word that captures the systematic stunting of our emotional development, so we no longer feel as free as we did as children. To me, it’s also a nod to the creeping suspicion I know I’ve certainly felt that being an adult or “adulting” is just a construct: it’s what we do to make money and get by. There’s a reason, as Nanci points out, that “young people will lead every major social movement from now until the end of time. Because they’re not connected into the economic system yet. It’s exactly why they’re devalued, and exactly why they know what doesn’t work. They’re not confused.”


How much do we tone-police our children because their behavior is truly disrespectful, and how much because we’re uncomfortable with their big emotions, especially around issues of fairness? I check myself every day to make sure I’m not perpetuating adultism in my kid. But it’s worth asking how much we tone-police ourselves as well, because perhaps we’re uncomfortable with 
our
 big emotions. Perhaps we’re feeling big emotions because something is not fair, but we try to convince ourselves we’re being ridiculous. 
You’re fine. You’re overreacting. You need to calm down.


But how we speak to ourselves about our emotions matters. We use the words “emotions” and “feelings” somewhat interchangeably in everyday life, but the distinction between them is extremely useful here: 
emotions
 happen in our body and they’re universal. 
Feelings,
 on the other hand, are defined by experts like Dr. Brackett as something else—not the chemical hits in our body, but rather our mind’s 
interpretation
 of those hits. Feelings are stories. Feelings are the narratives we tell ourselves or have been told 
about
 those emotions.


People can read our emotions, but they cannot read our feelings because our feelings are our inner dialogue. They’re where what we 
say
 we value in the abstract bumps against what we 
actually
 value when the shit hits the fan. And these conversations with ourselves are inevitably culture-specific and intensely personal. How we interpret what’s happening when we get emotional is subject to all kinds of biases we’ve picked up, and all kinds of stories our parents or our first boyfriend or our boss told us. Like “Real men don’t cry,” or “No one likes a woman who’s too much,” or “Crying at work is unprofessional,” or “Your anger is ugly,” or “Caring is uncool.”


Those whispers in our head check us just when we might 
break free. I think of writer-director Emerald Fennell accepting the Academy Award for 
Promising Young Woman
 and spending a sizable chunk of her time at the microphone talking about trying not to cry, rather than just crying and moving along. Or my friend Sandra, who courageously spoke to a crowd about the abuse she suffered as a child—but when she felt familiar emotions begin to bubble up, she reprimanded herself. “Stop it,” she told herself, loudly enough that we could all hear it. “Stop being such a baby.” I think of Soraya Chemaly’s book 
Rage Becomes Her,
 brimming with data about how women around the world compromise their health by suppressing their anger, and how their mothers did, and their mothers.
12
 I’ve heard it from clients and in workshops I’ve run again and again. We school ourselves to act emotionally neutral even if some deeper, wiser part of us knows better. We repeat these old clichés and they wreak havoc on our sense of permission, shutting us down when our emotions seek to burst forth. Yes, it’s self-defense, but it’s also self-silencing and self-sabotage, and that is a bummer of the highest order.


But this understanding of “feelings” offers hope. It means that how we talk to ourselves matters. And that the tiny ways we categorize our emotions as they arise all day every day add up. That which whispers we adhere to and which we reject can change the course of our life. It means that we’re not just reacting to the codes of our culture but continually perpetuating and creating that culture. We are taking the hits of millennia of messaging, but 
we are also the message
. We have within us the power to check our biases and reconsider how we respect our emotions and those of everybody we know. And we must, together.


We can build the world we’d rather live in—we’re already 
doing it, just by examining these old stories and who they serve. We can decide collectively that, as Elizabeth Lesser writes, it’s “cool to be talkative, brave to cry, noble to feel and relate.” Because the alternative keeps those of us who don’t look and sound like Brett Kavanaugh in a fraught relationship with our own voice. When we deliberately suppress our emotional connection to what we’re saying, we limit our pitch, tone, and expressivity. We hide. And inevitably, we limit how much feeling we arouse in our listener too. If that’s the goal, great. If your boss is like Petra’s and the times demand it, repress away. But I’m interested in the other times; I’m interested in you becoming the most powerful and potent version of yourself.


The world’s greatest minds have figured out how to get us to the moon, and still, no one has been able to build a larynx from scratch. This beautiful, versatile apparatus of ours has the potential to create sound waves that emerge from us carrying new ideas full of rich emotional content directly into the ears of our fellow humans, so they see us and get us. But that can’t happen if we keep believing the old story that emotions make us weak.


There’s another story, and I bet this one will sound equally familiar, even though it entirely contradicts the earlier one. This story isn’t about labeling big feelings bad. It’s the opposite. This one is that 
we love passion
. All of us. International management consulting firms McKinsey and Bain Capital even love passion—it’s one of the first words they use to describe the type of ideal employee they seek to hire, according to organizational behavioralist Dr. Lizzie Wolf, who runs studies on emotion in the workplace. And no matter how skeptical we 
might be of seeing that listed in a corporate job description, it’s hard to deny why companies might say it: we love being around passion too.


Think of who you look forward to spending time with. Is it the friend who succeeds at coming across as endlessly cool or the one who’s bursting with excitement? We might admire or envy people for whom things seem easy, who glide through life all breezy and blasé. But we don’t necessarily trust them. Or have fun with them. This game—the “who can hide their humanity the best” game—is not fun. For those of us who try to pull it off, it’s hell on our nervous system, it’s hell on our sense of joy, and it’s hell on our ability to speak publicly in a voice that 
works
.


Think about some memorable recent acts of speaking up in public. Former Australian prime minister Julia Gillard’s famous misogyny speech comes to mind. Or 
Fosse/Verdon
 actor Michelle Williams choosing to use her 2020 Golden Globe win to publicly recognize how crucial access to birth control was to her success. Ukrainian president Volodymyr Zelenskyy’s vulnerable addresses via cellphone as war broke out. Or Black Lives Matter activist Tamika Mallory reminding the American public who looted whom during the summer of protests in the wake of George Floyd’s murder. Cool, composed speeches are out, gone the way of stiff, early-twentieth-century acting. Neutral delivery devoid of personal touch once seemed like the epitome of authoritative speech, but it now seems suspect. We in the audience yearn for something that rings true and feels urgent. We lean forward when people speak with feeling, when they reveal that they are determined, angry, heartbroken, joyful, alive. We send around video clips when we catch a glimpse of it, turning moments like those above viral. Cool, 
composed speakers don’t go viral. Boring speeches don’t go viral. We want to feel the blood pumping when our politicians and CEOs and heroes speak. We want to believe their words, and we call bullshit when we don’t. We want to hear what caring sounds like.


In this version of the story, emotions aren’t bad; they’re a sign. They’re a breathless messenger from Marathon, relaying big news. The word “emotion” comes from the Latin 
exmovére,
 “to move.” They move through us and move along once their message is delivered, if we let them do so unimpeded. And they move others too. If we’re the one speaking, emotions are a sign that we care—maybe even more than we realized. If we’re the one in the audience witnessing someone speak with emotion, the big feelings are a sign that something 
real
 is happening.


In this version of the story, emotions aren’t bad; they serve a 
purpose
. In 
Permission to Feel,
 Dr. Brackett goes to great lengths to “clear up a misunderstanding that may have been building in your mind: that permission means license to let it all hang out, to whine, yell, act on every emotional impulse, and behave as though we have no control over what we feel, so we should just go for it and freak out.”
13
 That’s not what either of us is advocating. Vulnerability vomits are fine if you just need to vent; go for it. But when we’ve got a platform, venting is rarely the intention. What is? No matter the form of public speaking you envision, there is likely a call to action—to convince your audience to move forward or change course or donate or hire you or give themselves permission. What I’m advocating is that you recruit your emotions in calling your audience to action.


This is being emotional with purpose. This is 
using
 your 
emotions, not simply having them. In fact, as a quick fix Dr. Lizzie Wolf suggests reframing even inconvenient emotional outbursts at work as “passion.”
14
 She charts how that word is often used in association with men’s emotions and is well received because it implies agency, harnessing their emotional hits for good. It suggests being in control rather than out of control, using emotions rather than just having them. She has run experiments where women at work refer to their own emotions as passion, and the results are astounding. These women are promoted, they’re picked to be on the team, they’re given professional legitimacy.


It’s a great trick to flip the script for the sake of your less-evolved colleagues, but what I’m interested in is you flipping it for yourself. I recall a rally I attended in 2018, when news first hit about the family separation crisis at the U.S.–Mexico border. Former NARAL Pro Choice America president Ilyse Hogue spoke at a gathering of hundreds at a public park, under the shade of a willow tree. But partway through her speech, her young son started sobbing and ran to the stage with his arms up. She picked him up and got teary herself as he wailed. And rather than pretend she could fight her emotions and stick to her talking points, she acknowledged the moment. “I can hold my child in my arms,” she said, her voice rising in anguish. “I can hear his screams and do something about it—the parents held at the border cannot.” We were all wrecks. Her emotions weren’t distracting; they helped her message. She was being emotional with purpose.


You might find that if you trust you can use your emotions—whatever they are—to help your message, the internal drama around them will dissipate. If, for example, you sense the beginnings of a stress response because you can tell you’re about 
to cry, breathe and remember, the tears don’t distract from the message; they are a valuable part of the message.


In acting school, when a classmate would finish a spectacular performance with tears on their cheeks, our teacher was fond of turning to us in the class and saying about the actor, “They’re not good because they’re crying; they’re crying because they’re good.” He was right: as we roared with applause, we weren’t responding to the tears like they were a clever parlor trick, we were responding to the reverberation of truth in the performance, 
and the performer was too
