. Whether it’s playing a role or playing yourself, the tears aren’t the point; they’re the by-product. They have no intrinsic value, but they signal that you’re talking about something that matters, and that’s of immense value. Tears—or enthusiasm or anger or any noticeable emotion—are just what happens when we let our communication really, truly matter.


“Authenticity” is another one of those wily words that’s so overused, its meaning has become murky. Sure, we all want to be authentic—but how? Here’s my practical answer: It doesn’t just mean “be yourself,” whoever the heck that is anyway and however it aligns with workplace norms. It means reveal what matters to you 
like it matters to you
. Talk about what you care about like you care about it. If you can think of anyone whose voice you love, one of those examples above or someone from your own community who speaks up effectively, I can pretty much guarantee they are doing this.


I want to help you acquire this skill and access it more consistently so you stop attempting to, as Anne Kreamer puts it, “check our human side at the door.”
15
 We must move ourselves before moving others, and we must move others to get what 
we want—to get what we all want. Let’s start with three solid reasons to reclaim your emotions and use them when you’re speaking in public, despite messages to the contrary. Turn to these when your mind begins to rebel and you begin—like that sweet, infuriating scientist—to talk yourself out of trying.


One, when we try to uphold the separation of emotion and work, we’re each left with a false sense that 
we’re
 the one who’s doing it wrong. As far as I’m concerned this is an insidious tragedy of global proportions. I’ve seen it in my clients’ eyes; the 
why can’t I hack it?
 pain. We beat ourselves up for seeming human when we can’t contain ourselves. We feel alone and broken. Men and women both report feelings of shame after crying at work, and yet according to a widely cited 2018 survey conducted by Accountemps, almost 50 percent of people say they’ve shed a tear at work.
16
 Add bouts of anger, frustration, elation, and hurt into the mix and of course we’re all guilty of having emotions at our place of employ. We are wasting precious energy fighting them. Working while imperturbable is a test we’re doomed to fail, and yet we keep trying to nail the test out of habit. Would you report feelings of shame if you were surveyed? Shame and permission are pretty much opposites. And shame keeps all of us out of power.


Two, if we check our “human side” at the door we will be boring, joyless public speakers. Or panelists, presenters, meeting participants, whatever. And we will continue to feel crappy in the moments that 
could
 be the defining events of a self-actualized life. We will miss the chance to feel alive and let our audience feel alive too. We will miss the chance to have 
duende,
 which is a lush, classic Spanish concept that I bet we’ve each felt when watching an incredible speaker: it’s a mysterious force, a realness that makes the room hum, a presence that 
cannot be denied. Spanish poet Federico García Lorca famously gave a speech on 
duende
 in which he drew from examples of breathtaking performances he’d seen, dancers and musicians, as well as poets and speakers, remembering, “I heard an old maestro of the guitar say: ‘The 
duende
 is not in the throat: the 
duende
 surges up, inside, from the soles of the feet.’”
17
 This 
duende
 might be what we call bringin’ it. It’s the opposite of boring and joyless—and it’s available to each of us. But it does require that we let ourselves feel the music in our soul, in front of others.


And three, the science is on the side of emotion. Take this 2017 article in 
Frontiers in Psychology,
 focusing on the rise of online learning. It includes a synthesis of recent findings, stating, “Numerous studies have reported that human cognitive processes are affected by emotions, including attention, learning and memory, reasoning, and problem-solving. These factors are critical in educational domains because when students face such difficulties, it defeats the purpose of schooling and can potentially render it meaningless.”
18
 
Defeats the purpose. Render it meaningless.
 Is this how we want our audiences to feel? This article references 176 different academic papers that explore just how much the brain lights up when we’re presented with facts laced with feelings, offering examples like “activation of the amygdala during the encoding of emotionally arousing information (both pleasant/unpleasant) has been reported that correlates with subsequent recall.” In other words, without an emotional component, no one will remember what you said.


Here are tools to help you more readily tap into your emotional life and give yourself permission to express it—not just 
for the sake of your emotions (though it does feel fantastic to give up on fearing them) but for the sake of your message and your audience. Do the work because it will make you feel less alone, it will make you feel alive, and your message will stick.


The best way to get in touch with your emotions is to get in touch with your body. Either move it (as my favorite mug says, “When in doubt, dance it out”) or center it. The main difference when I work with actors and with nonactors is that a big portion of the session with nonactors is spent getting them loose enough in body and mind that they can surprise themselves and grow and change. Actors just have more practice. You might find it hard to give yourself permission to play, to act like a weirdo and not judge yourself for it, to give up trying to control everything, to be nothing but heart. It’s hard for the entirely understandable reasons discussed in this chapter. And yet, the good stuff happens on the other side.


Here’s a start: Lori Snyder, author and founder of the Writers Happiness Movement, contributed the guided meditation below. Try recording it yourself on your phone (more slowly than you think, taking a few extra breaths especially when you see the // symbol) and then find a comfortable position seated or lying down, press Play and close your eyes. If you’re new to meditation or resisting it, this is for you.




(BEGIN RECORDING HERE)


Take the first few moments to settle in, to arrive.
19
 // Next, take a long, deep inhale, full and round. Then open the 
mouth and sigh it out. // Soften the shoulders. // Smooth out the forehead. // Relax the jaw.


Now, draw awareness to the truest part of you, the part that is most 
you
. This is the part that exists separate from the labels you use to describe yourself or that others have given you, separate from any roles you play in your life, separate from any job titles. // It’s the part where all art and love and generosity come from, and it’s what we access when we say whatever it is we need to say in the way we mean to say it. // This part of ourselves is often locked away, buried beneath layers of protection. So if it’s not making itself known right now, that’s okay. It’s still there, even if you don’t feel it.


If you can feel that truest part of you, breathe into it. Let it grow, like you’re fanning the flames with your breath. // If you can’t feel it today, use your imagination as to how it 
might
 feel to connect with that part of yourself. Breathe into that. Notice how it feels in the body.


See if you can be here a few minutes. // When you’re ready, take one more deep breath.


And then, right here in this moment—without having to change anything else about the rest of your life—take off the armor. Set it off to the side.


Take off the mask. Set 
that
 off to the side. //


Now take off the mask behind the mask. Set it off to the side too. //


And lastly, lay down any shield you have been carrying. And see if you can just be here: Undefended. Unabashed. Unprotected.


When we set down the shield, take off the armor, remove the masks—we speak only truth.


And when we speak only truth,


we become exactly what the world needs. //


When you are ready to finish,


please take your deepest breath in and out. And then move slowly into the rest of your day,


Leaving the armor


and the masks


and the shield


on the ground behind you.




From this place, once you’ve opened your eyes and let them adjust, envision speaking to a group of people that perhaps you’re actually going to speak to soon, or that you present to regularly. Breathe as you look around the room in your mind’s eye. Feel what it feels like to be armorless. Say, “Hi.” Say, “I’m glad I get to talk about something I care about.” Notice if you tighten in your belly or chest or throat and get curious about it. Feel your feelings. Send some extra love to those areas of your body that yearn to clamp back down and tell yourself you’re safe.


As you leave the house, join a meeting, deal with your boss, notice when you put your armor back on and ask yourself if you need to. Sometimes we do. But sometimes it’s just a habit, and the holes in the armor are always more interesting than the armor itself.


Here’s another tool: At this point, it’ll come as no surprise that although our throats are meant to be a relatively passive passage for our breath and our sound … it doesn’t always work out that way. General anxiety or the specific fear that our emotions will get us in trouble cause most of us to tighten the muscles, so emotions won’t bubble up. Those throat muscles are fantastic at stifling us so we don’t come across as 
too much
—and habitually overusing them undermines our attempts to figure out what 
just right
 might be. We’ve all witnessed panels or presentations where the speaker on the dais barely resembles a living, breathing human being. It’s possible to show up with a voice that’s so constricted by throat tension, and a mindset so governed by the sense that caring is uncool, that we don’t really show up at all.


We’re stopping that routine right here and now.


Try this trick for relaxing the throat and see what it loosens up: place the tip of your thumb behind your top front teeth and pull hard while taking in a deep belly breath. You’ll feel the whole back of your neck and the spot between your shoulder blades engage and then release when you stop pulling. This movement puts your entire skull back into alignment with your spine. Actually, speech pathologists say it puts your entire 
body
 back into alignment. They call it “anchoring.” And I’d like to point out, it also has the benefit of looking quite subtle; I definitely pull it off at parties mid-conversation, if I can tell 
my throat muscles are working overtime to get heard over the din. Try it now, perhaps with your eyes closed, and see if you can feel that passive passageway open up.


Here’s another one: in a quiet space away from others, take a good breath, open your mouth, and slowly make the sound of a gate creaking open (also known as vocal fry). Take a breath and try again. It might require some experimentation to figure out just how little energy you need to use to get the cords to make this sound. Go for three slow breaths out on this creak, sounding sooooo chill, and then close your mouth and hum from high to low a few times before speaking again. This exercise loosens your cords and resets them if they’ve gotten tense either from speaking in a way that’s hard on them (more in the next chapter on what I mean) or from using them to curb your emotions.


Here are some suggestions of a few new lines to whisper to yourself, to add to your inner dialogue and swap out old, stale ones. First, think of something you talk about a lot. Maybe it’s welcoming everyone into a space, or maybe it’s more specific: a line from a pitch or the way you often describe what you do. Maybe it’s why you started your company or why you 
want
 to. Maybe it’s a call to action, asking your audience to sign up for your newsletter or follow you on social media.


Alone in a room, practice saying it with this one shift: loop it through the heart. Imagine that the words don’t just come up through your throat and out your mouth, but that they detour through your heart before they emerge. And that they come out with some residue from your heart—whatever that means to you. How does that feel? Imagine someone in front of you and say it to them. After experimenting with this a bit on your own, I dare you to try this with people you’re comfort
able with, and then with those you’re not. No one needs to know, but you will.


As famed voice teacher Kristin Linklater said, “Words have become largely utilitarian in everyday life and are conditioned to run from the speech cortex straight to the mouth. They seldom pick up an emotional charge except under extreme provocation,”
20
 like rage, grief, or joy. But we don’t need such extreme provocation to avoid the pitfalls of utilitarian speech. We can activate our words simply by remembering to bring our hearts along in our communication.


This “loop it through the heart” reminder is a way to practice saying something that matters 
like it matters,
 as well as like it doesn’t. Indeed, the inverse is useful to explore too: again, alone in a room, try saying “I love what I do because ___” and let it cost you absolutely nothing. Get familiar with the ways that you signal you’re excited about something and that you’re not. If you work in the service industry—or have a long history as a people-pleaser—notice, too, how you fake it. This is your chance to start matching your insides to your outsides.


An acting teacher I recommend to everyone in Los Angeles named Jen Krater first introduced me to the idea of looping it through the heart over a decade ago, and I’ve found it applicable to nearly every scenario imaginable for both my own life and my clients’. It might make you feel things, but I encourage you to stay there, unapologetically. Don’t forget you’re practicing what it is to believe that feeling things is good.


And here’s an extra gem from Jen: often when an actor would be performing in front of her class and they’d reach the part in the script where they were gearing up to ask a big question or deliver big news, Jen would gently whisper from the 
sidelines, “Get in trouble.” And inevitably the actor would transform in front of our eyes. The moment would crackle.


“Get in trouble” is the nudge to be just a bit more daring, to take the gamble, to reveal your heart. I offer this to you. When you feel a scary moment coming up—the big ask, the big call to action, the chance to shrink or expand to match the moment—gently whisper to yourself, “Get in trouble.” If you feel yourself bailing, “get in trouble.” If you’re wasting precious energy worrying that you’ll get in trouble, “get in trouble.” Just a little.


Maybe this private exchange with yourself results in a twinkle in your eye you didn’t have a moment ago. Maybe it means you surprise yourself and speak with more vulnerability, more 
duende,
 more joy. Trust that the gamble will pay off. And then trust it some more. By the way, I think this is probably the original intention of that old cliché about picturing the audience naked: when you’ve got a little mischievous secret, it can distract your brain into accidentally having fun.


Here’s another simple visualization. Mouths are what we most associate with speech. But imagine now that your mouth actually only accounts for one of three sources of your vocal energy. Imagine that your torso is a massive trident or pitchfork, and that a second, equally mighty prong juts out of your solar plexus and a third out of your gut. Imagine your vocal energy coming out of all three spots simultaneously. Try talking like 
that
.


Some say, open your mouth and your heart at the same time; I say, what if you bring your gut, and all its juicy emotional content, with you too? Your belly is “all heartache and 
ambition. Fires and tidal waves,”
21
 says poet Kate Baer. Give us that.


I’ve found when I introduce this trident image to clients, they stand taller. Inevitably you’ll bring more of yourself along when you include your heart and gut wisdom, but also, on a literal level it helps keep your spine strong—rather than allowing your shoulders to hunch or your chin to angle out. And when your spine is strong, your body is in alignment and you’re more likely to say what you mean and mean what you say. (Bonus: You can visualize this as the Care Bear Stare instead of a trident, except with three rainbow vectors. If you’re an ’80s baby, I’ve got you.)


And finally, try a mindset visualization. Dr. Brackett pointed out that it’s usually the person with more power in a scenario who’s allowed more latitude to show emotion—and you’ve probably seen this play out. But there’s also an intriguing way you can gain more power pretty instantaneously. Look, there’s no substitute for “social power,” as Dr. Amy Cuddy calls it in her book 
Presence,
 the kind of power we might gain by having “access to assets that others need—food, shelter, money, tools, information, status, attention, affection.”
1
 There’s no substitute for being the most dominant person in the room, or accidentally being born into a privileged position. But there are other kinds of power we can access from where we are. Dr. Cuddy calls this second category “personal power.” And it’s more about our inner resources than our outer ones; it’s about our hard-earned skills, our values, what we know and love about ourselves, and what makes us bold. If social power is power 
over,
 this is power 
to
. As Dr. Cuddy points out, “In an ideal world, our feeling of personal power would be unassailable. In 
reality, it’s apt to fluctuate, especially when the world plays rough with us.”
23


But you can 
generate
 personal power just by performing a quick thought experiment. You can prime yourself for power by thinking of previous occasions when you felt great. I use this trick all the time, and it’s kind of magic: it immediately makes me feel more free, more expansive, and more confident that I can be emotionally available without penalty.


Recall a moment you felt personal power, a moment when you were valued or celebrated or seen. Maybe it was a time when you had agency and audacity and swagger and caught yourself acting like the biggest and best version of you. Maybe it was at home, or with friends, during a high-stakes work event, or even a regular meeting where you suddenly shone. Write a few of these down, and as you think back on them, let yourself remember with your whole body.


According to social psychologists, by merely reflecting on a moment of personal power you will change your whole psychological state. Bring a good power memory to mind and you’ll be primed for the next one—and this is a huge deal because study upon study has shown that when we feel powerless, our thoughts are impaired in all kinds of ways, our ability to perform even simple tasks diminishes considerably, and we become self-absorbed and likely to ruminate later. But when we feel powerful, the opposite happens. We can nudge ourselves in the right direction with just a little recall.


As with everything, context matters. And who each of us is and what each of us wants out of an opportunity matter too. There is no one-size-fits-all approach to bringing more emotion into your professional life. Some rooms don’t want your authentic 
self, and that puts you, like it put Petra, in a difficult position. In her case, she left. After soul-searching (a process that included getting certified as a life coach and taking at least one trip to Burning Man), she abandoned the finance world altogether and started her own life coaching business—and now, years on, she’s merged her earlier passion for the markets with her interest in living an aligned life, and she coaches clients on ethical investing. She was unwilling to hide that she cared any longer and quit trying to live by that boss’s creed. Now she cares for a living.


As visionary business leader and Columbia Business School professor Hitendra Wadhwa put it, “Today, the business world is on fire. Corporate leaders are witnessing a growing hunger, from inside their organizations and outside, for the workplace to reflect the right values—values such as inclusion, empathy, autonomy and service to humanity.”
24
 You are not alone. And no matter the room, you can always interrogate, feel, find, and redraw the line between responsible and radical for yourself. You can always ask yourself, How armorless can I be? How much more emotionally honest can I be without putting myself in a position that feels unsafe? Perhaps you can reveal that your frustration has turned to anger, or that your disappointment is really grief. Perhaps you can reveal yourself, or at least begin.


As Ilyse Hogue put it on my podcast, the powers that be who are fighting for the status quo and resisting change reinforce a style of speaking publicly, and showing up publicly, where they repress their emotional needs. I think of former vice president Mike Pence during the 2020 vice presidential debate with Kamala Harris—where the fly stole the show simply by being realer than the head it landed on. The powers that 
be have codified the “ideal worker” and defined the word “professional,” ensuring that both describe someone who’s more machine than human. As bell hooks wrote, “The first act of violence that patriarchy demands of males is not violence toward women. Instead, patriarchy demands of all males that they engage in acts of psychic self-mutilation, that they kill off the emotional parts of themselves. If an individual is not successful in emotionally crippling himself, he can count on patriarchal men to enact rituals of power that will assault his self-esteem.”
25
 I think of the women who first trickled into male-dominated industries, desperate to be taken seriously, looking around to glean the rules of play in lieu of adequate mentorship—and crippling themselves to fit in. I think of them mentoring the next generation of women, reinforcing what worked for them, enshrining their hobble.


Former Fox News political commentator Megyn Kelly took to Twitter after that vice presidential debate to mock the freedom with which Kamala Harris displayed her emotional responses to Mike Pence’s interruptions and false statements. Kelly chided her, “Take it like a woman. Don’t make faces.”
26
 Madeleine Albright, the first female U.S. secretary of state, wrote in her autobiography about her own experience learning to take it like a woman, as it were. “Many of my colleagues made me feel that I was overly emotional,”
27
 she wrote, “and I worked hard to get over that. In time, I learned to keep my voice flat and unemotional when I talked about issues I considered important.” Secretary Albright’s quote sounds like success. It captures some prevailing conventional wisdom: flat is better than topographical variety. Unemotional is better than emotional. A steady voice gets the job done.


But Ilyse went on to say, “Our ability to tap deep into our emotions to channel a different future—that is our superpower. We should always use it.”
28
 From this perspective, we can reconsider Secretary Albright’s quote. For one thing, she was describing a transformation she enacted thirty years ago. For another, her colleagues were by default men and her job was international diplomacy, for which a poker face and a poker voice might be a career-specific boon. To hear her quote out of context, it’s all too easy to take the wrong lesson, but her wisdom may not be wise for you.


As climate journalist and Twitter rabble-rouser David Roberts wrote, responding to Megyn Kelly’s tweet, “Embracing your full humanity, in public, takes way more gonads than being yet another third-rate John Wayne rip-off, unable to identify, process, or articulate your emotions. It’s a kind of strength & courage that emerge out of, rather than deny, being a whole-ass person.”
29
 Indeed. I’m glad Secretary Albright found an approach that worked for her. But I don’t want to live in a world where it’s our best bet.


Fortunately, I don’t think we do. But it’s up to us to reclaim our emotions and allow our voices their full range of expression. Because—and maybe you already knew this all along, deep down—when the stakes are high, when you’re presenting your work or you’re pitching what you believe in, you must bring your heart.


And when you’ve got any social power at all, you must be vigilant in refusing to reinforce the old stoicism story so that others can practice bringing their hearts too. Every chance you get to make choices about who to work with and how, intentionally nurture spaces where enthusiasm can flourish and where people are free to speak emotionally without penalty. 
It’s your chance to respect your emotions and those of everybody you know and 
then
 redefine what “professional legitimacy” looks and sounds like from there. Redefine who gets to be fully human and who gets to sound like it. Toni Morrison once said, “As you enter positions of trust and power, dream a little before you think.”
30
 Put down your shield and your armor and dream.

















4


Pitch




and there was a new voice
 which you slowly recognized as your own,
 that kept you company


—MARY OLIVER
, “The Journey”
1




Maybe, when you’re uncomfortable, you go monotone. Maybe a voice inside your head warns, “Stay steady,” and you press on your vocal cords to make sure nothing extra comes out. I heard myself do it when I first began recording my podcast. I was sitting in an oversized swivel chair at a corporate conference table, speaking into a very expensive microphone at the iHeartRadio offices, opposite a guest who intimidated the hell out of me. I swallowed and attempted to ignore my wild heartbeat. I spoke and my voice came out steady—and totally foreign.


In theory, I knew better than to try to hide behind some fake “podcast voice.” And yet, on my maiden voyage at the mic, it’s as though a little generic monster in my head piped up to try to save me from myself. 
Hello, Samara, I’ve come to inform you that a voice devoid of character would be best here. No one wants the real you.
 And right away my defense mechanisms busted in to protect me, pushing down on my cords, constrict
ing my full range of expression, and rendering me a whole lot more generic. The producers couldn’t tell, but I sure could.


Vocal hiding and vocal freedom hinge on whether we feel safe, which is usually about whether we feel we have something to prove. That feeling—that others are judging us, doubting us—can suck the joy out of our pursuits and the character out of our voice. Clearly, I felt like I had something to prove, and hadn’t properly prepared myself to give that notion a hug and send it on its way—and the generic monster with it. It was my first time “going public,” and you could hear it in my voice.


Think about when you’ve spoken in a room where you had the impression you were being evaluated, or where you explicitly were—a job interview, a pitch, a competitive anything. These are the times that we must commit the fiercest to bringing permission along for the ride; otherwise, those imagined (or real) squinting eyes and judgy ears sizing us up will restrict us from expressing ourselves with all the vocal dynamics we have at our disposal. Which is a shame, because fear-based communication almost never elicits the results we want. The impulse is understandable; it’s safer to hide. But it’s not fun and not sustainable and likely won’t get us the gig or the audience we want—an audience that falls in love with us because we have shown them the truth of who we are and how we sound. We must save ourselves from this impulse to save us from ourselves.


How, you ask? The answer is pitch. Pitch is destiny when we speak. The inflections up and down the scale that this gorgeous vocal apparatus of ours has developed over thousands of years tell the whole story of whether we give in to the doubts. Simply put: when we’re on a roll, we broaden our pitch; when we get scared, we flatten it. That’s monotone. Our voice hides 
in our throat, and without the tools and bravery to let it out, we abandon the natural high and low notes we’d hit if we were hanging with our favorite people, the ones around whom we feel like we have nothing to prove.


The results are often disturbingly gendered; these are gross stereotypes, and if they don’t fit you, 
good
. And if you’re actively challenging the gender binary or exploring how to love your voice while in transition, I’ve got some resources in the notes section for this chapter.
2
 But here goes the crude version that’s all too common: For many women, when we’re uncomfortable, we tend toward “nice.” Vocally, this means we’ll speak at a higher pitch and limit how much range we use once we’re up there, as if to say, 
I’m so unintimidating, don’t worry about me.
 If you’ve ever been a server or relied on tips to make your rent, you’re probably quite familiar with this mode of speech. I call it the Starbucks Voice because it’s as though we’ve internalized the sound that service industry employees adopt in order to signal they have no needs of their own and exist only for yours. (“Hi, what can I get for you?” becomes “Hi, I think I might have an idea for a strategy if you guys want …?”) We are 
so
 nice.


Men, on the other hand, stereotypically tend toward “cool.” They go down in pitch when they’re uncomfortable and limit how much range they use as well, as if to say, 
Oh, hey, no worries, it’s all good, bro. I’m cool.
 I call it the Superhero Voice because it’s as though men have all gotten the memo that, like Batman, it’s important to be fazed by <Superhero Voice> 
nothing
. Obviously, it’s depressing as hell to consider that in stressful scenarios women sound like they’re trapped in the niceties of the customer service industry and men default to superheroes, but here we are.


As I lament this habit, I also appreciate its value, because as a means of getting what we want in certain situations, “nice” is the perfect lubricant. In fact, it’s an adaptive strategy found throughout the entire animal kingdom. Body language and the pitch of the voice play a critical role in animals’ performance of big and scary, or small and tender, although for them it’s below the level of conscious thought (perhaps for many of you as well, until now). Animals that are aggressively trying to repel a predator or compete for attention stand tall, raise the hairs on their body to appear bigger, and growl at their lowest pitch. It’s a size bluff—no matter how big they are, now they seem bigger. And likewise, if those same animals are romancing a partner or speaking to a baby, they’ll flatten their hairs (or their ears or their whole body) into a submissive stance and produce a high-pitched whine or coo to appear smaller and less intimidating. Animals play nice too.


But it turns out humans may well be the 
only
 animal that has the ability to voluntarily control our pitch patterns. We have this incredible prefrontal cortex that’s bigger and more complex than even other primates’, and it’s evolved to help us with executive functions like planning our cognitive and social behavior and expressing our personality. As Dr. Marc Dingman, the author of 
Your Brain, Explained,
 says, “The prefrontal cortex contributes a great deal to making us who we are as individuals.”
1
 Patients with brain damage in that region, he says, make clear that “if you took away the prefrontal cortex we would be ruled by our desires and impulses, lacking an ability to plan for the future or think about the consequences of our actions.” This brain region is also responsible for language and paralanguage, a term for all of our nonverbal communication, like pitch. And inside this region is a smaller area responsible 
for manipulating the voice box specifically, with the extravagant name bilateral dorsal laryngeal motor cortex. Pitch lives there.


According to Dr. Edward Chang, a neurosurgeon at the University of California–San Francisco whose team has run pioneering experiments on this bit of magnificent brain engineering, we differ from other primates in that we can manipulate our pitch in order to sing, to suggest intention, and to alter “perception”—in other words, to shape how others perceive us.
4


So yes, we can come across as nice and sometimes it’s our best bet to smooth over a hostile or uncomfortable situation. But this lubricant is a size bluff; it’s playing small. And I’m here to help us find alternatives. When we’ve got the chance to propose our big idea, speak of the work closest to our hearts, or offer a new plan, we can and must let 
nice
 morph into something that reflects that we don’t just exist for others. This is the voice, as Mary Oliver says, that we will slowly recognize as our own. And this voice will have fantastic pitch variety.


You know when we naturally exhibit pitch variety? When we’re talking to the kids in our lives. The exaggerated up and down intonation we use when speaking to a baby or a pet is universal across cultures and languages, according to Stanford linguist Dr. Anne Fernald.
5
 In fact, four-year-olds do it when they talk to two-year-olds! And it’s apt to come out of our mouths when we’re speaking to people who are still learning our language no matter what age they are, as well as older people we think may be hard of hearing. The implication is that this slowed-down, swooping speech is a deep-seated instinct we have when we sense a language barrier; it’s a teaching mode. But I think there’s something else provocative here: we 
use this broad range of pitch when we’re comfortably in the power position—when we have nothing to prove.


It’s a revealing exercise to singsong your statements to explore your own range. Try it, with a healthy sense of humor. While you say, “Hi, my name is ____,” go nuts and go big, incorporating fanciful swoops up and down.


But don’t mistake my meaning: the antidote to monotone isn’t to force ourselves to speak with pitch variation, like we’re talking to a baby regardless of context. As an exercise, it can be liberating; as advice for the real world, it’s real bad. It’s just swapping out one extreme for another. It’s still vocal hiding. If you push yourself to use more pitch for the sake of pitch, you’ll sound like “We’ll be back … after these messages,” the cheesy sound bite we heard blaring out of our TVs in the ’80s and ’90s, usually with ascending pitch on “back …” followed by a pregnant pause and a twangy slide back down on “after.” I think of this use of pitch on the airwaves as the vocal equivalent of a meme: repeatable, mockable, and absolutely everywhere. You can hear its legacy in Ryan Seacrest’s “This … is 
American Idol
.” It’s promo-voice, sales-speak, razzle-dazzle. It’s newscasters reading teleprompters with no sense of what they’re saying, only throwing practiced melody on it to approximate human speech. But it’s not natural.


For talking heads this mode was an industry standard they were once obligated to match. But in fact, the industry is changing; a number of news shows that connect with younger audiences now feature pundits who reject that style of speech for something more authentic. I’m thinking of Rachel Maddow, Nicolle Wallace, and Joy Reid covering the election of 2020 like humans, or Trevor Noah, or the journalists at Vice News.


For the rest of us, who may accidentally fall into this pattern of swooping extremes, reenacting this meme after its time has passed, it’s worth it to ask why. Although the original impulse among broadcast voices may have been sensible enough—employ a lot of changing pitch to keep an audience engaged—it strikes me as a bad-faith deal. It’s either disrespecting your listener or disrespecting yourself. It patronizingly assumes your listener is too dumb to follow natural speech, or it painfully assumes you the speaker are not enough as you are, and that you have to do something more in order to sound interesting.


But what’s “interesting” is when we tell the truth. If we’re saying that we’ll be back after these messages, we could just … say it. No more, no less. On the podcast 
How to Own the Room,
 Sarah Hurwitz, the Obamas’ speechwriter, memorably put the kibosh on jargony language like “leveraging verticals” or “hardworking middle-class Americans” by pointing out, “If you wouldn’t say it to one person, don’t say it to many people.” I think the singsong host voice is the pitch equivalent to that sentiment. If you wouldn’t speak that way to a real grown-up who’s right in front of you and whom you respect, it won’t do much good to speak that way on a larger scale.


The alternative to telling the truth isn’t lying, per se. But it is vocal hiding—which is actually another function of our highly evolved brain. We can control our vocalizations to cover whatever we want, but, let it be known, it doesn’t always work. There’s a fascinating explanation for why not: in what biologist Richard Dawkins and zoologist John Krebs famously called an evolutionary arms race, they found that over multiple generations predators’ skills improve (in other words, the skilled ones make babies and pass along their knack for the kill), but the skills of their 
prey
 improve over time as well (in other words, 
the ones who get away … get away and then make babies).
6
 If the arms race is really heating up, then these animal foes get better and better at about the same rate—so the results cancel each other out. Here’s our version: we humans have an incredible capacity for vocal deception. Go monotone, go singsong, hide, hide, hide. But you know who’s evolved to have an equally incredible capacity to pick up on teensy deceptions in the voice? Also us. Within just a few words we can detect distress in a friend who’s trying to pretend everything’s fine, a date who’s holding back, a boss who’s buttering us up, or our own voice when we try to sound powerful but don’t really buy it. We’re uniquely adapted to lie, but we’re uniquely adapted to recognize lying as well.


So how do we use pitch variation the “right” amount—meaning the amount that’s not about hiding but about revealing ourselves—even when we’re way out of our comfort zone and our body is going nuts? The answer is, we actively create the conditions within ourselves to play with our pitch. We get better at feeling comfortable even outside of our comfort zone, trusting that when we do, our voice will be so much more expressive. This is super doable, and not just according to me. Dr. Christine Runyan is a clinical psychologist and professor at the University of Massachusetts who specializes in the mind-body response to heavy burdens on our nervous system, from PTSD to the pandemic. Speaking to Krista Tippett on her 
On Being
 podcast, Dr. Runyan recommended simple self-care exercises to release hormones and neurotransmitters like oxytocin and dopamine into the body to work as anti-stressors and joy-makers and reset the body to its optimal state. If vocal hiding or vocal freedom hinges on whether we feel safe, then safety is key. Feeling physically comfortable is key. It’s easy to 
think of permission as a mindset, but in this case—maybe in all cases—it’s also a bodyset. We must ease our body into a sense of permission.


I listened to Dr. Runyan speak of basic exercises like lighting a candle or listening to music, which can calm your nervous system so your body can work for you, not against you. And it reminded me of something my mom used to say when I was growing up and feeling down: “You can’t think your way out of a funk; you have to 
do
 your way out.” So here are some suggestions I’ve collected along the way to add to Dr. Runyan’s, simple ways to leverage the body we have for the voice we want. To 
do
 our way out.


None of these strategies are a big time commitment, but all of them will have an outsized effect. (I think of a woman in a workshop I ran who was a pediatric resident battling constant burnout because of the pandemic but also, she admitted, because she was having trouble convincing herself she had “the right to two minutes alone.” You have the right to two minutes alone.) Try these now, but especially just before a speech or an important meeting when you might be feeling some nerves:


Yes, listen to music. But even better, if you’ve got a private space to step into, literally dance to music and get your hips in the game—you know what they say about hips and lying. But seriously, our hips are the receptacles for negative feelings in the body, so wiggling them can lead to a real sense of release. While you’re at it, whether you light a candle or roll on some essential oil, take the time to smell; it sounds trivial, but if we’re smelling scents, we are present in the moment. And breathe, of course, breathe. But alternatively, get out of breath—sprint or do jumping jacks or a quick yoga flow—and your body will release valuable endorphins. Walk in nature or, 
if that’s not available, look at pictures of something wild and free. Give yourself a hug or get a good one from a friend. Feel your feet on the floor, putting your weight in your heels. As Dr. Runyan says, “Fight or flight has a body posture of being on the toes,”
7
 so figure out how you can invest in a different posture.


You could also meditate, and rather than trying to clear the mind, focus on someone you love and how you look at them—or how they look at you. Remember the last time you belly-laughed. Sit with gratitude, or compassion, or sexual arousal, or goodwill. Let someone do something kind for you or do something kind for them; even opening a door for a stranger or making eye contact can release valuable chemicals that give your body a nudge in the right direction. Eat something that tastes incredible, that feels like food equals love. Put lotion on your skin like your own touch is healing. Or put your hand over your heart, caress it a bit, and, as my friend Liz Kimball reminded me recently, close your eyes and say to yourself, “I trust myself.” Or try “I am enough.” Or “I am showing up as myself, to help someone else do the same.” Or simply “I got this.” Even the word “yes” can alter our chemical makeup. Say the word “no”
8
 when you’re in an fMRI, and you’ll see a sudden release of dozens of stress-producing hormones and destructive neurochemicals that instantly interrupt your brain’s normal functioning and impair your sense of logic and language processing, according to Dr. Andrew Newberg and his coauthor, Mark Waldman, who wrote 
Words Can Change Your Brain
. Conversely, repetition of “yes”—or thinking words like “love” or “peace”—triggers a “relaxation response” that reduces psychological distress.


And finally, especially if you’re about to speak and you feel your nervous system going haywire, get curious—as Dr. Runyan says, curiosity rides through our body on a wave of dopamine, since dopamine is all about the pleasure of anticipation, and what is curiosity but the anticipation of learning something new? It’s a double whammy: the act of being curious releases feel-good hormones, and the curious mind will observe an anxious body and gain some perspective or distance from the feeling. I always find it a satisfying bit of rebellion to, in the middle of an attack of nerves, think: 
Huh, this is fascinating. I wonder what this story is and who it serves.


It’s easy to shortchange yourself and skip trying these exercises out, especially if you’ve got a habit of yelling at yourself for getting nervous. Perhaps you can recall a time that you were psyching yourself out by running through worst-case scenarios before an event only to discover that your body was in fight-or-flight mode, so you switched from worrying if you’ll mess up to admonishing yourself to stop freaking out. You can guess how that would appear on an fMRI scan. It’s a massive bummer and a waste of those precious moments before an event. Not only is it cruel to be that mean to ourselves, it also puts an undue burden on “mindset” when our body is right there, willing to help. Try some of the exercises above instead and get that relaxation response flowing. See which work for you; they’re yours. Each has the potential to shift your body into a mode of feeling safe. And when you feel safe, you’re apt to feel looser and freer in your body and mind. And you’re apt to make bigger, bolder choices, including with your pitch. I bet if you start paying attention, you’ll notice that in your private life, at home, at the bar with 
friends, around anyone who adores you and makes you feel safe to be yourself, your pitch is remarkably varied. Now let’s bring her into more spaces.


When I work with women, they are often coming into their own in new ways professionally and reckoning with a voice that isn’t evolving at the same pace. To get your voice to match your ambition, the safety measures above will help, but I want to offer you something else here, since pitch is indeed destiny: a chance to recognize your own voice story, to identify who told you (with words or actions) to be quieter, or louder, or gentler, or more assertive and to consider what happened as a result. What did your voice do? We don’t need to linger, we don’t need to ruminate, but bring some curiosity here too: why might you be speaking at a higher pitch (or sounding flatter or softer or more unintimidating or pushing more) than you would have if you’d, say, grown up on a utopian island with not a whisper of hierarchical power structures or traditional standards of speech? (Meet you there.)


After I launched the podcast, I started getting tons of messages from listeners sharing their voice stories. I received one from a woman named Jasmine who remembers that in seventh grade she overheard another girl saying that Jasmine had an annoying voice, and she never quite shook it. She stopped speaking up in class and talking on the phone, which fundamentally affected her relationships with friends. “Obviously,” she told me, “when I was older, I realized how totally ridiculous it was that I internalized this offhand comment.” But the damage was done.


I got one from a listener named Natalie whose high school English teacher told her, “Sometimes I think you’re just 
trying
 
to sound stupid,” and years later she still wonders if that’s how she comes across every time she opens her mouth. I’ve heard more variations on this story than I can count. From a friend who was told in second grade that she must be speaking with that high voice “to impress the boys.” From a listener who tried to heed her boss’s advice to have a “smaller footprint” at work because she was apparently too much—and remembers him suggesting she be more like a coworker who, she wrote to me, “was basically a mouse. She was a woman who never spoke.” From my client Sashka, who was keenly aware that her mom was shunned from the PTA groups with whispers that she was “too much,” “too weird,” “too opinionated”—and what Sashka took from that, about how to be an acceptable woman.


These sometimes stealthy, sometimes overt comments about our voice that can seem to be indictments against our accent, race, gender, sexual orientation, or perceived aggressiveness or overconfidence become views that we integrate into our sense of self. My first year of college I got cast as an angry old woman in an over-the-top farce of a play. Though I tried to approach the role with pathos and honesty, the local town paper sent a reviewer, and the middle-aged white guy wrote that my “shrill performance crossed the line from farcical to irritating.” I was eighteen years old, discovering what “angry” and “old” and “woman” sounded like to some ears. I didn’t have to look that quote up; it lives in me.


And it’s the actor who left me a voicemail in a squeaky voice, relaying that she had been a child star and now she’s older and going out on auditions to play grown women. In this message she explained that her acting manager had told her, “You need to find your leading-lady voice,” a sound to match this new era 
of her life. But she didn’t know how. I remember the embarrassment I heard through the phone, in this womanly request coming from this girlish voice. But in a sense, she’s all of us, whether or not there’s as wide a gap between our dreams and our reality. We have each constructed a voice from all these comments and messages, adjusted ourselves accordingly, and have each likely been rewarded for it—perhaps even paid good money for it, as she was. But when we outgrew this voice we’d constructed, when it stopped working in our favor, we had no idea what to do. It may be that your voice story needs a new chapter.


You, me, and this squeaky-voiced actor have a fundamental question to consider as we bend our voice story in a new direction. Is a lower voice the goal? Is its opposite always childish, feminine, “nice,” or <shudder> shrill? After all, we’ve each likely cataloged the accusations of shrill lobbed at women running for office we’ve heard our whole lives. What does that word evoke if not a high pitch? What does its opposite suggest but calm, collected, authoritative? Google “how to sound authoritative” specifically and you’ll see business coach after business coach counseling, “Speak in a low voice.” Seems the best advice to sound like you’re in charge is to sound like a man.


Welcome to the other side of the pitch conversation: we must use pitch 
variation
 if we want to reveal ourselves and express our best ideas as they deserve to be expressed. Range is an indicator that we’re free. But we must clear out the shambles of cultural assumptions around our 
baseline
 pitch if we want true freedom.


To point out the obvious, women’s voices tend to vibrate at 
a higher pitch because of our actual anatomy; our vocal cords are smaller. In general, we speak about an octave higher than men. There’s various data across cultures, but generally the pitch range for men’s voices is about 60–180 Hz and women’s about 160–300 Hz. According to some studies, there’s no overlap at all. And this is actually a huge deal: in nature, the pitch of an animal’s sound correlates to the size of its body, end of story. Little chihuahuas yap; big golden retrievers bark. They each have a bit of range from growl to coo, but their baseline pitch—the middle of their range, the sweet spot—is determined by their size. We instinctively know how big a bird is or how mature a lion is by the frequency of its sound as it hits our ears. And male and female members of those species sound the same. They might learn different calls or instinctively vocalize mating rituals differently, but their voice pitch is not related to their sex, only their size.


Not so with humans. In addition to having more control over our pitch variation than other animals thanks to that big, beautiful prefrontal cortex of ours, we’re also unique in that we get to claim a little animal kingdom anomaly called sexual dimorphism: although we all start out sounding the same as babies, by the time we’re adults, men and women’s bodies produce a noticeably different baseline pitch from each other.


In fact, this voice distinction is so noticeable that far before puberty hits, little kids begin “performing gender” with their pitch. According to linguists Dr. Penelope Eckert and Dr. Sally McConnell-Ginet in 
Language and Gender,
 “At the ages of four to five years, despite their identical vocal apparatus, girls and boys begin to differentiate the fundamental frequency of their speaking voice. Boys tend to round and extend their lips, lengthening the vocal tract, whereas girls tend to spread their 
lips (with smiles, for example), shortening the vocal tract. Girls are raising their pitches, boys lowering theirs.”
9


I learned this particular bonbon years ago and was properly dismayed. But the fact that other animals’ voices 
don’t
 discriminate between the sexes was news to me. When I first read about this, in John Colapinto’s 
This Is the Voice,
 I was struck with … 
why?
 For any woman who’s ever been told “You talk too high,” or “You’ll never be taken seriously with that voice,” indeed for any of us who’ve ever withstood negative feedback because the male voice is the standard and the female voice is the deviation, I had to wonder, what kind of evolutionary BS is this?


The best answer is sexual selection. If we consider that the big drop in men’s voices happens with their surge of testosterone during puberty, we can conclude it has to do with attracting a mate. Like any string instrument, a thicker, longer set of vocal cords produces a deeper sound, so the hypothesis is that women prefer this sound or that it’s associated with good baby-making. And indeed, various studies have borne this out. One such experiment, conducted by researchers at McMaster University in Ontario, Canada, began with “Humans appear to be unique among primates in that sexual dimorphism of adult voice pitch is well beyond what may be explained by height alone”
10
 and went on to find that heterosexual college students of the opposite sex consistently deemed high voices in women and low voices in men the most “attractive.”


Another, at University College London, asked male volunteers to listen to a sample of a woman’s voice that was systematically altered for pitch and asked women to do the same with a man’s vocal sample.
1
 According to the report, results 
showed clearly that male listeners preferred female voices that were higher-pitched, correlating with a smaller body size (and youthful, childbearing potential), while females preferred to hear male voices that were lower-pitched, suggesting larger body size (and hearty sperm). For a society that idealizes big men and small women, it all feels very cliché. Apparently, just as we pick up on the fact that it’ll behoove us to take up as little space as possible, to suck in and diet and shrink, we also raise the pitch of our voices to give off the impression of diminutive stature. The McMaster report suggests “high-pitched women’s voices are perceived as feminine and youthful and flirtatious …. Furthermore, women tend to raise their voice pitch when presented with an attractive man’s face and asked to leave a phone message for him.” Hello, Starbucks Voice.


But if higher-pitched women are hot, then how come the top-ten list of sexiest women’s voices is always weighted toward Scarlett Johansson and Kathleen Turner (of Jessica Rabbit fame), women with throaty, husky, low voices? If all this pitch stuff is about ancient mating rituals, then we’ve got to look at how the voice codes for sexuality and consider how that maps along power lines. After all, we’re probably not just trying to come across as hot; we’re trying to own our lives.


In her book 
Wordslut,
 Amanda Montell considers how to reconcile these listicles of sexy voices with the science, and muses that the low voices of the women on the lists sound like “the voice you wake up with after sleeping next to someone (and maybe doing other stuff). It’s a sign of intimacy. People hear that voice and they picture you in bed.”
12
 Notably, I might add, they don’t picture you holding the attention of a crowd or leading the rebellion. Glorifying sexy bedroom voice seems to 
me a convenient way to re-domesticate women, to push us back out of the public sphere, even when we perform the lower pitches traditionally associated with power.


And yet, speaking low has been a refuge for a lot of us. In the Introduction, I described my brush with vocal nodules the year I turned twenty-four. It was the year I learned that, at some point during my own path from girlhood to womanhood, I had begun to manipulate the pitch of my voice beyond what my anatomy could take. It was not to seem more attractive to men but perhaps to signal its opposite: I’m independent, take me seriously, don’t mess with me. I didn’t entirely know I was doing it, and it was ultimately self-destructive, but it also worked.


Throughout my teens, people thought I was older than I was, although my appearance didn’t suggest anything particularly adult. I sounded assured, competent, in on the joke, and grown-up. As a result, I 
was
 taken seriously, and I 
wasn’t
 messed with. Of course, the luxury of adults treating me with respect can in part be attributed to privileges I didn’t recognize I had at the time: being white, middle-class, and raised in a surprisingly functional, loving household. But I attribute at least part of how adults treated me to the fact that I spoke below my body’s “optimum pitch”—the official term for your voice’s sweet spot.


Margaret Thatcher, the U.K.’s first female prime minister, did the same, famously hiring a vocal coach from the National Theatre in London to help her lower her pitch to sound more authoritative. And it worked. Conventional wisdom is that lower is better, and especially for women, borrowing markers of male leadership is a quick fix for getting taken more seriously. Which must have been going through the mind of Eliza
beth Holmes, the disgraced CEO of Theranos, when she perfected her wunderkind sound. It’s well documented that her strikingly deep voice was a deliberate construct, and her case is the latest high-profile evidence that while sometimes our voice happens to us, sometimes we happen to our voice.


So, let’s happen to our voice. I’m certainly a fan of taking charge of our sound and getting conscious of our habits and our options—but the tool below is meant to ensure that you do so in ways that avoid the harm I caused myself or the joke Ms. Holmes’s voice has become. Ideally, we find the voice that’s right for us, not just another contrivance to replace the last.


Here’s a simple way to find your optimum pitch—the general range of vibrations your own vocal folds want to make independent of outside stimulus: notice the next time you say “mm-hm” in the affirmative, or you can record yourself answering yes-or-no questions if you want to set a friend up to help you, sometimes answering with words and sometimes just with that “mm-hm.” The gentle hum of yes-ness against closed lips is your optimum pitch. If you can trust yourself to “mm-hm” without manipulating it then add on. Open your mouth afterward for a simple “my” on the same breath. “Mm-hm … my.” Match your pitch. If it feels like singing at first, that’s fine. Then try a more complicated phrase. “Mm-hm my name is ___. It’s lovely to meet you.” Does it feel lower than you’re used to speaking, or higher?


For me, it felt much higher. Trying to match my “mm-hm” pitch meant a voice came out of my mouth that I didn’t recognize, and my brain went wild. Barbara McAfee, a voice coach, writes in her book 
Full Voice,
 “When you step beyond that cherished identity, in this case by using your voice in a new way, you’ll likely feel a reflexive ‘uh-oh’ from your ego,”
13
 the job 
of which, she says, is to defend the status quo. That’s what I felt.


Similarly, when I tracked down Barbara Buchanan, my own speech pathologist I worked with when I was twenty-four, she told me that the real challenge with finding your optimum pitch 
isn’t
 finding it, it’s reconciling it to your sense of self. In her experience, these days young women usually come to her because they’re speaking below their optimum pitch, doing the pressed-down monotone thing they associate with masculinity, and thus power. Their number-one resistance is “I don’t want to sound like a little girl,” she tells me over Zoom, throwing her hands up. “So, the options are boring man or flaky little girl? Where are 
you
? You have a voice, you have range, you have 
life
 in you.”


And she assures me that everyone responds as I did when I tried to speak in my optimum pitch for the first time and cringed at the sound. I was experiencing my voice kinesthetically in a different way, she says. “If you’re used to talking from your throat, it feels like pressure and that pressure feels satisfying—it feels normal. If you go up in pitch just a half-step, a step, to speak in your optimum pitch but now you switch to vibrating in your mask [this is what us voice people call your face], it feels different. Our perception is that this is 
really
 different, and everyone is going to know and we’re going to come across like a phony.”


As she’s telling me this, I’m brought back to that room all those years ago, with the fluorescent lights and the metal desk and probably the last tape recorder ever sold before they became obsolete. I remember the physical feeling of terror that I’d be perceived as a phony, which was even worse than the fear of coming across as a ditz. I remember listening to the re
cording of my “mm-hm” and my attempt to match my pitch. I remember that on the inside, the difference felt huge—my own inner ear, plus my assumptions, my stories, my fears, heard a great abyss between low and high. But on the outside, trying to listen back objectively, I noted that the difference between how I had been speaking and how Barbara was asking me to speak sounded almost imperceptible. Literally, the world would not know. But if I was able to do it, my entire life would come back to me.


I had been on “vocal rest” for a month before I started these sessions with Barbara. I had given up the first juicy acting role I’d gotten in drama school, I had withdrawn from socializing, and I attended classes mute and mostly ignored. I had been mugged during that month, by some high school kids who grabbed my bag off me with all my belongings, but I couldn’t yell after them. I opened my mouth to—I don’t know what exactly. Curse them so impressively that they’d be intimidated enough to drop my bag? In any case, I tried. But nothing came out and I stood in the street feeling utterly powerless. But with Barbara’s help, I saw that if I could just get over my ego, I would have a voice again. A new and improved voice—strange to my ear, but like home to my body.


It may be useful to consider the costs and benefits of speaking in your optimum pitch. Does a voice sitting comfortably in its natural register help you get what you want or hurt your cause? After all, Elizabeth Holmes convinced a lot of people to give her power and money until she didn’t. For a while there, it worked.


So I say, by all means, experiment. Lower your pitch to talk to that guy who keeps interrupting you and see if it shuts him 
up. Use your Starbucks Voice with that curmudgeon on the work call because he just might make a big donation to your organization if you sweeten him up, and playing the game in that context isn’t much sacrifice. But when you have the chance to speak as yourself, it’s my hope that you decide the cost-benefit analysis is moot. If speaking too high cuts you off from your sense of power so you’re constantly playacting your gender like a five-year-old, and speaking too low strips your vocal folds, neither is worth it.


Because here’s the bottom line: When you get a good massage or finish a run or come out of corpse pose in yoga, your voice is lower. It just is. The same is true if you were to perform basic speech pathology exercises designed to relax the larynx, like the ones I shared in the previous chapter (the thumb-on-teeth and creaking-gate ones). There’s a correlation between tension and a higher-pitched voice—for both men and women. Adrenaline, which often courses through us when we’re about to present publicly, tightens our vocal cords unless we help ourselves feel safe. Lying does, too, by the way. I was curious if we go up in pitch when we lie—a hunch I had from playing with my kid, and realizing that if he says, “No, pretend you don’t know where I am,” I instinctively raise my pitch to express faux-concern. “Uh-oh! I don’t know where you are!” And it turns out there are studies that provide evidence of an association between elevated pitch and false statements, in more sophisticated adult conversations. Anger or responding to someone in disagreement raises our pitch too. As does sensing that we’re in a relatively low-power position; studies like one at the University of Stirling, Scotland, show that “regardless of self-perceived social status, people tend to talk to high status individuals using a higher pitch.”
14


The common denominator in all these examples is that we’re not in a relaxed state. Excitement also raises the pitch of our voice, by the way. And you know I’m going to say nothing’s wrong with that—just don’t get stuck up there. Let each one of these impulses affect your voice, of course, because this isn’t about trying to inhibit this beautiful and versatile apparatus of ours from expressing all the dynamics available. But then—then!—wend your way back to your baseline. Reinvest in your sweet spot. Trust that you’ll have power not by sounding like a little girl or a boring man but by sounding like yourself, on purpose. If you feel yourself straining from topping or bottoming out, it’s possible to recover in the moment, even up on a stage, even at the mic. Take a breath. Take a sip of water. You can even give yourself a little “mm-hm”—it’s subtle and no one will know what you’re doing, except us.


I recently got a different kind of note, a direct message from a woman who had participated in a workshop with me. She told me that for the first time in fifteen years of conducting bicoastal live TV interviews for her job, she decided not to purposely lower her voice. She wrote, “I felt relieved. I just warmed up my voice and kept it hydrated and decided to let it go. It was cathartic.”


I dream of a day when that’s the new sound of power. No more of this Thatcher-esque bluffing and no more reinforcing old stereotypes by performing our gender. Maybe trans folks won’t feel obligated to conform to a vocal performance either—or rather, maybe cis-gendered folks won’t feel the need to enforce vocal gender performance in trans folks before we accept or protect them as fellow humans. Heck, maybe men will feel safe enough to find their optimum pitch too, and we’ll all be free. In fact, it turns out that as we get older, our voice 
pitches begin to converge anyway: men in their later years produce less testosterone and their vocal cords get thinner and their sound reedier, while women, thanks to the rush of estrogen during menopause, end up with longer, thicker cords and a lower voice. This sexual dimorphism folds back on itself, packs up, and slips away.


But in the meantime, the trick is to practice finding our own optimum pitch and give our egos a hug if they revolt. And the other trick is to question assumptions about pitch and power as they come up in ourselves or in society at large—and listen closely to the role models who represent a new sound of power. When then-bartender and candidate for United States Congress Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez came through Los Angeles in the summer of 2018, after her momentous primary win but before the general election, I grabbed a ticket to hear her speak at a sweaty church in Koreatown, sensing she was someone I should keep an eye on, or an ear. While I inched along city streets blasting the air-conditioning in my car, I called my mom and told her where I was going. She exclaimed, “Oh good, she needs you!”


Oh God, I thought. My mom’s my biggest fan and she meant well. “Mom, I think she’s doing just fine without me.”


“No, no,” she pushed back. “That voice. I can’t take her seriously with that voice.” My mother is a second-wave feminist, the kind who got a Fulbright scholarship and went to law school and kept her last name when she married. But my mother couldn’t take her seriously with 
that voice
. I knew what she meant, of course. AOC’s voice is a bit nasal. A bit higher-pitched than we’re used to hearing from elected officials. She sounds distinctly Millennial and distinctly female and like she 
figured out how to make a lot of tips behind the bar (I don’t say this to be flip; we inevitably pick up valuable vocal skills from the rooms we’ve mastered). She sounds like where she’s from and who she is. She sounds like she has no interest in saving herself from herself.


“Or maybe,” I responded, feeling a burst of fire that would go on to spark my podcast and this book, “maybe, she’s teaching us what getting taken seriously might sound like.”


After all, it is up to us, as the listening public as well as the speaking public, what we take seriously. It’s up to us whom we give authority, and it should be based on merit more than how we sound. This is totally obvious, of course, but in the real world the equation is complicated by implicit biases born of traditions and industries and policies meant to keep those once powerful, still powerful. My mom is not the bad guy in this story; most of us could stand to take a flamethrower to our preconceptions. Here’s your chance to start.


During the summer of 2018 I also met the man who coached Hillary Clinton for her televised debates two years prior, a man who gets brought in to coach female candidates tapped by leading U.S. organizations dedicated to preparing women to run for office. I was thrilled to nab a date with this top political communication coach and posed a question to him as we were walking along a muggy street near the Portrait Gallery in Washington, D.C., where I’d just seen the Obamas’ official portraits. “Do you think the paradigm is shifting,” I asked him, trying to sound detached, “around how women in politics can sound? Is it no longer a given that the woman who sounds the most like a man wins?”


He took in a long breath and narrowed his eyes.


“Huh,” he finally responded. Like it was an entirely new thought. Like it was something he’d simply never considered, or never had to. And that was that. I realized, suddenly, that I had been positioning myself on the outside looking in. I was newer to this communication coaching work than he was, after all. I realized, suddenly, that I’d been asking the wrong question—that I didn’t need to ask a question at all. AOC and RuPaul and Chanel Miller and Liz Plank and Greta Thunberg and Meghan Markle all speaking in their own voice, and us—we are the answer.


The solution to sounding powerful simply cannot be to artificially lower our baseline pitch, to act as an extension of the hierarchical system that says high voices belong to small, cute things and low voices belong to power. The solution is to reject that. Our destiny is to find our own best voice for our own body and then teach the world 
that’s
 what power sounds like.

















5


Tone




Gather all your parts. Leave nothing behind.


—JENNIFER ARMBRUST
,
 
Proposals for the Feminine Economy
1




At the start of quarantine, our little dog Moxie was suddenly my kid’s only companion. The high-energy four-year-old kept trying to pick the dog up, move her body, or get her to sit by whining “come onnnnnn” or pushing her backside down. It was rough. One day, when Moxie was getting particularly aggrieved at his advances, and I was getting particularly concerned because even little dogs will bite if threatened, I said, “My love, you can’t control others. You can only convince them.” He looked at me—eyes bright—and using his most determined voice, he said with optimism, “Moxie … sit!” 
And she sat.
 I thought back on all the times I had told my kid to use his “strong voice” when he would get whiny with me. But the dog taught him the lesson better than I ever could: tone of voice matters.


We can’t control others, but we can control our tone of voice in order to convince others. This does not mean that manipulating our tone is 
manipulative.
 It means that becoming aware of our tone, and aware of our tonal options, has enor
mous practical value for us and for our listeners. Tone is how we sound when we say something—inviting, firm, playful, disaffected, excited, disdaining. Nietzsche reportedly said, “We often refuse to accept an idea merely because the tone of voice in which it has been expressed is unsympathetic to us,”
2
 and Moxie really proved his point.


I realized with amusement, as I watched this transformation between kid and dog, that the impromptu pro tip I had given him is actually the entire basis of civil society. You can’t control others, but you can convince them. It’s how democracy works. It’s how healthy relationships work. And it’s how good public speaking works. So, how 
do
 we use tone to convince others? Well, it turns out, dogs respond to straightforward instruction and uncomplicated love. I dare you to consider that humans aren’t so different.


Of course, yes, we’re more complex and there are entire fields of linguistics and psychology devoted to mapping the nuances of social interactions and a billion books on how to persuade and influence and cajole people for good or for ill. Plus, dogs don’t mind a good sit; what if what you want of your audience is a harder ask? But when you’re preparing to pitch or present and you begin to feel overwhelmed by the audacity of your request, remember tone and remember Moxie. If in those moments you communicate with strength (here’s my big idea, here’s what I’d like you to do) and warmth (and I speak with great love) you’ll be pretty damn convincing.


Nell came to me because she was pitching an app for women to rooms of venture capitalists who were mostly men. Nell’s in her late forties, idealistic, with a background in the nonprofit sector. She’s sunny and eager and had a nagging sense that she was coming across as too “soft” in these 
meetings—that these potential investors were interested in her product but unconvinced about her. It was an existential crisis, she told me. Should she fight to come across as more authoritative, tough, and businesslike, even if it was inauthentic? One of her advisers was pushing her to do so—but the prospect felt icky. And what if she went too far and came across as cold? She was well aware of what’s said of women who get low marks on the likability front.


Clients have told me they’ve been called bulldogs, angry, a bitch, pushy, aggressive, defensive, and 
difficult
 (that one especially gets my heart pounding because it’s so insidious: all over Hollywood it’s whispered to suggest this female director, or this actress, is behaving in ways for which we’d never penalize a man). These insults get thrown about when a woman in any industry attempts to stand up for herself or her ideas, when she dares to say something inconvenient or shake up the status quo, when she’s stretching her leader wings or attempting to find her strong voice.


This is the famed “double bind,” the tension of two seemingly irreconcilable demands: being both authoritative enough to be taken seriously and likable enough to be kept around. It’s a real phenomenon and the source of endless headaches. “How do I shut down this mansplainer?” “How do I stop interrupters?” “How do I get the raise I deserve?” “How do I get the credit I’m due?” The unspoken part of every one of these questions is, 
without sounding like a
 …


But Nell’s crisis got me thinking. No matter who we are or what outdated power structures we’re up against, there are always two parts to communication: what we have to say, and how we have to say it so people can hear us. Those of us who don’t look or sound like traditional power may need to work 
harder on that second part than someone else does, and that is unfair. Find friends and allies and primal scream when you must—I do.


But the fact that each of us must package our thoughts for our specific listener is actually universal. It’s what happens when any of us asks our partner for a favor. It’s what happens when we talk to a friend about a prickly subject or call someone out (or in) on their biases. It’s what happens at the office and outside of it. It’s what happens across cultures and inside our own home. It’s certainly what happens in high-stakes rooms and on big stages. That we can’t just spew our thoughts but must filter them for the people around us is the cost of true connection. It’s the cost of living in a civil society. It’s the cost of getting what we want. The question isn’t “Why do we have to jump through these hoops?” That’s just being a human around other humans. The question is “How do we do it well and with great joy?”


The answer is all about tone. The double bind is about tone. And the best way to untie it, light the patriarchy on fire, and lead the world out of its ashes is tone too. Yes, I sound dramatic, but I mean it. I want you to feel the power that comes from finding your authority voice and being authentically you 
at the same time.


According to Merriam-Webster, tone is the “style or manner of expression in speaking or writing.” Which is to say, it’s a lot of things. “Tone” captures the overall feeling of a speech or a meeting or an event. In terms of a book or a movie, does it make an audience laugh or cry or sweat or relax? Those reactions are determined by tone as much as by content. And tone is also the word we sometimes use to describe the ineffable quality of someone’s voice. It’s the timbre, which is mostly due 
to the size of the speaker’s vocal cords, their age, and their health or hard living. French philosopher Roland Barthes wrote about the “grain”
3
 of the voice, meaning the unique 
us-ness
 in our sound that evades description. That “grain” is the reason we can recognize someone by their voice, just like we recognize their face.


And tone is the word for the small shifts in shade and feeling we give words or phrases every time we speak. Think about playing the instrument that is your voice: tone isn’t pitch (notes up and down the scale) but the feeling of the notes themselves (rich, thin, breathy, tired, energized, playful, direct). It’s not volume or tempo but the spirit of what you’re saying, which may indeed make you speak louder and faster, or in whispers and at a slower pace for suspense. Our parents told us, “Don’t speak to me in that tone” when we were little, and we’ve probably all found ourselves obsessing over an offhand remark from a coworker or a date that seemed to have an edge incongruent with the words themselves.


These days we even hear sarcasm and sincerity in text form—give or take those cringy occasions when we misread the tone entirely. Emojis add gesture and emotional nuance to flat text, and as SMS messages and social media have become central communication tools, burgeoning punctuation standards further help the cause: “Why did you use that period?” is the new “Why did you use that tone with me?” (Who knew a tiny black dot could blare with passive-aggression?) Real-time texting and tweeting are informal and conversational, more like spoken language than traditional written language, and the various methods we’ve each found to make our tone clear is what linguist Gretchen McCulloch calls, in her book 
Because Internet,
 “just the latest in a long list of creative strategies to 
say without saying.”
4
 Tone is saying without saying. Or, really, saying 
in addition to
 saying. It’s the crucial information we’re sharing beyond the words themselves about our attitude toward what we’re saying.


There’s a great video on YouTube that mashes up the moment in so many movies where an actor says “let’s get out of here.”
5
 When you listen to them all back-to-back it’s impossible to miss how different each one sounds. Which tracks. After all, convincing someone to run from a monster tends to require a different tone than, say, asking them to slip out of a loud party for a moonlit stroll. We operate this vocal machinery every day—but we’re not always conscious we’re doing it until we have to be. Until the tone we’re using doesn’t work. Until the interrupter keeps interrupting.
