 was an example of “pluralistic ignorance” or “cognitive dissonance.” Suddenly I was a Psych 101 zealot. But I couldn’t get over how satisfying it was that these words spun murky hunches into proven patterns of human behavior.


Naming a singular experience turns it collective, and there is, of course, power in the collective. Naming a lonely experience makes us less alone: See #metoo. Or digital blackface. Or the gender pay gap. Or phrases specific to your industry that hold communal power because your community has deemed it so (jargon, when used to include rather than exclude, has its plusses). Linguists know the knack words have, as Amanda Montell writes in 
Wordslut,
 to “legitimize experiences, as if an idea only becomes valid once it’s christened with a title.” Label it and it becomes real. Use a new word you hear and like, and you’re collaborating in the christening process. Crotchety types may lament new words entering common parlance, but I think grabbing the baton of a newly coined term and running with it is a generous act of civic life: the words you use may validate others’ experience. And the sacred space between us can become a truer reflection of those present.


If you’re anxious about losing your words in the hot seat, take just a moment before you open your mouth to jot down on paper words or phrases that will help you get your idea across, words that pack a punch. It might not be the most original advice to suggest writing down a few notes before you 
speak, but I’ve never heard anyone add the most important part: trust that the in-between words don’t matter. Let that shit go. What comes out will be right enough. It’s a birth, and people love babies.


Or there’s always coining new words or phrases yourself. (As my kid says, in the great neologism tradition of William Shakespeare, “When you make up a word … now it’s a word!”) Like Rebecca Solnit’s “mansplaining,” which surely spread because of just how satisfying it was to finally have a term for something real but not yet named. Or Priya Parker’s “pop-up rules,” a handy concept I didn’t know I needed till I heard it and it became immediately indispensable. Or the term “girlboss” morphing from a form of praise to a form of critique. My friend Liz Kimball says naming and claiming is a revolutionary act if historically we weren’t the ones to conquer land and plant our flag. Of course, I’m not big on conquering with all its toxic colonial connotations, but I am big on noticing the ways we might shy away from laying claim to even our own ideas. I wrote a note with “NAMING AND CLAIMING” in bold chicken-scratch after Liz told it to me and hung it on my wall while I was writing this book to remind myself to declare ownership when I had the chance. It’s definitely a practice.


Here’s an irony: When I work with actors, their job is to turn words on the page into spontaneous-seeming utterances when the camera’s rolling, and a good way to achieve this is by inserting a sort of struggle to find the next word. To sound, in other words, like real life. But in real life, we dream of hiding that struggle—the very thing that makes communication spontaneous. The very thing that makes it credible rather than contrived.


I’ve received so many emails from would-be clients con
cerned that they’re not articulate enough, wondering if I can teach them how to be, their anxiety glowing on the screen. They surely know on some level that forgetting a word or rambling isn’t life or death—but it feels like it. And first impressions do count. And how we’re perceived when we open our mouths is high-stakes. If we seem inarticulate, we might be dismissed as incompetent, scattered, ditzy, not worth anyone’s time. To be clear, when I say “we” here, I’m referring to those of us who sense we have something to prove. This obsession is not our fault, and we are not defective. We are responding to the reality of an experience that does not yet have a name: speaking without the privilege of the benefit of the doubt.


Those who put a premium on sounding articulate (you at your most critical, audiences at their most critical) are responding to a power imbalance: when you’ve got power, you don’t need to sound articulate. No one cares if you don’t. Name any hero of yours and I bet they’ve lost their train of thought in public, and I bet you’ve happily forgiven them, no-brainer. Will people judge 
you
 for fumbling, though? Yeah, maybe. Especially if you have melanated skin in a white-dominated culture, especially if you speak English as a second language or with an accent that sounds rural or strikes others as less educated, especially if you don’t have connections or cash or cachet. Biases abound. But is the solution to fear the fumble? It is not.


Perfectionism can paralyze us—and maybe that’s what the powerful want. But the opposite of perfect isn’t defective—it’s alive. Anna Deavere Smith has spent her career transcribing people’s real-life speech and turning it into art. She wrote in her book about politics and theater, 
Talk to Me,
 “I think we can learn a lot about a person in the very moment that language 
fails them. In the very moment that they have to be more creative than they would have imagined in order to communicate. It’s the very moment that they have to dig deeper than the surface to find words, and at the same time it’s a moment when they want to communicate very badly.”
5
 I love those moments. Start to notice what happens when someone you’re listening to struggles for how to articulate their next thought. Notice if you lean in. We may all hold biases, but I believe most of us also have biases 
toward
 honest speech. We appreciate something true more than something pretty.


The word “articulate” comes from the root 
articulus,
 a Latin word for small connecting parts. We can articulate our body, or we can articulate our ideas; both are about engaging simple components that, together, make something complex. In the case of speaking, monosyllabic words often get the shaft, but they are everything. “Yes we can” is and will always be more articulate than, say, “Let us remain confident in our commitment to the inalienable truth that in absolute terms, the likelihood of failure is vanishingly small!”


Maybe for you, “articulate” brings to mind those exceedingly polished people whose words effortlessly flow; maybe you associate the term with the biggest Scholastic Aptitude Test words you know, and it’s hard to believe that simple language can be better. I find a lot of clients carry a myth that formal speech is everywhere—that work is a formal space and part of being a professional or nailing the job interview or speaking at the podium is to conform to its formality and use big words to signal it. This myth may be stopping you from giving yourself permission to show up authentically in work contexts where it’s authenticity, actually, that would seal the deal. Say what matters like it matters, regardless of the words. 
Yes we can.


Practically speaking, formal language can be the dialect or code-switching difference between “walking” and “walkin’,” or “I’m going to go” and “I’mna go,” or avoiding idioms and swearwords versus betting the fucking farm. But its greatest distinction lies in decisions of sentence structure and word choice. “English is unique in possessing a synonym for each level of our culture: popular, literary, and scholarly,” says Bill Bryson in 
The Mother Tongue,
 “so that we can, according to our background and cerebral attainments, rise, mount, or ascend a stairway, shrink in fear, terror, or trepidation and think, ponder, or cogitate upon a problem.” In other words, we have a ton of English words that mean roughly the same thing and that seem to have stuck around only to offer extra layers of fanciness. (That’s my informal way of restating what Bryson said above. Call me a populist.)


I’m all for using fancy, show-off language when it serves us—to screw with people’s expectations, to take back our power, or to give us a tickle. But not to achieve some abstract formality we often don’t even need. Not when the attempt can leave us feeling like we don’t have the 
right words.
 If we’re in an emergency, we’re not going to tell anyone to mount or ascend anything, and let’s be honest, we’re not going to say “rise” either—we’re going to say “Go up!” And that has nothing to do with our cerebral attainments or our background.


I’m not aware of any studies tracking the proportion of spoken language that falls into each of Bryson’s three categories—popular, literary, scholarly—during, say, congressional hearings or TED Talks or your boss’s most formal address at that industry-wide conference. But I would bet that (a) it’s a healthy mix of the three, and (b) the moments that landed the best were utterances from the first category. As youth poet laureate 
Amanda Gorman told 
Vogue,
 she “looked to Abraham Lincoln or Frederick Douglass … or Martin Luther King, and the ways in which they used words to communicate the ideals of the nation in elegant rhetoric that [never] felt as if it was locked away in an ivory tower.”


Informal language, not formal, makes the world go round. Casual, conversational, simple language helps people connect. Unless you’re engaged in legal proceedings (one of the most formal of contexts for language, where the interpretation of speech is at its most humorless and every utterance is documented and dissected), you’ve probably got more leeway to speak informally than you think. Informal, however, is not the same as careless. Your words count, not because they’re impressive in and of themselves but because they’re your chance to be as accurate as possible in capturing what you mean for the specific ears you hope will hear them.


Accurate is not always about cold facts either. One of the most useful distinctions I’ve ever heard about word choice comes from progressive activist and minister Michael Dowd. He speaks of day language and night language, two modes of speech that serve different purposes. Day language, he explains, “describes what is measurably so, what is physically, consensually true.”
6
 Day language is hard, specific, facts, and data. Night language, on the other hand, “is the realm of poetry, myth, symbol, metaphor, and traditional religious language. It’s the language that inspires; it touches the heart, moves the soul, brings us to tears and calls us to awe.” Night language can help convey truths more accurately than facts ever can. And as you might imagine for these two modes of speech, each has its strength but each fits different scenarios. Consider if your audience or your industry has some ingrained 
assumptions about these different styles that you can either play into or disrupt. Do your people need facts or truths?


Final note: casual is not the same as mumbly. It takes just as much energy to speak informally as formally (though it may be more fun). Talking, at its most satisfying, is a full-body sport and English especially can be quite athletic. To get granular for a moment: some languages, like French, place equal weight on every syllable. 
En français,
 the word for “alphabet” is 
l’alphabet,
 pronounced lal-fah-beh (punch the air on each syllable to get the feel, right left right), and the result is that it can feel quite measured. But in English, most of the weight goes on the first syllable of that word (punch the air on 
al
). English speakers love strong and weak syllables so much that we’ll often minimize a vowel in the weak position to give the strong one an added oomph. In fact, the punch on 
al
 is punchier than any syllable in French. “Whaddya think” is an example of this phenomenon in English (“you” smooshes into a tiny 
yuh
 so more energy can land on “think”), or the fact that the word “our” in a phrase is usually pronounced like “are” instead of like “hour.” This strong syllable/weak syllable give-and-take is a move we make all the time when we’re not feeling self-conscious.


In fact, the more deliberate we are with our thoughts—when we really know what we’re saying and why we have decided to say it—the more we naturally energize our mouth muscles on the syllables that matter and conserve energy on the ones that don’t. We make our point like a boxer. And our audience feels the impact.


I’m into milking words—I’m an English major, for God’s sake—and I certainly have nothing against using elaborate language when the mood strikes. But the 
right words
 are the 
words that work. The right words are the ones that say something true, that feel good coming out of your mouth, and that move your listeners.


So how do you make language feel good? Yeah, it’s not obvious. But it’s not as abstract as it sounds, and you can do it with what you’ve already got. It is about recruiting your body—your breath, your heart, your juicy middles, your wild imagination, 
and
 your articulator muscles in your mouth. It is about 
using
 your language to stay in the moment, even when you’re nervous, so you can be present for the birth of your words. Here’s how.


My client Amina was running for city council. She’s got young kids, thick, wavy black hair, and a teacherly assuredness. But prior to announcing her candidacy, she’d never spoken to a large room full of people in her life. And when she came to me, she had a prepared speech written by committee and less than a week before she had to deliver it to a massive crowd. I asked her to stand at one end of the long table we were working at and say it aloud. She took a breath and launched in, reading it since it was still so new. I took notes, flagging sections that sounded more like written English, body language that betrayed her discomfort, and builds that weren’t yet building. She reminded me of so many other women trying to come across like a “person who has authority” but who end up coming across as … not quite a person. A bit robotic. A bit absent. A bit at the generic monster’s whim. As though hiding is the only path to power.


We began by rewriting sections of the speech to make it feel more conversational; that helped. We swapped out words that were more formal than necessary for ones that would in
spire connection. (Even “hi” and “thanks” instead of “hello” and “thank you” can prime an audience to relate to you better.) And we rewrote sentences that felt too formally structured. Written speech is organized around the unit of the sentence. But spoken speech is organized around thoughts, which can be a single word (“Plastics!”), or multiple sentences strung together or a collection of sentence fragments an English teacher would raise an eyebrow at, like the following. This is a moving moment from Scarlett Johansson’s 
Finding Your Roots
 episode where she processes seeing images of family who never made it out of the Warsaw ghetto:




Wow. That’s sad.
7
 [Tearing up] Sorry. And I promised myself I wouldn’t cry. But it’s hard not to. That is crazy. I mean, you really couldn’t imagine the horror. It’s just so … crazy to imagine—Now I know why you put the tissues there. [Laughs] It’s crazy to imagine that Saul would be on the other side, selling bananas. On Ludlow Street. And how different it would be, being in America at that time. The fate of one brother versus the other. Makes me feel more deeply connected to that side of myself. That side of my family.




That’s how we talk, and it should be. It’s what saves us from having to find the perfect words: we get to rely on groups of words to do the real job of communicating our thoughts. Linguists call it “chunking,” which might be my least favorite word in the English language—but it’s such a useful concept. (Why didn’t they go with bunching or bundling? Can I call it bunching? Yes, I can.) Bunching frees us from the shackles of the all-important word, but also from the period. Periods just don’t 
matter in spoken speech until we’re truly ending a thought and taking a pause or relinquishing the floor. Bunches are the reigning unit of meaning.


And bunches are notated not with any specific written punctuation but with oral punctuation: a breath. I transcribed ScarJo above using periods where I thought I heard breaths. But look to speeches on big stages (and where the camera doesn’t cut away as much) to see breaths that mark bunches and bunches that make meaning. Take, for example, Michelle Obama’s 2008 Democratic National Convention speech, starting around ten minutes in. I’ve notated the primary breaths with parentheses—and you can see they come just when it would behoove her to take a micro pause and gather the next thought:




All of us driven by the simple belief that () the world as it is just won’t do () that we have an obligation to fight for the world as it should be () and 
that
 is the thread that connects our hearts () 
that
 is the thread that runs through 
my
 journey () and 
Barack’s
 journey () and so many 
other
 improbable journeys that have brought us here tonight () where the current of history meets this new tide of hope () and you see 
that
 is why I 
love
 this country.
8




Try saying it out loud, taking in a breath at each of those ()’s, and don’t go on until you feel an impulse to speak. Breaths are not just everything I spoke about in 
Chapter 1
. They’re also punctuation; they’re a unit of meaning. They mean 
I’m here.
 They mean 
I’m letting what I just said affect me, and collecting myself for what I’m about to say.


But Amina, back in my office, kept falling into the same vocal pattern over and over, reading sentences instead of communicating thoughts, and the result was that each sentence sounded like the one before, regardless of its content. Each had a sort of “Duh-duh-DUH-duh-duh? Duh-duh-DUH-duh-duh?” quality (the question marks denoting an upward inflection that sounded like the baked-in “do you know what I mean?” from 
Chapter 2
). Listen for this when you hear people reading out loud. For most of us, when it’s not our words (and often even when it is), real work is required to inhabit the thoughts, to own each bunch out loud and breathe when one ends in order to energize the next one.


We’ll get into what that work is below. But here’s your word of warning: If you skip this step, if you haven’t gotten behind the thoughts or imagined the images or found the why, it’s way too easy to disconnect the sounds coming out of your mouth from the thoughts they were meant to convey. And without that connection, dramatic moments won’t feel dramatic, funny bits won’t land, and stories won’t move your audience. As listeners, we can tell the difference between a word that sounds abstract and one that is connected to meaning. We allow for this when we hear someone reading aloud something that isn’t theirs—the meeting minutes or a quote, perhaps. But we expect better when we’re listening to a human present their own ideas at a podium or into a lens. The social contract is that when you are in a position to speak in front of people, you own what you say.


In Amina’s case, she had come to me at the point in the process where the speech was still brand-new, still being written. Of course it didn’t live in her yet. There are two ways to go from there to a gorgeously delivered, lived-in speech. The first 
is time. If you sit with your words long enough, speak your speech out loud as you walk around your living room, visualize your audience, and breathe, you’ll likely get somewhere good, eventually.


The second way is more technical, but if you’ve never thought about language this way, it’s gold. As Kristin Linklater wrote, considering the earliest of human speech, “It is inconceivable that when the mouth first started to make words it did so in a manner divorced from its familiar exercises of chewing, biting, kissing, sucking, licking, snarling, lip smacking, and lapping. All these were practical activities, with sensual rewards and palpable side events of pleasure for most of them, and anger or fear for one or two. Words have a direct line from the nerve endings of the mouth to sensory and emotional storehouses in the body.” Not how we think about language today; maybe that direct line has been cut or short-circuited. Let’s link it back up.


As Kristin reminds us, words are sensual. They can play on our senses, if we let them. Try “thunder” out loud. Feel your tongue between your teeth, feel the air escaping above and below your tongue, like it’s blowing in the breeze. Think about hearing the rumble in the distance when you’ve been in a drought. Smell the rain. Smell the faint burn of lightning. Feel the walls shake. Feel wet clothes on your body. 
Thunder
.


Now try “catastrophic.” Or “honey.” Or “belong.” “Onomatopoeia” is the word that captures the concept of when sound and meaning merge. 
Drip, bang, bash.
 But really, all words have the potential to be onomatopoetic if you let them. And when you let a word like “belong” mean something to you—the two syllables emerging from you laced with how the soul feels when it finds its companions, the second syllable 
lengthened by yearning—you’ll hit your listener somewhere else. And yourself too. Our bodies respond to our own words when we use language well. We cue our words; our words cue us. We shape our words; our words shape us.


So, let’s warm up those articulators, aka the parts of your mouth that move and shape sound. Start with a little tour of your own mouth: touch your top teeth with your tongue, sliding the tip of your tongue against the bottom of those teeth. Next push against the inside of your top teeth with your tongue, and then farther up, feel with your tongue the ridge where your teeth connect to the gums, and where the roof of your mouth begins. Continue up, feeling the roof of your mouth—hard palate first with its little grooves, and then farther back the soft palate, the more slippery part that goes up when you yawn. Now push that tongue against the bottom teeth, and then ease into a more relaxed version, the 
y
 sound as in “yes.” Feel the middle of your tongue flatten and the back bunch up to create the 
y
. Now say “yesterday.” Notice if your lips head toward pursing for the 
ter
 and back out to a sort of smile for the “day.” Gently place a hand on your jaw and notice how it shifts from the beginning of the word to the end. Is it less movement than for the word “yawn”?


Vowels are the sounds we make when our mouth stays open and the air continues to flow out uninterrupted; consonants are the sounds we make when we shape our articulators to get in the way of that air. 
B, p, m, v, l, g, sh, th
 … Make those sounds while looking in a mirror if you want to see some shapes in action, and notice which articulators are moving to get you there. All bunches of words have both consonant and vowel sounds, so you’re constantly opening your mouth and then maneuver
ing your articulators into new positions and then opening your mouth again. It’s kind of incredible. Those speech muscles of yours, pulling and pushing your articulators into place rapidly throughout the day, do a lot of thankless work, so take a second to thank them.


Inevitably, however, your accent and personal speaking style have strengthened some muscles and let others atrophy, so after thanking them, drag ’em to the gym. Here’s an exercise you can do every day to counter the effects, or just before a big moment to loosen those speech muscles up: Start by yawning as big as possible, massaging your jaw joints on either side of your face with your fingertips, and then stretching out your tongue till you feel a pulling sensation at the back.
9
 Purse your lips and send them in a circle in one direction and then back in the other. Stretch out any facial muscles around the mouth or cheeks that seem to need extra love. Blow through your lips and trill them if you can; if you can’t, it usually means you’re still too tense around your lips. Take big, silly bites out of a fake apple and then try again. Now say “wah wah 
wee
 wah” five times slowly, and then as quickly but accurately as you can. (I’ve italicized here and below the sound that gets the emphasis in the phrase, so you can feel the rhythm.)


Now say the following, building up to doing both lines on a single breath:




puh puh 
pah
 // buh buh 
bah
 // tuh tuh 
tah
 // duh duh 
dah
 // kuh kuh 
kah
 // guh guh 
gah


guh guh 
gah
 // kuh kuh 
kah
 // duh duh 
dah
 // tuh tuh 
tah
 // buh buh 
bah
 // puh puh 
pah




You can tell that the first set of consonants rely on lips, second on tip of the tongue, and third on the soft palate—this warms up all of the above.




Try 
guh
 guh luh luh four times fast


And then 
luh
 luh guh guh four times fast.




Then back and forth as many times as you can before it gets super sloppy:




guh
 guh luh luh // 
luh
 luh guh guh // 
luh
 luh guh guh // 
guh
 guh luh luh, and so on.




For American English speakers, this one is surprisingly hard; our tongues need the workout.


Now say “The lips, the teeth, the tip of the tongue; the tip of the tongue, the teeth, the lips.”


Work your way up to being able to say it three times on one breath. Are you pronouncing the 
v
 sound in the word “of”? No judgment, but try it both ways.


Say “Fearless bears surely roar far.”


What’s happening with those 
r
’s at the end of the words? Depending on your accent, you may be using more muscle tension than you need (called a “hard 
r
”), or you may pronounce only an open vowel sound where the 
r
 is written (a feature common to dialects of England, Australia, New Zealand, South Africa, Boston, New York, and some of the American South). Experiment with how to say this phrase as easefully but clearly as possible. And especially if you pronounce a “hard 
r
” here, practice sending the energy out at those 
r
’s instead of 
letting it get stuck in your mouth. I don’t care about the sound itself, but I do care that you’re sharing it with us rather than keeping it in.


Speak the following (150-year-old!) poem by Arthur O’Shaughnessy, making sure you voice all the way to the end of each phrase. (Do all those plural 
s
’s in the spelling sound like 
z
’s coming out of your mouth?)




We are the music makers,


And we are the dreamers of dreams,


Wandering by lone sea-breaks


And sitting by desolate streams


World losers and world-forsakers,


Upon whom the pale moon gleams


Yet we are the movers and shakers


Of the world forever, it seems.




Did you notice where you breathed? Try it again, bunching each thought-bunch and punctuating it with a breath. Perhaps you breathe at the end of each line, or every other line. Fill just the room you’re in—not too loud or too soft.


Now consider what “music” means to you. And “dreamers” and “dreams.” The words “lone” and “desolate.” Consider what it would mean to forsake the world. Think about how the moon makes you feel. And the roguish delights of being a mover and shaker. You don’t have to overact here; this isn’t about doing bad children’s theater. But it is about letting the words evoke images and feelings, letting them mean something to you. (Here’s a silly example: You likely pronounce “bong” differently when you’re thinking of a church bell than when you’re 
thinking of 420 paraphernalia. The first gets more length, as you hold on the 
ng
 sound, and you’d never pronounce it that way when you’re at a dispensary. Meaning affects sound.) And as you do this work, alone, giving yourself permission to be playful, trust that when you speak the text aloud again, even in front of others, that work will linger. Please try this with any favorite bit of language, or maybe even something you wrote.


Now try saying something that isn’t written in advance. “My favorite part about my job is ___,” and see if this warm-up affects your spontaneous speech. Are you feeling the words? Are you still breathing? Are you still filling the room? What’s happening with your pitch?


Often, it’s phrases we say all the time—what we do for a living, or perhaps a logline for a script we’re trying to sell or an idea we’re talking to everyone about—that can accidentally get disconnected so the words coming out of our mouth stop feeling alive. Dulled by overuse, phrases that once sounded good, and felt good to say, now feel like nothing. Amina’s cold reading on the one hand and this speaker-fatigue on the other end up with similar results: if we haven’t gotten behind the thoughts or imagined the images or found the why, we won’t communicate well. But if we’ve forgotten the original thoughts that led to those words or lost the images or neglected our why, we won’t either.


If this happens, get practical. Throw new energy toward feeling the words and bring to mind images that relate to the words you’re saying. If it’s not the first time you’re birthing these words, it’s still the first time they’re hitting air 
here
. For 
these
 ears. Make the labor and the delivery count. Sweat a little. I can’t help but remember, as I write this, the week I pitched the proposal for this book—fourteen times over a 
three-day period, over Zoom since we were newly into the pandemic. I told the same stories and covered the same material, engaging with a new Brady Bunch of faces each time. But following my own precepts, I dared myself to invest in speaking like it was the first time every time because of course it was, for them. It felt exhausting and fucking great.


If you’re on the hook to deliver a brand-new speech, like Amina was, and you’re planning to memorize or read from a teleprompter, I want to help you prepare. Especially if you’re writing it yourself, you’re probably thinking about the what, the who, the why, and the in-what-order—and when that’s all sorted, it can feel like the job is done. But of course, it’s not: a beautiful speech doesn’t come alive on its own. Here’s a step-by-step process I’ve developed for lifting a speech from the page so it lives in you, and then lives outside of you.


I call it the Four 
S
’s. The order doesn’t matter so much, but doing each step justice does. I’ve used it with CEOs, politicos, and movie stars; it works. Actors call this sort of preparation “scoring,” which is a lovely reminder that, like music, when we notate and get analytical and practice, practice, practice, it’s so we don’t have to on the day; we can just play from the heart.




1. SENSE: Even if you wrote the speech yourself, what does every word and idiomatic expression mean? What does it 
really
 mean? Use a dictionary but then take the time, too, to sit with that word or phrase and make it personal. If English is your first language, it’s easy to skip over this step—but it’s essential. If we’re using a term of art, is it really, or is it jargon? Is it vague or is it specific? Check if what you’re saying is too formal or “scholarly” 
because you’re trying to play at something you’re not, or if it’s more like written speech than spoken speech. Check that all the words or phrases you’re preparing to say hold value for you. If not, swap them out for ones that do. We are not here to waste time and space. Hold yourself to a high standard of comprehension: I know what I’m saying, I know what it means, and it means something 
to me
.


2. SOUNDS: Practice speaking aloud phrases from your speech. Notice sounds that repeat, whether it’s on purpose or not, and feel what happens when you use those sounds in rapid succession. They may very well 
do
 something to you—affect your connection to the words, affect how you hold your body. Try Adele’s line “I can’t keep up with your turning tables.” Say it aloud. Feel the 
k
 sounds at the beginning and then the 
ur, ur
 and the 
t
’s near the end. Can you give the 
t
’s a little flick, like you’re pushing someone away with your words? Because English is so influenced by both Romance and Germanic languages, you can always explore which sounds feel round and gentle and which feel sharper. Like day and night language, both are useful. Both hold power. (There’s a reason our favorite swear words are almost all Germanic. The 
f
 and 
k
 of “fuck” are quite satisfying for our articulators. Bring some “fuck” articulator energy into the 
k
’s and 
t
’s in the Adele line above and see if that evokes something.) Do this work out loud with everything you’re preparing to say and let yourself be surprised. Stand up and walk around and experience the version of speaking sounds, words, whole thoughts, where your entire body comes along for the 
ride. Trust that your words won’t become stale or tepid with overuse as long as you decide to bring the heat when the time comes to deliver them. And while you’re here in this step, make sure you can pronounce everything with confidence, including the name of your host or the institution you’re speaking at, so there’s no question mark in your voice. Otherwise, consciously or not, you will attempt to hide when you get to that word: you’ll mumble or get quiet, or inadvertently cover your mouth or your heart. In order to own your words, you must know what they mean 
and
 feel good saying them out loud—and I mean it. If you have a regional or English-as-a-second-language accent and you worry you won’t be understood, ask someone in your life who you trust will be constructive, and then listen to how they say it. Practice slowly. Make an audio recording and listen back. There is no magic trick to linking unusual sounds together except saying them till your mouth muscles learn the movements and know the dance—and quieting your critic mind so your mouth can do the work it was made for.


3. STRESS: If words don’t all matter equally, which stand out? Good public speaking requires that you 
do something
 to make each word that matters pop. Almost all spoken communication revolves around what words get emphasized; we just do it so much we take it for granted. Here we do it on purpose.


Try this: “I never said she stole my money.” But say it out loud as flatly as possible, trying not to indicate emphasis at all, on any of the words. Try again, but even less. 
It’ll probably sound like a vague negation, or not much of anything. Each word in the sentence has an agreed-upon meaning, but strung together and delivered flatly the collective meaning seems just out of reach. Like, are you being defensive? Trying to set the record straight? Avoiding a confrontation? Or are you asking for one? Is this the beginning of an argument or the end of one?


Pitch variation is complicated (see 
Chapter 4
!), but bottom line: flat speech reads as confusing. Even if you have a point of view about what you’re saying, 
you’ll sound like you don’t.
 Helpful if your point of view will incite a conflict you’re desperately trying to avoid. Deeply problematic if you’re going for something bold.


That nebulous phrase about stealing money takes on clearer meaning if we try again and this time emphasize the “she”: I never said 
she
 stole my money. (Oh, so someone else did it? Cheeky.) Or if we emphasize the “stole”: I never said she 
stole
 my money. (Ah, so she acquired it fair and square? Call off the search party.) Or if we emphasize the “my”: I never said she stole 
my
 money. (Wait, then who’s the victim here?) Or if we emphasize the “money”: I never said she stole my 
money
. (Well, now the plot’s really thickened. What’s actually gone missing?) Linguists like to use this sentence because it’s a pretty straightforward example of the power of emphasis, but the reality is that nearly every thought we utter aloud in English works like this—or rather, it can, if we don’t let it die a droopy, inconsequential death by flat delivery. The words that matter are usually the new information words, or the words that challenge a previous statement or assumption. (“Why? Why 
not
?”) They’re technically called the opera
tive words because they operate the whole thought. In writing we can italicize; what’s the out-loud equivalent?


If you play a musical instrument, the answer will sound familiar: volume, tempo, tone, and pitch. You can get louder, faster, funnier, or higher on any given word. Or their opposites. You do this in real life all the time: away from a podium, each of us (with variations for accent or native language) stresses words based almost exclusively on meaning. Practice doing it with your prepared speech. Play.


4. SENTIMENT: If you’ve done the first three steps, this one should already be happening, but I want to be explicit: let your body feel things while you speak. Let it respond to the sense and the sounds and the rhythm of stressed words. Even if your talk is about third-quarter earnings. Even if it’s a pitch to take on a project you aren’t sure if you care about yet. Find what you care about and say it like you care about it—animating your language using the three steps above. This final step is a reminder that emotions are good and to use them with purpose. This is a reminder that caring is more powerful than the power move of seeming blasé. This is a reminder that your point of view matters. What do you know for sure? What do you want? How does it make you feel to express it?




Sense, sounds, stress, and sentiment. You might very well find something fresh in the moment when you’re in front of your audience, picking up nonverbal feedback from them. But trust that if you do the Four S’s in advance you won’t have to think this technically when you’re delivering your speech.


Hot tip: Look back at the section of Michelle Obama’s speech I included earlier in this chapter. You’ll see I italicized the operative words. But now look at the final thought-bunch. “And you see 
that
 is why I 
love
 this country.” Check out the emphasis. Her statement would mean something quite different if she had said “and you see that is why 
I
 love this country.” It would have implied she was comparing herself to someone else. But instead, she emphasized “that” and “love”—suggesting that her love of country was in question. Indeed, as she describes in her memoir, 
Becoming,
 she delivered this speech during an excruciating moment when previous words she’d spoken had been misinterpreted and her criticisms of America were dominating the news, endangering the narrative around both her and her husband.
10
 Here, on this stage, she doesn’t reference the media flurry directly. She goes high. But with the emphasis of a few key words, she references it indirectly. She challenges assumptions. And if you watch the video you’ll see the crowd cheer, and the cheer build as the 20,000 audience members connect the dots. They are with her. The accusations she’s fighting against hang in the space between them, unspoken, thanks to her subtle use of emphasis. The mark of sophisticated, nuanced communication among people with any shared history at all, even shared cultural history among strangers, is that it’s full of implications. Once we’re conscious of this, we, like Michelle Obama, can use it.


Watch, too, a few minutes of soccer star Abby Wambach’s famous 2018 Barnard commencement speech online and see if you can underline the operative words—not just the most fun words, but the ones that supply new information and move the argument forward. Abby begins a section of her speech by telling us that “like all little girls, I was taught to be grateful. I was 
taught to keep my head down, stay on the path, and get my job done. I was freaking Little Red Riding Hood,”
11
 which she refers to as “just one iteration of the warning stories girls are told the world over.” Then she relates it to her own life:




I stayed on the path out of fear, not of being eaten by a wolf, but of being cut, being benched, losing my paycheck. If I could go back and tell my younger self one thing it would be this: “Abby, you were never Little Red Riding Hood; you were always the wolf.”




Did you catch the contrasting words “never” versus “always,” and “Little Red Riding Hood” versus “the wolf”? These sorts of word-pairs are called antitheses, and you can find these types of opposing ideas everywhere we communicate; our brains often organize around pairs, whether we notice it or not (see?). But how would you say those words out loud to make sure your audience 
feels
 the opposition?


And, no, you definitely don’t need to be Abby or Michelle to pull all this off. Once Amina got the hang of this, she suddenly sounded like what she was saying was important, and important to her. Operative-word work helped her remember her why, propelling her from important idea to important idea. She found her fire. She lit up. And she was what I lovingly call “a person” up there, rather than that robotic alternative. And she did win her election for city council; in fact, now she’s the mayor. How’s that for antithesis?


I hope the work above helps you discover the version of yourself that uses language and owns it, but I also hope that the occasions where you have to stick to an exact script are rare. I 
hope you get to use language 
and
 think on your feet, surprising yourself with how you articulate your thoughts in the moment, smiling at the chimera and letting her rest in some cave far away from your spotlight. I hope you’re ready to honor your ideas enough that they matter more than the words.


When my friend Stephanie Ybarra took over as the artistic director of Baltimore Center Stage, she was the first woman of Latin descent to run a major American theater company. When you hear her speak, she sounds like a leader. She sounds exciting, and fresh, and real. And like herself.


I asked her how she prepares to speak in front of a crowd, and she told me that she learned a lot from observing her old boss, a charismatic man whose remarks she would prepare. Although he’d often go in with material he’d already said at hundreds of functions, he would electrify a crowd (when she told me this, she mimed everyone taking their shirts off like he was a rock star with feverish groupies). “It’s not what he’s saying,” she recalls thinking, “because I’ve heard that shit over and over—it’s the same message. But how he says it sounds authentic, it sounds off-the-cuff every time he says it.” So she practiced. For years. “First I started with a script, and then I went down to bullet points, typed. And then bullet points, handwritten. And now, legitimately—including at my own father’s funeral—bullet points on the back of my hand.”


I asked her if she ever feared being inarticulate if she’s only relying on bullet points, or if she ever dreaded losing a word mid-thought. She nodded in recognition, then replied thoughtfully, “At this point, I’ve gotten so much good feedback about the authenticity of my public-speaking style that I feel much more comfortable taking a pause or struggling in plain sight. There’s something about that … people appear to really dig.”


Good public speaking is simply not the same as pulling off pre-programmed automaton delivery. We dream of making no mistakes, dropping no “uh”s, finding the perfect turn of phrase at the perfect moment to come across as more articulate than we’ve ever been before. It’s probably Winston Churchill’s fault. Or those old standards erected in his image that put us at a disadvantage and make us wish to inoculate ourselves against mistakes. But struggling in plain sight is a gift to your audience, and authentic is more important than articulate. We want you up there; the operative word is “you.”

















8


Heroism




From my point of view, which is that of a storyteller, I see your life as already artful, waiting, just waiting, and ready for you to make it art.


—Toni Morrison
, Wellesley commencement address, 2004
1




It started with a meme. I was scrolling aimlessly, needing an escape; it was summer 2018 and stories in the news were so much. A callousness I hadn’t totally believed was possible now seemed too rampant to contain. I got back to my hotel room after wandering Washington, D.C., on a day off from filming the 
Wonder Woman
 sequel and thought, 
Dear lord, we need a real-life hero.
 I had joined protests and planning meetings and helped raise funds, and saw how exhausted everyone was, how despairing, like our empathy meters fritzed out at 10, and I worried what would happen if the helpers burned out. My own body was brittle and overtired and I was constantly distracted by new pains. A friend told me my light had gone out—and you can’t unhear something like that.


Then I saw this meme on my phone, and just stared at it, releasing a shaky sigh. It was a Venn diagram made with two simple circles. One said, “what breaks your heart most about the world,” and the other, “where your skill set lies.” The overlap, according to this anonymous image, is where we’re called 
to serve. I’d seen versions of this idea before—it’s the Japanese concept of 
ikigai,
 meaning life’s purpose or raison d’être. There’s a famous line sometimes attributed to Aristotle about finding your vocation where your talents and the needs of the world cross. But I needed to be told specifically that my heartbreak held meaning, that it was pointing me in a direction. And I needed the assignment: What exactly broke my heart 
most
?


I didn’t know it then, but at the same time, the progressive organization MoveOn.org was looking to pair each of their chosen candidates—a sea of mostly women, mostly running for the first time—with a coach to improve both the content and delivery of their stump speeches. When I got back to L.A., a friend called to ask if he could refer me over to them. I believe my response was a squeal.


Over Zoom, on an August afternoon, I settled into an onboarding session with two fellow coaches who’d already started working with these fledgling candidates. They told me about the biggest problem they were seeing so far, besides nerves and general public-speaking anxiety. Turns out, these newbies all had a habit of skipping over their origin story—not where they were born and raised, not that kind of origin, a coach named Monte explained. But the moment they had realized that 
they
 could represent their neighbors—that they might be the hero they’d been waiting for. Were they in the shower when it happened? Were they having a conversation inside their head? What was it? What was the struggle? What were they up against? What had they realized mattered most in that moment of decision? How had they come to: 
Fuck it—I’m in.


Yes, I thought, listening to Monte. This makes so much sense. Sharing your aha moment offers a singular chance to reveal your character to your would-be constituents. 
Here’s 
where I’m from
 holds little power compared to 
Here’s what I did in an impossible moment.
 It was the first time I had heard the term “origin story” used this way—if anything, I’d associated it with superheroes and bloody crimes in dark alleys or lab accidents gone wrong. But this conversation was, quite unexpectedly and absent a single radioactive spider, my own origin story. My own aha. It completely changed my life.


I will tell you what I mean. But first, I want to talk about you. There are two questions that come up more than any other when coaching. The first is “How do I handle nerves?” And the second is some variation on “I’m so bad at talking about myself—how do I get better at telling stories from my life and promoting and all that gross me-me-me stuff?” It’s the same two issues those candidates faced in going public for the first time. Maybe it’s the same two issues you struggle with too.


They seem like opposites. Nerves tend to hit when we accidentally make it too much about ourselves and we obsess over how we’ll come across or how we’ll do. A resistance to the me-me-me stuff stems from not making it 
enough
 about ourselves, from downplaying our accomplishments or dismissing our stories because we think they’re too insignificant. If you’ve gotten this far in the book, you won’t be surprised to hear my diagnosis: I smell patriarchy.


I believe both impulses come from a lifetime of learned insecurity around claiming our full membership in public life. With nerves, it’s a matter of feeling as though you’re not worthy of being seen and heard. In telling your own story, it’s … a matter of feeling as though you’re not worthy of being seen and heard. If you’re on a stage or in an office presenting your project, there’s a good chance you’ve earned your way in—but there’s also a good chance you don’t entirely believe it. Maybe 
these impulses take the form of a taunting voice in your head that says, 
You’re not ready
 or 
You’re no expert
 or 
You’re not good/interesting/important enough
 or 
Your story is boring
 or 
No one wants to hear about you
 or 
Who are you to …?
 I hear them in my head. My clients hear them. Those candidates, brilliant and worthy future leaders all, they heard them. This is what breaks my heart about the world—because it keeps those who deserve power out of power. And this, it turns out, is also where my skill set lies.


Maybe because I’m not only a coach—I’m also client zero. Here’s a Samara quirk: Most of the time I’m a grade-A storyteller. With friends, and even with relative strangers if I’ve been able to get a read on them, I 100 percent trust that my anecdotes will kill. In response to someone else’s share, in dialogue, in context, organic tales just flow out of me. But if I can’t read the room or I’m speaking into a void (ahem, badly orchestrated Zoom webinars), suddenly nothing feels organic. I shrivel up. I go blank. I can’t remember anything that’s ever happened to me. I second-guess stories that I already know work. 
Pretty sure you’re a total weirdo,
 I think to myself, 
and your stories are irrelevant. Nobody wants to hear from you.


The best explanation I’ve heard for this (I’m just going to say it) dumb voice in my head comes from Dr. Carol Gilligan.
2
 She’s a psychologist and ethicist who wrote the groundbreaking 1982 bestseller 
In a Different Voice,
 and is now in her eighties with Woodstock hair and a rebel spirit like the inextinguishable Olympic torch. When Dr. Gilligan was a young researcher at Harvard with only male mentors, she discovered that all the studies about adolescence focused on boys, as though their experience was not just the default but the sole experience of growing up—and no one seemed to notice. As 
she writes in her follow-up book, 
Joining the Resistance,
 “Psychologists had assumed a culture in which men were the measure of humanity.”
3
 She regularly observed “so-called normal, everyday conversations in which men spoke as if the omission of women was irrelevant or inconsequential and women overlooked or excused the omission of themselves.”


And so she dug deep into the experience of female adolescence, interviewing girls about the decisions they were confronting before and during puberty, listening to their answers, and charting, among other things, when the 
I-knows
 of childhood morphed into the 
I-don’t-knows
 of their preteen years. She discovered that she could track when the language girls used seemed to reveal their growing sense of obligation to everyone but themselves. In other words, she could hear their inner turmoil at becoming or resisting becoming a “good girl.” She named the process their “initiation into patriarchy.” And it generally happened around age eleven or twelve. In 
Joining the Resistance
 she shares eye-opening transcripts of her subjects, full of vocal hedges and fragmented thoughts, like this from a thirteen-year-old named Judy who was asked why she didn’t want to talk about her family life:




I don’t know, because I don’t—I don’t know. I mean, I do know. It’s just like—I can’t explain—I don’t know what, how to put it into words …. I don’t even know if I know what it is. So I can’t really explain it. Because I don’t know. I don’t even know like in my brain or in my heart what I am really feeling.




I wonder if this feels familiar. A friend recently left me a message saying, “I feel as though my brain receptors for figur
ing out what I want to do and how I want to do it and when I want to do it—it, it, they’re all broken. They’re broken in my head somehow—like even when I have a quiet moment to think, I still can’t think.” Dr. Gilligan says this initiation is “driven by gender and enforced by shaming and exclusion. Its telltale signs are a loss of voice and memory, an inability to tell one’s story accurately.” Well, that’s real real. The things that should belong to us most—our own memories, our own life—feel increasingly hard to hold on to the more we become “good.”


As Dr. Gilligan and her colleagues listened to girls in the thick of this transition, they heard humans who had found through trial and error that “they must silence their honest voices in order to be accepted and loved.” Boys get the message to choose independence over relationships, and so many boys suffer from loneliness as a result; girls get the message to choose their relationships over all things and compromise their connection with their own inner voice as a result. Their inner knowing becomes harder to know, and, conveniently, ignoring it is rewarded. My friend Liz Kimball posted on Instagram after Simone Biles dropped out of the Olympics, asking us to consider “How old were you the first time your body or heart or mind said no and you pushed through anyway, because that’s what the culture that raised you taught you to do?”


Maybe these days this means you suck it up and don’t insist on credit for something you’re proud of because it’ll bruise egos that are used to coddling. Or you muscle through when you should rest. Or you’re paralyzed over whether to go on a trip with friends because you’re so used to thinking about your kids’ needs, you have no idea how to interpret your own. Or you don’t share good news because you don’t want to brag and 
make others feel bad—in fact, you may not even feel all that good about the good news, linked as it is with the prospect of jeopardizing relationships. We want to be accepted and loved at all costs. We put ourselves last and it almost feels good, because it makes so many other people happy. We, not all of us but many of us, have raised people-pleasing to an art form and, as Dr. Gilligan reminds us, it’s not our fault. If we wind up as perfectionists and people-pleasers it’s thanks to “the crusades of a culture profiting from your not enough-ness,” says Kimball.


All of which is terrible for our public-speaking skills. We must get up there feeling like we are enough—more than enough—that we are there to save the day. We must get up there feeling like a bona fide hero, in fact. The hero they’ve all been waiting for. Which requires that we face down a lifetime of muffling our inner voice, “good girl” demurring, and internalizing insidious messages that it’s virtuous to avoid being the center of attention. Because it turns out the solution to overcoming nerves 
and
 the solution to not feeling good talking about ourselves is actually the same thing: the secret is to focus on helping our audience.


The secret is to make it about them.


Sounds like people-pleasing, right? But there’s an invaluable distinction between offering help at your own expense to accommodate others and offering help from your bounty because you’ve got what they need and it feels great to give. One depletes you; the other fills you back up. One makes you feel like a martyr; the other, a hero. Speaking publicly is an act of service, but it’s only sustainable if you trust that what you’re serving up is absolutely fantastic. Then, you get back as much as you give.


“Make it about them” forces each of us to reckon with how much we actually believe that what we’re serving up is fantastic. How much we believe we are of worth. But let it be known, even just deciding as a bit of a thought experiment that you’re there to help and that you’ll succeed has the power to hack your nervous system, reorganize your mind, and rejigger your body’s chemical cocktail for less inhibition and more permission. Here’s the deal: according to evolutionary scientists, we have a long history of interpreting eyes on us as a threat. Prehistoric humans literally registered other people watching them as predators, and thought, 
Danger! Danger!,
 triggering a corresponding physiological response: shallow breathing, flushed face, higher-level brain functions short-circuiting in order to fight, fly, or freeze instead.


“So today,” says Sarah Gershman of Georgetown’s business school, writing in the 
Harvard Business Review,
 “when we speak in front of a group and feel the eyes watching us, we feel painfully visible, like a caveman exposed in daylight,”
4
 and we do whatever we must to protect ourselves. But since running off the stage isn’t socially acceptable, instead “we construct walls between ourselves and the source of danger—in this case, the audience—to repel the attack and blunt any danger.” I’ve seen this over and over. One such wall is not making eye contact and relying too heavily on your slides or your notes. Another is flattening your voice, so you hide what you’re feeling but also end up hiding everything else: your pitch and tone, your unique musicality, your ability to express yourself. You find ways to be there, but not really be there.


But if you consciously shift to thinking, 
I’m here to help my audience,
 you can disarm the panic button. Sounds tiny, right? But when we choose to look out at our audience with kindness 
and offer what we’ve got with generosity, we actually trigger an entirely different physiological response. The walls come down and we free ourselves to be ourselves. Which is not tiny at all.


Stop and think about what it feels like to show up for a friend who’s going through it. When you see them in pain, you might jump in and share some parallel from your life. I’ve asked roomfuls of women in workshops: “Who here hates talking about themselves?” and all hands go up. But we talk about ourselves fluently when we sense that there’s a clear outcome it’ll achieve; if sharing what we went through will put a friend’s tough day in perspective, ease their suffering, or help solve their problem, we do it without another thought. I bet you wouldn’t hesitate to mention your credentials if they were directly applicable: “I actually have a background in child psychology” or “I was a professional dancer” or “I’m certified in CPR.” It’d feel ridiculous to hold back if someone were choking in front of you. It isn’t bragging; it’s generosity. It isn’t about you, it’s about 
them
. In my online parent community, we save one another’s asses every day, whether it’s via threads about raising little humans or negotiating tricky career moves or insisting that our partners partner better. Reading and responding is a constant opportunity to flex this particular muscle: What have I got that could be of use?


We see someone in need; we help. This is a generalization, of course, and plays on tropes of women as nurturers that Dr. Gilligan enumerates. But there’s good reason for us to participate in this exchange of kindness, beyond putting others first: when we help someone, the gratification is almost instantaneous. Our friend cheers up or we get a Like, our bond is strengthened, and dopamine releases in our brain. Suddenly we’re actually a little high. We likely get a hit of oxytocin, the 
“love hormone” that makes us feel cuddly and boosts trust but that also helps our hearts: it turns out oxytocin triggers our bodies to release the chemical nitric oxide into the bloodstream, which reduces blood pressure and gives our hearts a break. We see someone in need; we help; it pays off. What’s not to love?


And it gets better: studies show oxytocin dampens the fight-flight-freeze response. “Oxytocin, the compound known as the love hormone and the cuddle chemical, deserves a new nickname,”
5
 announces 
LiveScience,
 “the fear fighter.” Oxytocin heads right for the amygdala, the fear center of our brains, and tells that killjoy to chill—in fact, it may be an evolutionary feature especially designed for moms. Science journalist Luke Yoquinto explains that “the brain speedily delivers the hormone oxytocin—which new mothers have in elevated levels, starting with childbirth—to where it’s needed, freeing them to protect their young.”
6
 But men are perfectly capable of getting that oxy high as well when they offer help, according to an international study by Drs. Nina Marsh, Dirk Scheele, and their colleagues in the 
Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences
.
7
 They may just not be incentivized to pursue it. (In the dad version of that online parenting group, I’m given on good authority, they mostly just try to outdo one another posting funny memes. I am telling you, the patriarchy helps no one.)


The trick to advocating for yourself and your most precious ideas when you’re speaking in public is to scale up this exchange—the need, the helping, the hormone hit—even if you don’t see their pain. You can use all the other tools in this book to prepare for that moment, but they won’t settle the chaos within or allow your voice to ring out without this final tool. Step into that room with the intention to help. Make it about 
them
.


My favorite TED Talk is “The Art of Being Yourself,” and in it Caroline McHugh, a Scotswoman with mischief in her eye and an international coaching business teaching individuality, tells the story of feeling shy as a young girl as she prepared to perform for a family gathering. She remembers her mother giving her a no-nonsense nudge, saying the job is to entertain; no one’s interested in her. It left Caroline with, as she puts it, spectacular disregard for being the center of attention. “Since then, I’ve 
never
 been the center of attention,”
8
 she says from the stage of TED. “You’re the center of mine.”


You know that thing where it’s easier to get a job when you don’t really want it? “Care less” seems like an obvious takeaway, but that’s not quite it. Rather, when we aren’t desperate to prove ourselves, we’re outward-focused, we’re seeing and hearing, which ironically helps us be better seen and heard. Public speaking is fundamentally about being looked at and heard, but 
it is also fundamentally about looking and hearing.
 When you feel yourself slipping into nerves or starting to bail on telling a story, feel the floor under your feet, smell the room, hear the breathing, look at the people you’re there to help. Make the others in the room the center of your attention. And if the faces are inscrutable, if the room is intimidating, and it seems ridiculous to think that they could possibly need your help, think, 
What if they’re choking and they’re just hiding it well?
 If you decided to believe it, how many systems of power would crumble? For that reason alone, you might as well try it.


“But where’s the evidence?” the dumb voice in your head will heckle you. You know what, give that voice a name. Maybe name it after your least-favorite person—maybe it’s that guy who represents everything that’s wrong with the world—and 
then practice, even just for a day, calling him out whenever you hear the voice. “Dude, no. Just no. You don’t have my best interest at heart.” I know this may be easier said than done; but it is also easier done than we sometimes think. We help our friends when they need us by revealing our own vulnerabilities, so they know they’re not alone, and by celebrating our resilience, so they remember their own. We help by telling our stories for practical reasons: it’s the surest way to make our point. We let ourselves be seen and heard 
because it will help
. Our public deserves this too.


Maybe heroism is the problem. We can’t very well step onto the stage as our audience’s hero if the concept feels outdated or made for other people. We can’t tell stories from our own life like we’re the hero in them if heroic stories are the stuff of legend and 
Star Wars
. Whether you’ve read Joseph Campbell or not you’ve likely got a sense of the classic structure of the “hero’s journey”: it’s epic; it’s a test that often requires physical force in order to pass; it’s a thing men go on. A client named Parker, who was embroiled in an ugly lawsuit because of how she was fired, first told me the story of what went down 
from her former boss’s point of view
 rather than from her own. I recognized it at once because I’ve seen it before; often we can’t narrate our own journeys because we can’t recognize them as such. We can’t tell our story accurately. It’s the same reason we seem to like high-powered women more when they have a job than when they’re publicly vying for a better one, a statistic that’s trotted out every time a woman runs for president. The act of vying puts her on a quest, and we don’t know how to compute that. Men go on quests; women … support? Wait at home? Tempt them off course?


Well, we’re long overdue on blowing up that paradigm. And the solution isn’t just 
Wonder Woman
. Heroism, bravery, and doing good against the odds belong to all of us. The hero’s journey has nothing to do with gender and nothing to do with the size of the journey either. It can be as tiny as an aha moment in the shower. In his Nobel lecture in 2017, novelist Kazuo Ishiguro described a theme he noticed around important turning points in his life: “Often, they are small, scruffy moments. They are quiet, private sparks of revelation.”
1
 And does the act of moving through one of these moments with grace qualify as heroism? Well, what do you consider heroic? Does it require muscle and glory? Or is it listening closely to your inner knowing and doing the next right thing? Dare we call that heroism too?


Maybe we balk at casting ourselves as heroes because good-girl conditioning allows little space for pride in our accomplishments, but maybe it’s because on some level we’re suspicious of heroes and the source of their pride. As Liz Phair sings, “He’s just a hero in a long line of heroes / looking for something attractive to save. / They say he rode in on the back of a pickup / and he won’t leave town till you remember his name.”
10
 This kind of heroism is masculine-coded. It’s about vanity and violence and promoting yourself over the group. It’s about fame for the sake of fame.


Maybe Hollywood is the problem. As actor and writer Brit Marling put it in “I Don’t Want to Be the Strong Female Lead,” an essay my friends sent around like a whisper network when it hit 
The New York Times
 in early 2020, “There are centuries of trial and error inside the ‘hero’s journey,’ in which a young man is called to adventure, challenged by trials, faces a climactic battle and emerges victorious, changed and a hero. 
And while there are narrative patterns for the adventures of girls—‘Alice in Wonderland,’ ‘The Wizard of Oz’—those are few and far between, and for adult women, even less so.”
11
 As Jia Tolentino says in 
Trick Mirror,
 surveying the whole canon of Western literature, stories about girls growing up portray them from “brave to blank to bitter,” that is, if—and this is a big if—they survive to adulthood at all. Marling faced a career playing Hollywood’s shiny new solution to telling stories about adult women: the “Strong Female Lead.” And she rejected it. “It would be hard to deny that there is nutrition to be drawn from any narrative that gives women agency and voice in a world,” she writes. “But the more I acted the Strong Female Lead, the more I became aware of the narrow specificity of the characters’ strengths—physical prowess, linear ambition, focused rationality.” Why mess with the traditional hero’s journey when you can just recast it with a lady and put her in a sexy tank top?


God knows, I’ve coached on enough scripts the world doesn’t need. The gatekeepers who green-light projects in Tinsel Town only know how to tell these sorts of hero’s-journey stories, Rebecca Solnit suggests in her 2019 essay, “When the Hero Is the Problem,” and so inevitably they’re the only types of tales we hear over and over. “The standard action movie narrative,” she says, “requires one exceptional person in the foreground, which requires the rest of the characters to be on the spectrum from useless to clueless to wicked.” If we don’t recognize ourselves in the heroes, we’re relegated to identifying with one of those other categories, which doesn’t exactly set us up for success. Maybe, muses Solnit, the real problem is that 
communal
 heroism, the feminine-coded kind, that functions not by putting individuals on pedestals but by distributing 
power more evenly, entails sitting through a lot of long meetings, and meetings don’t play well onscreen.


Quite a bit of what I consider heroism would make for unconventional movie fodder—building consensus in a meeting, making a small decision with a ripple effect, standing up for someone, changing your mind once you know better, choosing to talk about your shame rather than stay silent, taking responsibility for something hard to own—but these are all excellent bases for stories we can each tell, if we let ourselves.


When U.S. congresswoman Ayanna Pressley’s hair began to fall out a year into her term, she held the secret close, afraid to go to sleep at night because she knew she’d find chunks of hair on her pillow in the morning. She questioned the vanity of just how acutely our hair feels like it defines us, but she felt ashamed nonetheless. And then she decided to make a bold move: to trust that a story of hair loss is a story worth telling. She made a video for 
The Root
—and looking right into the lens, without quick edits or fancy production, she simply spoke. She spoke of the joy of finding her look on the campaign trail (Senegalese braids, and the cultural implications therein—as she says “hair is political”)
12
, and of her rapid descent into baldness, the despair that accompanied the diagnosis of alopecia, the relief of knowing, and her still-raw vulnerability as she attempted to make peace with her smooth head.


If you watch the video, you’ll see her unscripted, thinking aloud on camera, finding her words in the moment, and holding herself accountable for saying true things. You’ll see her tell the story, beginning, middle, and end-ish, whatever she’d call where she is now in the journey. You’ll see that she lets us see her and hear her. And not just that: she makes it about us. 
She releases her own private shame and offers us a chance to release our own. “I’m not here just to occupy space,” she says in the video, “I’m here to create it.” This is how we tell our stories. This is heroism. And this is the new sound of power.


“They must silence their honest voices in order to be accepted and loved,” Dr. Gilligan had observed of her female subjects. This speaking-honestly-versus-being-loved thing is tricky in our personal lives—I don’t want to dismiss how tender that is, the tiny concessions we make to keep the peace at home, or white lies we tell to protect those we love, and the cost if we didn’t. Perhaps it’s even tricky in your professional life, if your job requires that you do things that don’t sit right with you. But as evidenced by the 
Little Mermaid
 myth, in the long run trading your voice for love is a crap bargain. When you get to do work that matters to you, presenting in public or pitching what’s close to your heart, your honest voice is the very thing that will make an audience love you. We in your audience know Instagram filters and ghostwriters and party lines; we expect a disconnected voice speaking pleasant words that uphold every status quo there is. As Priya Parker says in 
The Art of Gathering,
 “we spend much of our time in uninspiring, underwhelming moments that fail to capture us, change us in any way, or connect us to one another.”


Against that expectation, speaking truth is power. It captures us, changes us, connects us. I think of X González, taking the mic three days after seventeen of their classmates were gunned down at their high school, with their shaved head and fresh grief. I think about them speaking unapologetically, directly calling out the media and politicians, repeating, “We call BS.” X brought unvarnished truth, impolite and unpretty and forged in fire. They got pushback and hate. They were mis
taken by no one for a “good girl.” But they also became a symbol of heroism around the country, for saying something true when they could have said something pretty.


Another example is Alicia Keys hosting the 2020 Grammys like she’d invited all 16.5 million of us into her living room. Standing at her keyboard about to launch into a parody song she’d written for the occasion, she made eye contact with the crowd and spoke at full volume into her mic, “And can I also have a little more piano in my ears, please, so I can properly serenade the people.” She could have gone for pretty, too, and tried to pretend she didn’t need something she needed to make her presentation better. But she said what was true instead. And it endeared her audience to her even more. (A recovered people-pleaser, it took years, she says in her memoir, 
More Myself,
 to start asking herself, “What’s in it for me?”)


As a friend in a twelve-step program shared with me about all the stories she’s told and heard in church basements and social halls: if it’s not true it’s not useful. And this is the point—
this
 is what we must practice if we want to tell our stories accurately. It’s what works for me when I go blank. We must believe that we tell stories 
to be of use.
 When we talk about ourselves and our life, it feels like me-me-me. But it’s really you-you-you. And it’s 
really
 us-us-us. Anthropologists say that storytelling is central to human existence and a feature of every known culture on Earth. According to 
The Art of Immersion
 author Frank Rose, it involves “a symbiotic exchange between teller and listener—an exchange we learn to negotiate in infancy.”
13
 That exchange can be anything from pure entertainment to lesson to laugh to feeling less alone. But what it’s not is one-sided. What it’s not, as long as we intend to be useful, is irrelevant.


Casting yourself as the hero in your stories and then sharing them is a revolution you can join just by opening your mouth. When we tell these sorts of stories instead of glossing over them—the quiet ones, the collaborative ones, the ones about topics that don’t show up in ancient mythology or even in 
The Wizard of Oz,
 the ones in boring hotel rooms without flashy endings—we give audiences the chance to hear them, and that changes everyone’s relationship to their own brand of heroism. Like Ilyse Hogue suggested, the most important preparation we can do to speak in public is to be proud of our life. And if we acknowledge the life we’ve actually lived, the setbacks and mistakes, what was our fault and what wasn’t, if we take the time to own all the parts, then we can turn them into offerings.


What stories can you tell? First, help yourself out by thinking through what your audience may need. Research who you’re speaking to in advance. Collect demographic data if it’s a big group or read press if it’s someone with an online presence. Ask mentors, consider trends in the industry, brainstorm with a friend about what help your customers or investors or future constituents might really need to make their lives better. Consider what car they drive or subway they take, what potholes they pass on their way to work, what hurts and what they’re proud of. Imagine what they read and watch and love and laugh at. Fantasize enough that they no longer feel like strangers.


You can also begin by asking them questions once you’re there—questions you really want the answers to. I help clients build this into their presentations all the time and do this at the beginning of my own workshops, too, for a few reasons: to gauge my audience, to encourage them to participate and hear their own voice out loud, and to hear one another. “Why did 
you come to a workshop about using your voice?” They give me a few answers, and voilà, my talk is a dialogue. A question I offered a client who was running for office in a district roiled with corruption: “Show of hands. Who here trusts that your elected official has your best interests at heart? Okay, real question—who here wants to?”


As you consider your stories and what your audience needs, it can be liberating to use a simple structure to start. Sarah Hurwitz, first Barack Obama’s and then Michelle Obama’s speechwriter during all eight years in the White House, shared with me a handy way to slide into any presentation: Start with gratitude. As Sarah says, “Thank you so much to this person and that person …” And then tie that impulse into celebrating the audience’s story. “And most of all, thanks to all of you.”


Sarah told me that that’s not enough, though. It’s important to say something truly meaningful about your audience. “Like if Mrs. Obama is speaking to a bunch of nurses, she’ll say, ‘You guys are extraordinary. Here’s what you’re doing every day.’ And it’s really important to show in those moments. Everyone always says show, don’t tell—no one does it!” Do not rattle off a long list of pretty adjectives. As she put it with a dollop of tough love, “Blah-blah-blah, no one cares. What’s better is: ‘You know what? Right now, in this pandemic, you’re the one sitting by the bedside of people gasping to breathe, holding their hand, holding up an iPad so they can speak to their loved ones. You’re the one commuting an hour a day, risking your own life, taking the train in.’ … You’ve got to really make it so we can see it.” This is showing the audience that you understand their need. This will make whatever comes next feel relevant 
to them
.


Finally, answer, so why are we here today? Sarah says it may 
well be “because I care about the work you’re doing, and here’s how I want to support you ….” Maybe you even have a story about nurses, in this case, and you should definitely tell it. I asked Sarah how Michelle Obama would figure out her specific offer for the specific room when it wasn’t so clear. She said, begin by asking yourself, 
What is the truest thing I can say here?
 Below are some more questions I find generative, as you prepare:




What do I admire about my audience that I want to make sure they know?


What do I think they need? Do they know it or not?


What’s my favorite thing to talk about here?


What lights me up?


What makes me laugh or feel playful?


What would breaking the rules a little bit mean?


What would breaking the rules a lot mean?


What is the truest thing I could say if I had unlimited courage?


What do I know for sure?


What do I wonder?


What am I curious about that I can only learn from my audience?




And it always helps to ask, What am I offering? Okay, and what am I 
really
 offering? When I began the proposal for this book, the offer was a treatise on public speaking from a feminist perspective. But what was the 
real
 offer? Coaching. Not facts but a huge hug of it’s-not-your-fault and you-are-not-alone validation, which is what I know actually frees our voices and allows for joy in communicating. Often, our overt offer is 
in day language, but our real offer is in night language and speaks to our audience’s deeper needs—deeper than they may even be aware of. Truths, more than facts. This is so fundamental that if all you have is two minutes before you speak and you’re freaking out, breathe and gently ask yourself, What am I offering—and what am I really offering? And then, of course, dare yourself to believe you’ve got what they need.
