Even with corporate clients who know their offer intellectually because they’ve mapped out pain points by committee, they often haven’t done the work to internalize the answers to the extent that they actually believe they’re doing something that matters. And it shows. They’re embarrassed to take the lead on a project, awkward with all eyes on them. I coached a man named Xavier who was the new CEO of a billion-dollar tech company. He had an accent from growing up outside the United States, but that wasn’t the problem at all—in fact, the company was using his accent as a decoy to get him a communication coach (a thing I only learned once I was in the room). The real problem was that he was an introvert with some serious fixed-mindset mentality: he had told himself he was bad at speaking in public. He hated it, and he wasn’t interested in being persuaded otherwise.


I asked him what he was thinking about while preparing to get up in front of his employees for the all-hands meetings he had to run every Friday. He told me, “What information I have to share” and “how much I don’t like this part of the job.” Needless to say, resentment doesn’t play well. But more to the point: the resentment was about him. He was thinking about himself, not about his audience and how each of those real humans in the room could best be served. What they needed, as his employees. Or what he could offer, as their boss. And what 
he could 
really
 offer. He didn’t realize he could ask himself more generative questions, use the opportunity to connect with his employees—and honestly, have a way better time up there.


For me, after that onboarding session with the MoveOn coaches, I ended up working with a bunch of women running for office for the first time, many of whom are still serving today. And I saw over and over how helping them pinpoint their aha moment gave them instant 
duende.
 Like, boom. Telling a story of decision made them come alive when they spoke in public; it allowed them to truly show up when they showed up. And I realized: That’s it! That’s my Venn-diagram overlap. Sitting in one of those sessions, with a magnificent young candidate named Emily who dodged her story until she didn’t, I had a full-on aha: I need to help more women show up in public, I thought. I need to help those who’ve been left behind by the old story of what power sounds like change that story. That’s the work. Because us, together, no longer hiding, vocally or otherwise? Showing up as whole people who’ve given ourselves permission to speak the music in our souls—we can change what power sounds like. Which will change who has power. Which will change the world. So I said yes to Princeton when they asked if I’d speak about women’s voices and yes to coaching friends, which became yes to coaching strangers, which became a new business and a podcast and a book and a calling—my bid to use the skills I have to ease my heartbreak about the world. I could 
feel
 my light turn back on.


I love that story. But this is one I don’t usually tell: Six years before those midterm elections, to the week, I had gotten married, honeymooned, and then promptly left my new husband to fly to Denver and canvass for Barack Obama because a col
lege friend had rallied a few of us to help in a swing state. We went door to door, chatted with potential voters, made sure they had a plan to get to the polls, listened to their concerns. I remember feeling awkward, not always having the answers, but giddy to be there and connect with total strangers and be part of something way bigger than me. I remember oodles of stapled spreadsheets and cheap snacks and asking to borrow a proper winter jacket from a kind Denver native. I remember a surprise rally when Obama came through town that weekend and I volunteered at the gate, letting people in and sharing the thrill with each of them. It was not as glamorous or influential as coaching actual candidates for office, but we turned Denver blue that week. Alone at the L.A. airport on election night, waiting on the curb for my ride, my phone pinged that Obama had won Colorado. I burst into tears, sobbing so hard that more than one concerned passerby asked if I was okay. Was I the most heroic member of the Obama reelection team? Not at all. But that’s the thing, I realized, as I recalled this story that I hadn’t considered worth sharing: heroism isn’t competitive, we don’t have to be the most anything. With humility and perspective, we must nonetheless practice centering ourselves in our own narratives, wresting heroism into our own tales, not because they’re the most heroic but because they’re ours.


Mine your life for stories. Here’s my cheat sheet on how.




1. Find your valleys. Motivational speaker and master storyteller Lisa Nichols teaches that if we hoof our way out of a valley in life, it serves us. But if we talk about it, it serves others who are still in valleys of their own. When we share stories, we multiply their value. What valleys have you been in? How did you get out?


2. Find drama in the little things. As writer Boze Herrington writes, “Re-enchant your life by making the mundane exciting. You are not ‘going to CVS,’ you are visiting the apothecary to buy potions. You are not ‘running an errand,’ you are doing a side quest. You are not ‘feeding the birds,’ you are making an alliance with the crow queen.”
14
 Tiny moments can serve an outsized purpose, and, as Boze reminds us, wonder and delight deserve attention too.


3. Find drama in the unconventional. Look for the ways that your stories played out the way they did 
because
 you are a woman or 
because
 rooms of power weren’t built for you, so you built something new. Let yourself consider problems you solved using tools like empathy or collaboration, plot points missing from classic hero stories. In 
Cassandra Speaks,
 Elizabeth Lesser asks what if “next to the Vietnam War memorial there was a similar wall with thousands of names of the people who have honed other ways of dealing with conflict—like maybe communicating, forgiving, mediating? Working for justice so that the economic and social conditions that spawn unrest are transformed before they explode?” Search for stories in the world that speak to this kind of heroism—but search yourself as well.


4. Get specific. We love specifics. On the campaign trail and again during the 2020 Democratic National Convention, Elizabeth Warren told a story:


One night my Aunt Bee called to check in. I thought I was fine, but then I just broke down and started to cry. 
I had tried holding it all together, but without reliable childcare, working was nearly impossible. And when I told Aunt Bee I was going to quit my job, I thought my heart would break. Then she said the words that changed my life: “I can’t get there tomorrow, but I’ll come on Thursday.” She arrived with seven suitcases and a Pekingese named Buddy and stayed for sixteen years. I get to be here tonight because of my Aunt Bee.


The story is powerful because it reveals a moment of crisis and what she did next, but it works especially well because of the specifics: Thursday. Seven suitcases. Pekingese. Buddy. She could have said, “I needed childcare and my Aunt Bee came to help”—that’s the version any of us might tell if we let the dumb voices run the show. But because of the details, Senator Warren beamed images from her mind directly into ours.


Dr. Uri Hasson, a professor of psychology and neuroscience at Princeton, explains the science behind this exchange in his TED Talk “This Is Your Brain on Communication.” He uses sample audio of a comedian sharing a setup without even getting to the punch line—just a reminder that stories truly don’t have to be particularly dramatic or sad—and shows with visuals that when listeners’ brains are scanned while listening, the same patterns and shapes appear in their mind as appear in the mind of the speaker. 
We sync with our audience
. Dr. Hasson calls our brain’s ability to transmit and receive other people’s memories, dreams, ideas, and stories like this “neural entrainment.”
15
 It’s like a mind meld. If we 
share a story with enough details, we can literally transfer the brain activity in our mind into the minds of our listeners—almost like they’re living the story themselves.


5. Do the work of developing the narrative. Sometimes a story pops out of us fully formed with a beginning, middle, and end. But mostly we’ve got a collection of moments, when we look back at our valleys (or mountaintops or low-stakes hikes). Real life takes a little more finesse to turn it into what we’d recognize as a story. If you don’t think of yourself as “full of stories” that may be because you haven’t finessed those moments yet. As Toni Morrison says, “Make it art.”


Consider one such moment and ask yourself: What was the problem? What was on the line? (That’s the beginning.) How did you solve it? Did you 
do
 something? If so, did you know it was the right thing to do, or did you take a wild stab? Did you ask a friend and sit with your choices and not know, before you knew? (That’s the middle.) What did you learn? How has it changed you? (That’s the end.) Begin a list. Maybe five aha moments you’ve had. Add to it over the next week—they don’t have to come right away. And then develop them into something with a beginning, middle, and end, even if it doesn’t feel organic yet.


6. But also, and this is important, fuck it. If all that work works, great. But if it makes you feel disconnected from your story, let it go. It is 
yours,
 after all. Allow the story to play on you, and have feelings about it. And don’t forget, those feelings can be delight or joy. Just make it 
matter to you. As Ishiguro also said when he accepted his Nobel, speaking pointedly to a roomful of global tastemakers, “The next generation will come with all sorts of new, sometimes bewildering ways to tell important and wonderful stories. We must keep our minds open to them, especially regarding genre and form, so that we can nurture and celebrate the best of them. In a time of dangerously increasing division, we must listen.” Keep your own mind open to your own ways of telling stories.


7. Finally, don’t dodge. Notice the instinct to deflect and demur. Notice if you try to leave out details because you worry you’ll be boring or sound self-involved. Notice if you leap to making it universal right away, using the editorial “you” instead of putting the story in the first person. Tell the story, the actual story. If it bores you, okay, don’t. If it’s too close and you’re not ready, don’t. But if it excites you or makes you feel things, tell it. If there’s any chance it’ll help the listener see you, or see themselves better, tell it. I believe talking about ourselves is 
required
 to truly help others, so you’re not doing anyone any favors if you hide.




Dr. Dacher Keltner, the Berkeley scholar, reminds us that “stories aren’t just the territory of films and novels. They punctuate our daily lives in jokes, friendly exchanges, accounts of the day at family dinners, and bedtime stories children eagerly anticipate at night.”
16
 The opportunity to tell them and join the revolution is waiting every time you say yes to speaking in public.


The opportunity to be the new sound of power is waiting too. Take it. We’re here for you, and we need to hear from you. Just like AOC. Like those women who won their districts. Like Simone Biles. Priya Parker. Valarie Kaur. Emily Nagoski. Rachel Rodgers. Greta Thunberg. Even Carol Gilligan and Jane Goodall and Mary Beard and Maxine Waters (you don’t have to be young to bring something new). I listen to them speak and I think: Thank you for the reminder. I, too, can sound sure but loving. I, too, can scare myself with the audacity of my call for change. I, too, can be playful and mischievous and unabashedly humanist and heart-forward and serious, all.


Hearing the new sound of power—voices that reveal a life—is galvanizing. It’s inspiring. It makes me want to rise up and expand out. It makes me want to light up the sky. It makes me want to reveal myself more and speak to the part of you, my reader, that’s your most hopeful, longing self, full of possibility.


So, hi. I speak to your possibility, not to your fear. Your fear is always welcome to come along, but I’m speaking to your possibility. Is taking the stage with bravery and joy a practice you’re committed to? Then practice. Find opportunities not just to get up there but to do the work ahead of time—to learn how you prepare best. Maybe you do a breath warm-up and you take your time with it. Maybe you prime yourself for power and encourage yourself to take up space. Maybe you surround yourself with people who make you feel you are indeed enough and indeed worthy and your stories kill. Maybe you think about your audience and what they really need and make it about them. Maybe you think about the Venn-diagram overlap and settle into how your skills can address what is, to you, the greatest heartbreak in the world. Maybe you put on kickass 
“permission to speak” lipstick and dance to a power ballad and declare it your new entrance music. Maybe you watch your favorite TED Talk and visualize yourself up there. Maybe you define heroism just broadly enough that it includes you.


Try it all, knowing some rooms you won’t warm up (but maybe you’ll still do what you came to do) and lots you will. Knowing if you really show up when you show up, you’ll say something bold in a voice you recognize as your own, and you’ll ask things of your audience they didn’t dare consider when they woke up that morning. You’ll use your speech to build the world you want to live in. For you, for me, for us.

About the Author


Title Page


Dedication


Introduction: The New Sound of Power


1. Breath
:
 
How to breathe like you’ve got permission to speak


2. Size
:
 
Vocal fry, upspeak, sorrys, and the size of your voice


3. Emotion
:
 
Own your feelings—for good


4. Pitch
:
 
The highs and lows of power


5. Tone
:
 
Talk to get heard


6. Sounds
:
 
Accent bias, white supremacy, and liking the voice you actually have


7. Words
:
 
The words you choose and how to say them like they’re yours


8. Heroism
:
 
How to talk about yourself (hint: it’s not about you, it’s about us)

About the Author


Samara Bay helps women and other folks with fire in their belly find their public voice and use it well in the moments that matter. Her clients are political candidates, entrepreneurs, celebrities, rising leaders, rebels and dreamers. She’s taught at Pace University in Manhattan, the Alan Alda Center for Communicating Science and the Stella Adler Academy of Acting and Theatre in Los Angeles, and has presented and run workshops at organizations around the United States and abroad. Bay has an English degree from Princeton University and an MFA in acting from Brown University. Her work on rethinking the sound of power has been featured in 
The New York Times Magazine, Forbes, Slate
 and 
Jezebel,
 on 
CBS Sunday Morning,
 and as part of NBC’s Know Your Value.


She lives in Los Angeles with her husband, son and dog, Moxie.


samarabay.com
